TO KNOW WHEN
. . . YOU HATE A GOOD THING
is a species of is very often difficult to acquire. Retrospective sighs
are often heaved and lamentations heard when it is too late. Then—the having a
good thing is a joy which is turned to pain by the loss of it.
CAMEO CIGARETTES
are “a good thing” in the way of smoking.
See that you stick to them.
THE RED PAGE.
An American paper reproduces
this interesting sketch byDuMau-
rier, representing him- self, Charles
Keene, and the only Whistler.
The original is of the same size.
Owing partly to the use of some of his clever
portraits in ‘The New Review,’ William Nicholson
is beginning to find himself famous ; and sour
Pennell (it is apparently he whose Lond, ‘Daily
Chronicle’ lead is followed -by Syd. ‘Daily
Telegraph,’ which reprints part of a criticism)
unkindly holds him as pattern to Phil May
—-as ‘if~ appreciation of the one artist implied
depreciation of the other As one of ‘ The
Beggar.staff Brothers ’ (-the other was his brother-
in-law, Pryde) Nicholson’s talents—especially for
posters—have been familiar to the London art-
world for some years. Recently he has published
an illustrated alphabet, of- which two examples
are imperfectly reproduced on this page. The
artist posed for letter A is drawn from Nicholson,
himself'. ' The reproduction-can <Jnly’suggest his
method of working with strong lines and rich
blacks opposed to a neutral tint (in the original
the ‘ artist’s ’ clothes are tinted like light ‘ brown-
paper,’ while his face, sleeves, and the foreground
are still lighter.) The" ‘ huntsman ’ combines
black-against light ‘brown-paper,’ with a coat in
pink-red.
* * * *
More lately Ni-cholson has issued ‘Twelve
Sporting Prints,’ with verses by Rudyard Kip-
ling attached. The prints preserve Nicholson’s
character : of the bad verses this is best—
The Pious Horse to church may trot,
A maid may work a man’s salvation ;
. ■ Four horses and a girl are not,
However, aids to reformation.

Easel : ‘ Amusing to see Sydney D.T. reprints
London “Daily Chronicle’s” recently - given
advice to Phil May to “conform to the standard
of beauty and appropriateness, and even humor,-”
which his friend W. Nicholson has just set up in
his splendidly-drawn “Alphabet.” When Nichol-
son and^Pryde —the other Beggarstaff brother—
had theix studio in Bedford Park, “ Good old
Phil ” (as the “ Daily Chronicle ” writer called
him—writ sarkastie, apparently for, you
see, Phil has so thoroughly captivated the
“common herd,” as well as all sorts
of artists, including two who differ to
such a degree as do Herkomer and Whistler)
used to ride down on that gee of his (off which he
says he has fallen so many times) to -see- them.
Natural that two such draughtsmen as M. and N.
should thoroughly appreciate each other, and cer-
tain that the former will continue to show us, as
he did in his ‘ Bulletin ’ days, that “lb is enough
for a work of art to have the quality of its own
order.” “Nicholson, in recent months (says
‘ SketCjh,.’ Dec. 8), took up with -eagerness the arti
of; wo()ff-cuttihg—he has by very quick stages
: prbive4 hiihlelf to‘be a master of this province of
his aft. ’’ And thereupon some of the gentle-
men ..who write about illustration at ’Ome want a
brilliant master of pen-drawing to get wooJcwf-quali-
ties into his work—the “Daily Chronicle” man go-
ing sb far aTsto declare that May shows an entire in-
difference to the making of even a decent page in
“Guttersnipes”—the book on the strength of
which he was elected R.I. O, and P.S. : In
‘Punch Almanac,’ just to hand, May’s work is

plete answer to the yarn that he’s “ going off.” ’ i
* * * ' *
The Australian artist's whose pictures are in-
cluded in the consignment—a measured 60 tons !
sent to England for exhibition mav now be sup-
posed to be palpjtating for the verdict of British
critics. One may prophesy it:' ‘ Promising.; but
amateurish—with brilliant exceptions.’ Anyway,
N.S.W. Art Gallery trustees have given everybody
a show including trustee Julian Ashton, who
oalmly broke the rules of the game by refusing to
submit his pictures to the subordinate committee
of adjudication, Ashton is represented by his
portrait of Parkes : by the * Prospector ’ who
borrowed his mother’s milk-dish; and by some
choice water-color and black-and-white. Among
other pictures goes Torn Roberts’s shearing-shed ;
Mahony’s ‘Straggler’ and ‘Days of Old;’ Sid.
Long’s ‘Tranquil Waters’ and ‘Spirit of the
Bush ; ’ Lister’s ‘ Ever Restless Sea ; ’ Streeton’s
‘ Still Glides ; ’ M'Cubbin’s ‘ Wallaby Track ; ’
Longstaff’s ‘ Lady in Black;’ Piguenit’s ‘Flood
in the Darling ’ and others. FuLlwood, Spence,
Lambert, and many more are represented—often
by several pictures ; in fact, it’s a choice collec-
tion, and includes the cream of local work. If
the ‘ Himalaya ’ splits on a Red Sea rock—
which all the Muses of the studios forbid !
Egypt’s ancient loss won’t be a circumstance to
ours.
* * * *
To most Australians Dudley Hardy is known
entirely by his black-and-white work, and the
glimpse of his quality as painter in Dec. ‘ Art
Journal ’is very interesting. The writer of the
article, Arthur Lawrence, remarks ‘ the
fine colouring, harmony, refinement, and
sense of power ’ in Hardy’s pictures; and
judges that, despite his brilliant illustra-
tive talent, he is painter first and will
soon be painter wholly. Hardy himself is
q'u'iteJ re a familiar dispute. ‘ln persistently
doing black-and-white work,’ he says, ‘ you
may, perhaps, momentarily lose sense of colour,
but, if you really possess the instinct, you
can get back to color-work almost at onoe. . . .
And I think constant practice as a draughtsman
enables me, when painting, to see things in a
broader way, and to aim at breadth of effect with
greater simplicity.’
Writes Louise Mack—
Lewis Carroll has gone to his wonderland, a
fair and merry wonderland, no doubt, but not a
funnier one than he led dear little Alice to three-
and-thirty years ago. Passed behind the lookings
glass, and now seeing face to face, he.inay have
arrived at the certainty that he held a warmer
place in the heart of the grown-up than in the
heart of the child, may grin to find himself
further above the children’s heads than ever. For,
though we call him the nursery classic, and buy
him for our children, and cling to him ourselves,
and - edition after; edition comes and goes,
and all the languages take him into .their
own, isn’t it a truth that the normal
child is a little worried and perplexed by Carroll’s
most delicate quips and cranks, and finds the road
a weary one that is brightened by his cleverest
nonsense? Tenniel’s pictures will always be a
great seduction to youngsters, and invite many
to read who afterwards give up in despair, but
not even Tenniel’s pictures could make 4 Alice in
Wonderland ’ a bopk that qhildren.would love and
long for, unprompted by some older mind.
The child who loves books for books’sake can
read to pass out of the big, bright world of
childhood into a bigger, brighter world of things
happening always, loves Carroll because he tells
his nonsense with such a straight and serious
face. Never fora minute does he give the
children a clue that it is all a hoax, a make-up.
Just as one would relate the ludicrous impossi-
bilities of one’s own most impossible dreams, so
smilelessly and earnestly he brings his Jabberwocks
and Borogoves, his oysters and his walruses, before
the little eyes—but so smilelessly and earnestly
that the sense of the impossible ceases to exist,
and a feeling of flatness comes creeping over the
normal child mind, greedy for surprises, anxious
for absurdities when it understands they.areabsur-
dities, delighting keenly to be pleasurably startled.
In the midst of his finest incongruities, Carroll’s
peculiar restraint or sedateness is always in evi-
dence. The ordinary child-mind feels the sedate-
ness, is puzzled by it, and cries out for the fairy-
tales it knows are fairy-tales, for the Fauntleroys
it fondly believes are roaming the world in velvet
suits and gtlden curls, with the beautiful Dearest
in tow. And the gentle, simple, little Alice, with
.her natural manners and; straight hair, deepens
their perplexity. Did,-she/really go'through the
looking-glass ? For they feel that, she is of them-
selves' earthy,-and how to -reconcile' her with the
leg of mutton that gets up and bows, the pudding
that conversates when you cut it, the oysters
whose shoes were clean and neat,
And this w is odd, because, you know,
They hadn't any feet,
is too hard a problem for the little ones to solve.
Says ‘The Saturday Review'-
The [late] Laureate, after declaring to Mr. Pal-
grave that, had he an unpublished Autobiography
of Horace in MS. in his hand, he would burn it,
beoause a poet’s life is to be found in his work
alone, did himself arrange with his son to have
this prolonged Biography produced, and to
include in it, to the great increase of its bulk and
its price as literary merchandise, the second and
third-rate poems his finer taste suppressed during
his lifetime ! Without any fear of the ‘ghouls,’
there could have been a bonfire with these. The
‘resurrectionists’ were they of the poet’s own
household ; and when they invoke his authority
for the deed they invite us to deny the sincerity of
his constant protest against the publication of
inferiorities, and to impugn for the first time the
critical judgment hitherto held to be in him all
but unerring. Again, with the suggestion of
a pose, the poet writes to Mr. Gladstone :
‘I. heard of an old lady the other day to whom
all the great men of her time had written.
When Froude’s “Carlyle” came out, she rush d
up to her room, and to an old chest there in
which she kept their letters, and flung them into
the fire. “They were written to me,” she said,
“not to the public!” And she set her chimney on
fire, and her children and grandchildren ran in—
“ The chimney’s on fire!” ‘.‘Never mind,” she
said, and went on burning, I should like to raise
an altar to that old lady, and burn incense upon
it.’ Yet Tennyson, at this very time and always,
had the habit of keeping nearly all the letters he
received, including mere invitations to dinner.;
so that when they came to be turned to the pur-
pose of a biography, two devoted triends of the
Tennysons had to wade through over forty
thousand of these human Or inhuman documents !
The smoke of their burning had made a fit incense
indeed to offer to the ‘old lady ’; but to her the
poor honor of lip-service alone was rendered.
Some Tennyson letters indeed were burned ; but
the fact is not remarked with the exultation the
reader might expect : ‘ All the letters from my
father to Arthur Hallam were destroyed by his
father after Arthur’s death—a great loss.’ The
same confusion of pose and of practice confronts
the reader again and again. The biographer has
a partiality for letters addressed to Dukes, and a
preference—perhaps still more subtly mundane —
for those addressed to workmen.
* * * *
In a letter to the Duke of Argyll, Tennyson, at
the time of Macaulay’s death, recalls his first
meeting with the historian. There had been a
cataract of chatter, until, in one of those infre-
quent* flashes of silence,’ Tennyson was presented.
‘ Macaulay turned to me and said, “ Good morn-
ing ; I am happy to have had the pleasure of
making your acquaintance,” and strode away.
Had I been a piquable man, I should have been
piqued.’ That is naif enough ; but you do not
classify your poet so easily. The next man who
meets him assures him of the honor, &e., and
‘Don’t talk d d nonsense’ is the retort.
Again, the Queen tells the Duke that she admires
‘ln Memoriam,’ and has marked her favorite
lines ; and the Duke tells the Poetund the Poet
tells two friends. At this point the Duke-,wHtes,
and implores the Poet to tell nobody. ‘I am
vexed,’ writes the Poet, ‘ because the Queen
might fancy that her private Comments were
public prey,' and then follows talk about ‘multi-
tudinous 1 babble.’ But the babble .WAS
Queen’s', the Duke’s, the Poet’s ; and why should
it stop with the Poet’s friends, and the friends
of the friends? To crown the perversity, it is
all made ‘ public prey,’ if that is to be
in these volumes at any rate, where we are told
that the Queen said to the poet, on his first visit
to her in 1862, ’Next to the Bible, “In
Memoriam ” is my comfort.’ More allusions are
ma le to the craving of the age for personalities—
then the eye lights on a passage which
r cords that Mr. Ruskin has beeh to lunch and
that he wore his accustomed blue tie ! ‘This
1 horrible age of blab,’ is thrown at us once more—
and then we hear that the Poet’s own ‘ anecdotes
and sayings were taken down as soon as spoken,’
and that, for instance; ‘he admired much Miss
Mary Anderson, and held her to be the flower of
girlhood!’ ‘Confound the publicities and gab-
blements of the nineteenth century!’ again he
ories, when an Edinburgh paper has unblushingly
announced a new poem of his as in the press. But
turn the pages, and you are fully informed by his
official biographer that he liked a dinner of
‘ beefsteak and potato, a cub of cheese, a pint of
port, and afterwards a pipe (never a oigar) ’ ; and
that, to take a random illustration, he once
stopped reading to Joachim because t m
in bed in the next room. By all means to 1 tj
but do not interlard the recital with denuncia-
tions of other reciters.
• . * *
This ‘ gabblement ’ about ‘gab'hl »k lit goes on'
until it is tiresome even as a study of human in-;
consistency. There is a Nemesis that awaits it,
too at the end of the Memoir. In the closing,
scene of his father’s life the son records : AtJ
three o!eloek he was pleased with the telegram
About him from the Queen, but he- muttered,,
“Oh! that Press will get hold of me now !
The day after that he died ; ,an 1 the day after
that his own medical attendant published to the;
Press what the biographer still calls ‘ the medical:
bulletin 5 as follows;—‘On the. bed a figure of
breaching marble, flooded and bathed in the light |
of the full moon streaming through the oriel win-
dow ; his hand clasping the Shakespeare which he;
had asked for but recently and which he had, kept
by him to the end ; the moonlight, the majestic
figure, as he lay there “drawing thicker breath,”
irresistibly brought to our minds his own “Passing
of Arthur.”’ Surely the irony of history could
not further go, nor retribution be more complete.
* * * *
Again, it was a way with the bard to resent a
stare. The New Englanders who came, light- :
heartedlv certain of a welcome, to look over the
wall at Farringford, and the British tourist who
made picnic at Alum Bay in the hope of at.least
a distant glimpse of him, became the dearest
object of his dread. Yet one must be forgiven
the suspicion that he missed the tourists if they
were not there. Readers of Sir Henry Taylor’s I
Autobiography will remember ' the diatribe
uttered by Tennyson to Mrs. Cameron against
the persecutions of autograph collectors : a dia-
tribe which ended with the quite contrary lament
that he had received no letters for three days, and
that he feared there was an atrophy in the world
about him and his fame. In that anecdote you
have the man for whom, as Mr. A. Q. Benson
rightly says, you seek in vain in the Memoir.
Tlie mood was so variable that if one day lie met
in the lanes two girls who did net "turn round to
look after him, he would growl, ‘They don’t
know who I am ; ’ and the next, if they did look
round, would cry, ‘Americans!’ His cos-
tume bore out the same delightful contrari-
ness; The wideawake, that Mr. Gladstone
really did shy at when it threatened the House of
Lords, he wore not as one who hates to be regarded
by the curious eye. And.there was one '’occasion
when a little hov proved as frank as another of his
age and sex in Hans Andersen’s story. He had
been out with the bard, who, returning, com-
plained in deep tones of the intrusive’eyes of
woman and ' marr. ‘ Then why do yob wear that
blue cloajc ? ’ was the home question that came
back in a treble tone : and then all was still. The
incefngruity of the pose is given (with all uncon-
sciousness. of course) by the. biographer himself :
1 If a totirist, ’ he says, ‘ were seen o >ming towards
him, for many ivould recoaniSe his broad-brimmed,
wideawake and his short blue cape with velvet collar,
he would flee.’ His capriciousness, in short, was
that of the courting cat.

Madge: ‘Prof. Morris’s error, in his “ Dictionary of Aus-
tral English ” (pointed out by Ernest Favenc), in stating
that there is no species of dabchick in Australia, is ail
the more curious, as Gould specially to an Aus-
tralian variety in his “ Birds of Great Britain.” Morris
seems to be a bit of a literary dabchick himself, and to
have compiled his dictionary in dabchickery fashion—
“ As when a dabchick waddles through the copse
<sn feet and wings, and flies and wades and hops.” ‘
Pope — Dunciad.
Gould, in his “Birds of Australia,” also describes and
devotes a plate to the Australian dabchick (Podiceps
Polio cephalus), which, he says, the natives of Westralia
call lPi/-00-da,-and of which he states-that its “distribu-
tion over all the southern parts of Australia may be said
to be general.” (Vol. VIL, plate 8%.)' . . . X. : ‘A. L.

more. Writing' verse is excellent training for writing
prose, anyway ; but you can’t reverse It; I speak of
style, not mat£€r.’ ...6xB: ‘Solitude and a strong

SURGEON LANGSTON, M.R.C.S., Eng.,
THE GREATEST AND MOST SUCCESSFUL PRACTI-
TIONER IN THE WORLD.
But few of noted specialists have sprung- into pre-emin-
ence and an immense practice as Surgeon Langston, a
resident and practitioner at 121 Collins-street, Melbourne.
His name has become almost literally a household word :
almost every other man you meet has a wonderful story to
relate of some almost miraculous cure he has performed
in an acute or chronic case of long standing, where other
physicians have failed. He addresses himself in particular

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WRITTEN AFTERWARDS.
So the days of my tramping are over,
And the days of my riding are done —
I’m about as content as a rover
Will ever be under the sun :
I write, after reading your letter—
My pipe with old memories rife,
And I feel in a mood that had better
Not meet the true eyes of the wife.
You must never admit a suggestion
That old things are good to recall;
You must never consider the question:
Was I happier then ? After all ? ”
You must banish the old hope and sorrow
That make the sad pleasures of life,
You must live for To-day and To-morrow
If you want to be just to the wife.
I have changed since the first day I kissed her.
Which is due—Heaven bless her ! —to her ;
I’m respected and trusted—l’m “Mister,"
Addressed by the children as “ Sir.”
And I feel the respect without feigning—
But you’d laugh the great laugh of your life
If you only saw me entertaining
An old-lady friend of the wife.
By-the-way, when you’re writing, remember
That you never went drinking with me,
And forget our last night of December,
Lest our sev’ral accounts disagree.
And, for my sake, old man, you had better
Avoid the old language of strife,
For the technical terms of your letter
May be misunderstood by the wife.
Never hint of the girls appertaining
To the past (when you’re writing again),
For they take such a lot of explaining,
And you know how I hate to explain.
There are some things, we know to our sorrow.
That cut to the heart like a knife,
And your past is To-day and To-morrow
If you want to be true to the wife.
I believe that the creed we were chums in
Was grand, but too abstract and bold,
And the knowledge of life only comes in
When you’re married and fathered and old.
And it’s well. You may travel as few men,
You may stick to a mistress for life ;
But the world, as it is, born of woman
Must be seen through the eyes of the wife.
No doubt you are dreaming as I did
And going the careless old pace,
While my future grows dull and decided,
And the world narrows down to the Place.
Let it be. If my “ treason’s ” resented,
You may do worse, old man, in your life ;
Let me dream, too, that I am contented —
For the sake of a true little wife.
HENRY LAWSON.
Mangamauna, Maoriland.
“OFF.”
So Emma’s broke it off !
Of course ! I might ’a’ known !
Things seemed a-cqmin’ round a bit
If they was left alone.
Th re was the bit o’ land
A-gettin’ on Al,
She said she’d have me when it went
An acre to the ton !
It’s just a little ’ard
To graft away for years :
Your days all give to bullocking,
Your nights to nameless fears !
An’ all to come to this !
Oh, Emma ! where’s your ’eart !
Can it feel half the bitterness
That’s tearin’ mine apart ?
There stands the little cot
I built for me and you !
I feel a kind of chokin’ when
I look at it—l do.
The patch o’ garden plants
I sowed to please your eye
May all get swamped with summer-weed
You’ll never see them die !
I used to sit an’ dream
Of evenin’s, after tea,
Of, someday, fixin’ up a swing
Beside the pepper-tree.
It won’t be wanted now,
Nor nothin’ of the kind.
I don’t care what becomes o’things
Now Emma’s changed her mind.
NIL.
“DUNNO!”
‘Member Jim ? Long, lanky slab,
Seemed he had no tongue to gab.
Shed all clucking, he’d lie low;
Ask him, he’d say, “Oh ! dunno ! ”
Mighty hard to interest Jim,
Most things wuz the same to him ;
Sport or politics had no show ;
Jim’d just say “ Oh, I —dunno l "
Jim got struck on Quigly’s girl,
She on him —she was a pearl!
But he couldn’t talk, an’ he wouldn’t go.
An’ what to do Jim didn’t know.
So, seein’ ’s how Jim made no play,
“ D’yer love me? ” she asks him one day
Jim he thinks, looks at her slow,
An’—s’elp me ! —says he, “ I dunno ! ”
Broke it off ? You bet a quid !
Took it easy too, Jim did.
Not a chap much on for show,
But he felt it —yes ! —I know !
Night the fire burnt Quigly’s place
Jim—yer should ’ave seen 'is face !
Rushed in—bli’ me, he would go !
What for ? Quigly’s girl, you know.
Got her safe —but he , my word !
Parson came’s soon’s he heard.
“ You a Christian ? ” he says fow,
Jim just gasps out, “ I—dun—no ! "
Died then, Jim did. Parson, well,
He guessed Jim would go to Hell.
’Cos he wasn’t “ saved,” I s’pose—
Mebbe there’s a God who knows.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 29, 1898.
A Leap in the Dark.
The latest contribution of the New South
Wales Government to Federal literature is a
work by State Statistician Coghlan on the
finances of Federation. It is a sad and
pessimistic publication. Apparently the
writer was under severe limitations, and was
confined to showing, as far as possible, the
impossibility of every solution of the
financial difficulty which has been proposed
up to date, and then had to leave off. In the
course of his work Mr. Coghlan explains
that the Federal Government must raise a
large customs and excise revenue, and, if it
doesn’t take over the debts and the railways,
must divide a big surplus among the pro-
vinces to keep them solvent. Then the
calculation proceeds on this basis : The pro-
vinces, in 1896, imported so much each from
foreign countries, under their respective
tariffs. If they import the same amounts
under the uniform Federal tariff—which
they certainly won’t—they will yield so much
each in customs and excise revenue to
the Federal Treasury. The proportion
which each province should pay in ac-
cordance with its population towards
the Federal expenses is so much, and
there is a balance to bo divided.
If the balance is divided, giving each pro-
vince enough to keep it solvent, New South
Wales will get £980,000 less than her fair
share, and this amount will be divided among
her neighbours. If each province is handed
back what remains of its own contribution
after paying its proportion of the Federal ex-
penses, Victoria and Tasmania will go broke
for want of revenue, and South Australia
will go partly broke. If the surplus is
divided according to population, Westralia
will burst up utterly, New South Wales will
lose a little, and Victoria, South Australia,
and Tasmania will revel in ill-gotten contri-
butions afc the expense of their neighbours.
Then, having alleged that all these things
are hopeless, this profoundly melancholy
pamphlet stops suddenly.
* * * *
Even the little light it professes to throw
on the subject is mostly delusive. For, in
the first place, it is necessary to begin by
assuming that the provinces will each con-
tribute so much to the Federal customs and
excise revenue, and will therefore have a
lien upon so much of the surplus. And all
the calculations are based on the fact that
the provinces import so much already from
countries outside the Federation under their
respective tariffs, and on the assumption
that they will continue to import the same
amount from the same countries, no matter
what the tariff may be. Therefore, if the
Federal tariff is twice as heavy as the present
New South Wales tariff, the assumption is
that N.S. Wales will raise twice as much
revenue, so far as its foreign imports are con-
cerned, as it does now. And, if the Federal
tariff is only half as heavy as the present
South Australian tariff, the further assump-
tion is that South Australia will pay only
half as much in duties on foreign imports as
it does now. Which is just as logical as the
assumption that, if a man makes £5OO a year
working eight hours a day, he would make
£l5OO a year working 24 hours. On mere
bleak arithmetic he would make £l5OO a
year ; in reality he would only make himself
a corpse inside two weeks. Also, the same
kind of mechanically-accurate arithmetic
clearly proves that if one man is bitten by a
lion eight men will be bitten by eight lions,
and that if two men can dig out a cubic yard
of soil in 30 minutes, 18,000 men could do it
in one-tenth of a second—which they
couldn’t, for they would merely trample
each other to death in the struggle of 18,000
men to get on to a place where there was
only standing-room for four.
» • * *
In reality, things would pan out very
differently. If the South Australian tariff
was reduced one-third, the probability is that
the South Australian imports would pre-
sently so far increase that the cash results
would be about the same as they are now.
If the New South Wales tariff is trebled,
New South Wales, when it shakes down a
little, will probably pay about 50 per cent,
more than it does now, instead of 300
per cent. New South Wales, at the end
of ’9l, changed its low revenue tariff
into a comparatively high one, and on the
mechanical variety of logic the customs reve-
nue should have about doubled. Even at
the beginning, however, it did nothing like
this, and inside four years things had so re-
adjusted themselves that the high tariff
yielded, to all intents and purposes, the same
as the low one. The alleged difference of
“consuming power” of the different pro-
vinces is a matter which rests on no visible
evidence whatever. Inside a very few years
(probably five would be more than sufficient)
a uniform tariff would almost certainly make
the consumption of foreign goods per head
and the customs revenue per head so nearly
equal all over Australia that there would be
no difference to quarrel about. The removal
of border customs duties would so alter the
entire situation that arguments based on the
present imports and exports of each province
are of no possible account. The products of
each province would go, free of duty, all over
the Federation, and, if the local supply of
any dutiable article ran short, it would run
short all over the country, to all intents and
purposes simultaneously. Then the import-
ing process would commence in all the pro-
vinces. Even the fact that Westralia raises
four times as much Customs revenue as the
average of the provinces makes no difference
in this flattening-out process. If Westralia
didn’t manufacture for itself, it would simply
import its slop-clothes and its tinned-dog
from Sydney, and Melbourne, and Adelaide,
duty free, instead of from Europe or America,
with a heavy duty upon them. If the supply
of clothes ran out all over Australia, then all
the provinces would either have to import or
fall back into barbarism, but no one province
would be, to any noticeable extent, worse-
circumstanced than another.
* * * m
If the Federal Government keeps an ac-
count of the revenue derived from each pro-
vince for, say, five years, and during that
period returns to each its exact share of the
surplus, and after that simply assumes that
they all contribute the same amount per
head, and distributes the balance on that
basis, it will probably get as near to mathe-
matical accuracy as possible. The other
question to be settled is whether some of
the provinces, getting only their fair share
of the surplus, will be in a state of wild
affluence while others will be practically
insolvent l Coghlan’s table seems to imply
that New South Wales will have £750,000
more revenue than it has now, Victoria
about £60,000 less, Westralia £600,000 less,
and various other horrors. Still these
horrors only mean that each province will
get its fair share of its own customs and
excise revenue, so that some of them will
tax themselves a little more in this particular
direction for their own use and benefit, and
some will tax themselves a little less and
make up the revenue in other ways. But all
these things are just about as speculative as
the prevalence of religious mania in Jupiter.
If the same set of calculations had been made
ten years ago, when the Victorian boom was
at the pinnacle of its glory and the Westra-
lian one wasn’t even thought of, the situa-
tion would have been so wholly different
that no one would recognise it now. Five
years hence it will probably be so different
again as to be equally unrecognisable. Before
the Federal tariff is even drafted all the cal-
culations of to-day will most likely be waste
paper. The question whether the provinces
can all keep solvent if they federate,
and have equal customs duties, and the
surplus is divided fairly at the same
amount per head all round, is easily
answered. The Bulletin doesn’t know.
Nobody knows, or can possibly know.
All the elaborate estimates, showing how
things will pan out if the foreign im-
ports of each province remain the same, and
the populations preserve the same ratio, and
the expenditure remains the same, and the
income from railways and other sources of
revenue, apart from customs, remains the
same, are only good for gun-wads. Notone
of these things will remain the same for a
month after the new system is inaugurated—
nor even for half a day. Also, they wouldn’t
remain the same for a month if there was no
new r system at all. Whether all the pro-
vinces would remain solvent if they didn’t
federate, or whether they would be more
insolvent one way than the other, is equally
undiscoverable.
* * * *
The Bulletin still believes, as it always
has done, that the Federal Government
should take over the railways and public
debts in addition to the other proposed ser-
vices, and prohibit new provincial debts
altogether, and then there would be a vastly
more efficient system and no surplus to
wrangle about. But if the railways
and debts are left out, the most
hopeful policy is to have a period
of (say) five years of separate account-
keeping between the provinces, so as to
give things time to equalise themselves,
and after that to let the surplus be divided
among the provinces according to popula-
tion. If any province can’t meet its liabili-
ties under this arrangement the Federal Go-
vernment must think out a way of taking
over all the debts and consolidating them.
If some other crisis arrives the Federal
Government must devise a way out of it —
that is exactly what it is there for. The
provincialists’ reply to this line of argument
is that Federation, without a full under-
standing of the consequences, is a leap in
the dark. So it certainly is. So, also, is a
continuation of provincialism. Everything is,
more or less, a leap in the dark, and no one in
all this world ever has a full understanding of
the possible results that may arise from doing
anything or leaving anything undone. Even
dirt isn’t so common in this world as un-
expected consequences. The people who
demand a full understanding of the results
before Federation is effected are asking for
something that has never been on this globe.
If they had any fuller a knowledge of the
results of not federating the request might
be reasonable, but they haven’t. Federa-
tion, it is hereby frankly admitted, is a leap
in the dark. So is the other thing. So is
everything. The Bulletin merely advo-
cates this particular leap because the gloom
looks rather less dense that way than the way
Australia is leaping at present.
The Fiscal Referendum.
Supposing Premier Reid keeps his promise to
take a popular vote on the question of Free-
trade v. Protection at next N.S.W. election,
sundry interesting things may happen. The
collapse of the Party Government machine is
one of them. For instance, it is quite possible
that the general election may send Reid and
the rest of the GoD-fearing Ministry back into
power, and that the popular vote may give a
solid majority for Protection. No one really
knows why the community returns this or any
other Government to power. It may be be-
cause of its fiscal policy, or its direct-taxation
policy, or its Upper House reform policy, or its
promise to make the community jolly-and-fat,
or its GoD-fearing characteristics. If it gets
back into power it may be because the public
has a personal dislike to Lyne, or because it
has a sort of personal liking for its little, bulgy
comedian and thinks that life would be a dreary
thing without him, or for the sake of Federa-
tion, or because it doesn’t believe that Car-
ruthers hugged that girl some ten or twelve
years ago, or because of the Ministry’s land
policy, or anything else. It is perfectly within
the bounds of credibility that the community
might give the present Government a working
majority again and still vote for the other
party’s fiscal policy, or vice versa. And then
what would happen ?
* * * *
Presumably it is not proposed to ask the
country for a direct mandate and then refuse
to obey it. If the Government invites the
community to send it back to office, and the
community does so, the inference is that the
Government will go back to office. And if the
Government asks the community to say if it
wants Protection, and the community says that
it does, presumably the Government won’t
reply that it will see the community dashed
first. If it doesn’t intend to carry out the
nation’s definite instructions the natural infer-
ence is that it wouldn’t ask for them. Yet
supposing George Reid introduced a thorough-
going Protectionist tariff in obedience to the
popular mandate, where would the principles of
the GoD-fearing Government go to ? And sup-
posing he refused to do it, despite the direct
popular mandate which he had himself asked
for, and yet retained office, where would the
blessed Constitution go to? And supposing he
refused to do it, and resigned office, and left
Lyne— as the only alternative under the Party
system —to form a Government, well-knowing
that Lyne hadn’t a majority and couldn’t
carry on, where again would the Constitution
go to ? In fact, where would everything go to ?
One thing which is tolerably certain is that
the Party system and the Referendum won’t
work together—at least not for any great length
of time. Under the Party system the country
can’t make itself understood on matters of
detail; it is only a great mute monster trying
vaguely to explain itself by flapping its ten-
tacles in the air. Supposing it didn’t want
Lyne and yet did want Protection, and the
above-suggested deadlock ensued, how could it
furnish particulars? Possibly it would be will-
ing to give a solid working majority to Barton
or Dick O’Connor if either of them presented
himself, but as neither had presented himself,
and there was no definite way in which that
great, floundering mass, the community, could
explain itself, the situation would be difficult.
If one House of Parliament was elected by the
whole community voting as a single electorate
with manhood suffrage, any man who got a
towering majority over all .other candidates, as
Barton did at the Federal Convention election,
might fairly be set down as the popular choice
for Premier. As it is, there is no reliable way
of indicating the popular choice. Even if the
electorate of Bogged Cow gives him the biggest
majority on record, and burns its hamlet as a
bonfire in his honor, and every single elector
gets the horrors by way of celebrating the vic-
tory, that only expresses the feelings of one
electorate after all. If every other electorate
got the horrors also for the same reason it
wouldn’t even have any constitutional method
by which it could explain what it had got the
horrors about.
* ♦ • *
It is one of the fictions of Party Government
that the country only wants either Reid or
Lyne, or that if it is tired of Turner then it is
craving for nothing on earth except John
M'lntyre, and that it has no aspirations what-
ever beyond these limits. Another fiction is
that it is entirely satisfied with the crowd in
power—all of them; that it fully believes every-
thing they say, and entirely approves of every-
thing they do, and that it regards them generally
as being perfect. The country isn’t supposed
merely to regard them as being possibly a little
less of a fool than the other crowd, or to think
that perhaps there is so little difference between
the two that it isn’t worth the trouble of having
a change. If it or its representatives show that
they haven’t absolute faith in the Ministry on all
matters, the Ministry is supposed to cast its
portfolio from it with outraged dignity, and
wrap its insulted feelings around it, and retire.
The mere introduction of the Referendum
knocks this tradition endways. The Reid Go-
vernment is supposed to be in office because
the public approves of all its principles and all
its doings. At any definite sign of disapproval
it is expected to gather up its toga and make a
lofty Roman exit. At the same time, a Refer-
endum on the fiscal question implies that the
public, even while it supplies the Reid majority,
may, after all, disapprove of one of Reid’s
leading principles, which is itself an outrage
on the Party fiction. And if it turns out that
the country does so disapprove, there is nothing
in the Party system to meet so awful and un-
precedented a case.
• * * *
Supposing the community gives Premier Reid
a majority at the general election, and, at the
same time, that the Referendum vote gives a
majority for Protection. If such a thing wasn’t
possible, then there would be no earthly use in
having a Referendum at all, and Premier Reid,
by promising to have the Referendum, tacitly
admits the possibility. In that case it will be
evident that the nation, at three successive
elections, has been a large, dumb object making
signs which have been wholly misunderstood.
There will then be three possibilities in view
perhaps more. One is that it didn’t want
Reid because of his Foreign-trade proclivities,
as was generally supposed, but for some other
reason, which it has been trying to explain by
waving its limbs in the air to no purpose. An-
other is that it didn’t really care for Reid so
much as for some of his colleagues, whom it has
been unsuccessfully struggling to indicate. An-
other is that it didn’t want Reid at all, for
reasons which it couldn’t explain, but wanted
Dibbs and Lyne still less, also for reasons which
it couldn’t explain, and so put up with Reid
till it could contrive to make itself understood
to some other man whom, for some reason which
it couldn’t explain, it preferred to either of
them. Anyhow, the result of the combined
vote would be that the country had only
partial confidence in the Premier, without im-
plying that it had any confidence at all in the
Leader of the Opposition; and the Party
idea implies that the country must have
absolute confidence in one of them. An
as it would be an outrage on the Pait}
system for Reid to remain in office when the
country hadn’t faith in his fiscal creed; and 1
would be another outrage for Lyne to ho
office without a majority ; and as the country,
if it was prepared to give a majority to some
other Protectionist politician who could carry
out its mandate without losing his principles or
his dignity, couldn’t indicate clearly w
politician it was—the Party machine wou
begin to bump considerably. In fact, wie
the Referendum becomes a regular feature o
the Constitution the clumsy machine of ar
Government is bound to find itself bumpi g
over some very rough ground. _
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Hanging It Up.
New South Wales’ shifty Premier has struck
still another idea for wrecking •
His latest proposal is that the popuar
which is to decide whether N.S.W. wil ,
not join the Commonwealth shall be pos p
till after the general election. No reas
any kind is given for the postponemen y
the statement that George Reid thinks 1 <
able to hang the matter up. The present
vention is to definitely draw up t e
Constitution, and it is generally agree t
will have fixed up a good or bad or in( , ,
one by the end of February, or, at a .
some time in March. Then there is a s
nothing to prevent its work be,ng f J U epl ance
the popular vote straight away, for r


or rejection, when the Convention debates will
have so far drawn attention to the matter that
there is a chance of getting the community to
roll up and give the necessary 80,000 votes.
Also, there is nothing to prevent the vote being
taken at the general election in June, when the
people are rolling up to the poll in any
case, and there is a still greater chance of get-
ting the necessary 80,000 votes. The visible
ends to be obtained by a postponement for four
or five months are that the subject will then
have grown cold ; the Convention will be ancient
history ; the little excitement it created will
have been forgotten; the weariness of long
delay will have made the very name of Federa-
tion more or less objectionable to a great, tired
mass of the community ; and the battle of local
politics will have distracted attention, in a
great measure, from this big national question.
It is a well-known fact that when two elec-
tions take place within a short time of each
other, the voting at the second one is almost
always very small, no matter if the issue is the
same, and that fact alone counts for something
in diminishing the chances. Again, the inter-
val will come in very handy by giving the anti-
Federal crowd in Parliament another show.
The delays which have already taken place
gave them and the turncoat Premier a
chance to raise the minimum affirmative vote
required for the acceptance of the Federal
Constitution from 50,000 to 80,000. The
proposed extra delay will leave them a
whole Parliamentary session in which to make
further alterations in the Federal Enabl-
ing Act. And if the Constitution drafted
by the third Convention is not sufficiently
narrow and provincial in sentiment, The Bul-
letin is prepared to wager a small sum that
there will be an attempt to further alter that
Act—either by again raising the minimum vote,
perhaps to 100,000 or 120,000, or by enacting
that Parliament may alter the draft Constitu-
tion, and so wrecking the agreement arrived
at, or by repealing the Federal Enabling Act
altogether, so that the matter shall not be sub-
mitted to the people at all. These are the
possibilities by which the delay may ruin the
Federal movement, and there isn’t one possi-
bility, so far as The Bulletin can see, by
which it will help the national cause along.
Even George Reid doesn’t profess to show any
good cause for this proposed further breach of
the Federal pact. And the pity is that the
matter can’t be mended, unless something unex-
pected happens, by the violent ejection of Reid
next June. Under the accursed party system
the only present visible alternative is Lyne.
And the difference between Lyne and Reid in
this matter is that one is a long, gaunt enemy
openly, and the other is a short, barrel-shaped
one in secret.
As to Some Celebrities.
Bernard Espinass* to The Bulletin :
Australia should, ere long, gain a reputation either for
setting up a standard of criticism more exacting than
that which seems to prevail in most other parts of the
world, or as a country from which the “celebrity” had
better keep away for his own sake. As a matter of fact,
the latter would appear to be an inevitable sequitur, in
any case. How many celebrities, who have attained to
pre-eminence in various walks of life elsewhere, have,
after journeying out to this sun-dried continent, been
straightway summed-up in the pregnant phrase, “ He
ain’t wot he’s cracked up ter be!” In fact, has this
half-baked island yet taken to its arms one who has
proved to be what he or she was cracked-up to
be ? Johnny Toole, for nearly half-a-century, was
accepted by Englishers as the low-comedian of the
Victorian era, yet Australia found him to be a prosy
individual whose humor consisted in wearing baggy
trousers and getting into corners. Wilhelmj was styled
“ Prince of Violinists ” ; one Melbourne paper referred to
him as a “ fiddler.” The consensus of opinion (not news-
paper opinion, but “bar” and “pavement” opinion),
passed upon Sarah Bernhardt was “a fine actress,
but nothing to rave about.” Antoinette Sterling,
Talmage, Mark Twain, Zimmerman, all disappoint-
ing, not what they were cracked up to be, as per
Australian judgment. And the list may be stretched to
include almost every visitor with a “made” reputation
who has ever come out here. Look at Nat Goodwin,
America’s “ leading actor”! And now there’s Ranji.
One more who has failed to realise expectations founded
on the “cracking-up” process. Once out for a
“ duck ” ; once clean - bowled, and “ out for 6.”
“ We’ve got • champions ’ ’ll do as good as that," says the
man in the street. Either the Australian expects too
much, and must suffer a corresponding reaction, which
finds its own (the true) level after all, or he is right and
the “ celebrity,” in nine cases out of ten, is not up to
advt. standard. The latter’s friends, of course, assert that
the “ colonial” doesn’t know a good thing when he sees
it. But I have a midway theory. The Australian is es-
sentially a nil admirari animal. He has a rooted convic-
tion that anything that is “ boomed ” is trading on a
bubble reputation which it is his bounden duty and
peculiar privilege to prick. And so he goes to see his
“ celebrity ” prepared beforehand to be disillusionised.
And, as such a state of mind is the most fruit-
ful soil to sow seeds of discontent in, it follows
that he generally is disillusionised. The celebrity
is a queer biped,_ too: he requires “petting” in
order to show off his paces ; above all things, a sympa-
thetic audience, and one that jumps with him. Conse-
quently, when he faces the Australians (who are mostly
“on their own ■’ as to judgment, and don’t care a rap for
anybody’s “name”), he seldom appears to the best ad-
vantage. He feels like Twain's guide when the party
refused to be interested or surprised at anything. And
once the celebrity is out of his atmosphere he is just
ordinary, common, clay man. Goodwin was a notable
example of this. Ranji is another. He is undoubtedly
a good cricketer, and just now the Adelaide barrackers
have themselves prevented him from showing that
he is a great one. Persistent jeering robs any
man of his nerve. Which, by the way, obtrudes
the failing of the average Australian. He can’t
stand criticism if adverse to himself or his coun-
try. Praise he is as fond of as a child, but criticism of
almost any kind moves him to “barrack.” Hence his
inability in the present instance to dissociate, in the
dual capacity of cricketer and critic, the man with the
bat from the man with the pen. In effect, the Australian
himself kills his “celebrity” goose and then blames it
because it doesn’t lay golden eggs.

A Dual Referendum.
The Victorian Premier still sticks to his ridi-
culous scheme of the “ dual referendum ” to
settle Federal deadlocks, which, considering
Turner’s usual level-headedness, is rather re-
markable. In one House of the Commonwealth
Parliament it is proposed that representation
shall go by population, so N.S.W. and Victoria
can command a majority. In the other it will
go by States, so Tasmania, S.A., and Westralia
cau command a majority. If one House is
solid for a measure and the other is solid
against it, the general meaning will be that the
majority of people are on one side and the
majority of States on the other. Turner pro-
poses to get over the difficulty by submitting
the matter to a popular vote, and if the majority
of the people avd the majority of the States
say Yes, the matter shall be carried, but not
otherwise. And, seeing that if the majority
both of the people and the States felt inclined
to say Yes, the two Houses would be in agree-
ment and there would be no deadlock to settle,
Turner’s scheme isn’t any settlement at all, and
why the author of the dreadful thing can’t
see it passes comprehension. Owing to the
constitution of the Commonwealth Parliament,
so far as it has been agreed on, the Referendum
seems of very little use for adjusting disputes
between the two Houses. One of them will
speak for the majority of the people— in other
words, it will mainly represent N.S.W. and
Vic. The other will speak for the majority of
States, and represent mainly the three pro-
vinces which have no populations worth men-
tioning—Tasmania, Westralia, and S.A. If
they disagree, and the matter is referred to the
people, a majority vote toi decide, that is prac-
tically the same as leaving the matter to the
Lower House, where the majority of the people
runs the show, and there is no need for any
Referendum. If a majority of the States is to
decide, then that is practically the same as
leaving it to the Upper House, where the
majority of States runs the show, and there
is no use for any Referendum. If it takes a
majority of both States and people to decide,
that is leaving the deadlock just where it
was, and again there is no use for the
Referendum. Obviously, neither House will
agree to a Referendum at which the
other House’s constituency will have the
sole power of decision, and yet a Referendum
at which the constituencies of both Houses
have to agree before anything can be done is of
no more use than a wheelbarrow or an old boot.
Turner’s wild fad is also noteworthy, inasmuch
as it gives three chances to one against any-
thing being done. It provides one way in
which something can be done—by the majority
of both States and people saying Yes. On the
other hand, if the majority of States says No.
or the majority of the people says No, or both
majorities say No, things will remain as they
were—which is altogether too heavy odds in
favor of things always remaining as they were.
The only expedient likely to meet cases of
hopeless disagreement between the two Houses,
so far as The Bulletin can see, is to combine
them in one and let the combined majority de-
cide. The process will be quick and cheap,
whereas an all-Australian Referendum would
cost several months and probably about £50,000.
It w'ill give both the State majority and the
popular majority a show. And it will give a
definite answer, which Turner’s referendum
often wouldn’t.
Departure of Capital.
Melbourne Punch, which is decidedly more
democratic than it used to be, has still
occasional relapses into Toryism. On these
occasions it wails like a banshee in a moated
grange about the way capital is being driven
out of the country by the hobnailed legislation
of a dreadful democracy. A few days ago the
moaning Punch broke in foam upon the beach
with a remark that “the calling away of so
much English money w'as a very serious matter
indeed. Also the causes of the regrettable exit
were, “ the low rate of interest ruling for first-
class securities, the heavy taxation under the
Land and Income-tax, and the uncertainty as
to future legislation in this free and demo-
cratic country.” It finished up with the usual
plaint that the Turner Ministry should take
“ a broader and more statesmanlike grasp of
Governmental questions,” which is the old
trouble ; Punch always thinks the Government
should do something in a large, broad way, but
never by any chance explains exactly w'hat it
should do or what broad way it should do
it. The “uncertainty as to future legisla-
tion ” in Victoria is a weird complaint
anyhow; future legislation is always un-
certain in every country ; so is every other
thing. Meanwhile, as regards the departure of
British capital—which presumably means Bri-
tish cash, as the lands, buildings, railways,
harbors, and similar heavy and securely-nailed-
down forms of wealth haven’t departed much—
it appears that the visible coin in the province
at 31st December, 1897, as shown by the bank-
ing returns, was £7,358,000. And in 1891,
when there was no democratic party in power,
and no uncertainty about derhocratic legislation,
because such a thing wasn’t then dreamt of in
Victoria, and the banks hadn’t burst, and there
was no income-tax at all, and the province was
still bringing in vast sums of British money in
the form of loans, the visible cash—also as
shown by the banking returns—was £6,956,640.
These disgusting figures seem to show that
Victoria has £400,000 more coin, now that
British capital has been flying from the pro-
vince for years and confidence has been
wrecked by democratic legislation and Income
Tax, than it bad when confidence was quite
sound under a Tory Administration, and
British money was arriving in large bagsful
by every steamer. Under these conditions,
the “withdrawal of British capital,” which
the Tory always declares is going on “ some-
thing awful ” when his gang isn’t on top,
looks like a very small ghost indeed a
baby ghost that is leading an unhealthy
existence on tinned milk, and not growing
worth a cent. Every province has had a turn
at that bogey now. Maoriland got it particu-
larly bad in ’9l, at the beginning of the Bal-
lance era, but, after capital has fled out of the
province for seven years, there seems to be more
capital in it than ever before.
If Premier Reid comes back to power after the
N.S. W. general elections he will not be averse,
he has allowed it to be understood, to having a
larger lay element in the composition of his
Cabinet. Law reform is impossible with the
present proportion of lawyers in the Cabinet
and the House. “You keep them out of the
House,” said Reid one day lately, “and I’ll
fix the Cabinet to malcli.’ But Reid’s Cabinet
has stood at seven lawyers (or rather legal
practitioners) out of ten—which greatly exceeds
the proportions in Parliament. Law reform
made considerable strides in S. Australia
because, as Kingston once pointed out, there
were only three legal men in the Assembly
there. The Cabinet has more influence on the
making of laws than the body of the House,
and more than one half of if should be com-
posed of laymen, whatever the proportions of
lawyer to layman in the Legislature. In fact,
a leader of Opposition would only do his duty
in tabling a want of confidence motion as re-
gards any Cabinet, the composition of which
shows a predominance of the lawyer element.
A few months back a Church of England vicar,
or rector, or something, writing in the Con-
temporary, referred to the decadence of the
Wesleyan clergy ; “ while in his private
capacity a Methodist, as a rule, will go beyond
a Scotchman in his efforts to make the best of
the main chance.” Now, this evolution pre-
sents an interesting problem, and The Bulletin
has been wrestling with it. Having no State
aid and no family livings, the Methodist parsons
have had to hustle all the time, and the most
successful of them have, generally speaking,
been those who have been able to beg best.
But, if the adherents parted every time a most
persuasive appeal was made, they would be
perpetually empty - pocketed. Result the
Methodist, more than most men, is trained to
harden his heart; and that is the beginning
and the ending of his special aptitude for
making and holding on to the cash. Watch if
the operations of Booth’s battlers don’t have
the same effect !
4
The proposal to get the charters of Australian
universities altered so as to allow them to con-
fer divinity-degrees is one which was, sooner
or later, to be expected. But it is one which
should be none the less strenuously opposed, on
the simple grounds that a university is a State
institution, and that the State—an absolutely
secular institution—doesn’t, or shouldn’t, know
anything whatever about so-called divinity. It
is the business of the State to provide instruc-
tion only, either in what can be known, or in
what is of demonstrable benefit to the mind of
man, and divinity not only deals with what
cannot possibly be known, but has ever been
the greatest clog upon true intellectual advance-
ment. Theology is the dry-rot of the mind,
and the case is, therefore, essentially one of
obsta principiis.
The French are extremists. The middle course
of more phlegmatic races is rarely, if ever,
adopted by them—in matters political, at any
rate. It is, therefore, within cooee of proba-
bility that the telegram is correct which
informs us that New Caledonia is to be relieved
of its convict strain, and that henceforth
French black sheep will be sent to Kerguelen’s
Land. Now, Cayenne, on the equator, and
Kerguelen’s Land, in the 40’s, are surely anti-
thetical points of climate. It may be, however,
that to the French genius it is wise and politic
to consider this question of latitude in relation
to the offal of France. Even the French
criminal may be held in check by the reflection
that if he is nabbed he will have to either
frizzle or freeze.

Cup. They, of course, will complain that,
while they are out of pocket, the absurd
fellows, doncherknow, who take the tickets at
the gates and drive the engines on the Fleming-
ton line have had their pay regularly. Which
is grossly unfair, you see, because if the
Jugginses had owned Gaulus and backed him
for a million they wouldn’t have thought of
keeping the boodle themselves—they would
have divided it equally among all those absurd
fellows at the gates and elsewhere, dontcher-
know, like all other winners do !

Federal Convention item :
Mr. Gordon : “ New South Wales was, of course, in a
peculiar position. She held the body of the tributaries
of the Darling.”
Mr. Reid : “ The whole of them.”
“ I said the great body of them,” answered Mr. Gordon.
“ I say ‘ the whole,’ ” retorted Mr. Reid.
“ Certainly, with the exception of a few’tributaries in
Queensland, which do not amount to much,” Mr. Gordon
admitted.
The above quotation shows that there are a
body of men, picked by election, assembled to
consult about the federation of a continent, the
simple geography of whose principal rivers they
are as ignorant of as the last imported cockney.
Premier Reid stated, and was not contra-
dicted, that New South Wales held the whole
of the tributaries of the Darling, and Gordon,
of S.A., admitted that some insignificant
tributaries did come from Queensland. Let us
see how it stands, and what anyone but a pro-
fessional politician knows. Roughly speaking,
the drainage area of the Darling amounts to
231,000 square miles. Of main tributaries,
Queensland contributes the Condamine (Culgoa),
the real head of the Darling, draining 47,923
square miles ; the head of the M‘lntyre, drain-
ing 15,232 square miles ; the Warrego, draining
26,368 square miles ; and the Paroo, with a
drainage area of over 25,000. Therefore,
Queensland supplies 114,523 square miles of the
drainage area, or nearly half. So far as the
water-flow is concerned, the sub-tropical rains
of Queensland supply most of it. All the
N.S.W. tributaries north of the Lachlan are
not in it compared to the Queensland waters.
And not only does Queensland supply about
half of the drainage area, and the bulk of the
water which Reid claims as being wholly
N.S.W. property, but in actual mileage nearly
one-half of the river system which Reid says is
wholly in N.S.W. is in Queensland! Yet
Reid’s fat-headed ignorance of his own country
was so extensively shared by his neighbours
that no one knew enough to contradict him.
Some day, when all the provinces are federated,
and each has absolute control of its own river
system, it will suddenly transpire that Queens-
land, should it choose to go in for huge irriga-
tion and conservation works, can pretty nearly
abolish the great N.S.W. river system.
——♦-
The first liberal idea submitted to the third
Federal Convention was thrown out almost in-
stantly. Howe, of S.A., proposed that the
Federal Government should take over the Old
Age Pension business. If the provinces run
it they have to demand that the pensioner
shall have lived for (say) 15 years in one parti-
cular province, otherwise the State with the
most liberal system would have to maintain all
the aged of Australasia. As a natural result, a
man who had wandered a great deal back and
forward among two or three provinces might
reach the age of 152 without having a claim
on any one of them. Federal control would
settle this difficulty. But the dreary M'Millan
asked “ why should the people of one part of
Australia have to bear the poverty of the rest
of Australia ? ” and that settled it. Of course,
it wasn’t proposed that the people of one part of
Australia should bear the poverty of the rest of
Australia ; but that the people of all Australia
should bear the poverty of all Australia. And,
if the balance didn’t come out right to a frac-
tion, it would be no worse than it is now, when
the people of one part of N.S.W. have to bear
the poverty of the rest of N.S.W. The voice
of M'Millan was that of the Scotch hunks cry-
ing in the wilderness about the possibility
of losing an odd halfpenny; and all along
the odd halfpenny has run the entire
business. The proposal for Federal river
control is evidently coming to grief for the
same reason. South Australia has absolute
control over the lower Murray; N.S.W. and
Victoria over the upper Murray ; N.S.W. and
Queensland over the Murray’s principal tribu-
taries. It is suggested that the whole thing
should be lumped under Federal control, but
the general fear that the other fellow will get a
pint of brown, muddy water the best of the
deal blocks the way. Nobody really wants
that pint for himself, but everybody will see
the other fellow dead before he’ll let him have it.
A Cure for Curious Males.
Editor Bulletin, —From your last number I
learn with surprise that the Vic. Govt, reprehends
that ancient and artistic instinct which collects
its citizens in the vicinity of ladies’ bathing-
places. The Vic. Govt., in its individual com-
ponents, can never have read Halked and Sheri-
dan s translation of the seventh epistle of
Aristsenetus, which recounts how Cyrtion was
fishing when a lady came along and asked him to
mind her clothes while she had a swim—
“ I want to bathe, sir, and I wish
You’d watch my clothes while I’m away.”
Cyrtion promised not only for himself, but for his
dog also—
“ I’ll watch your clothes, and by their side
My faithful little dog- shall lie.”
Then the epistle proceeds—
She bow’d, and doff’d her mantle blue ;
Good heavens ! what beauties struck my sight ;•
Thus morn’s sweet ruddy skies I view,
Fresh from the midst of lagging night.
And those are the aesthetic raptures of which
Premier Turner and his graceless crew deprive
young Victoria. The poem charmingly con-
tinues—
Two rising globules at her breast,
Whose swelling throb was such,
They seem’d upheaving to be prest,
And sued impatient for the touch.
The wind was hush’d, the sea was calm,
And in she leap’t, and plough’d the tide;
The froth that bubbled as she swam
Lost all its whiteness by her side.
But soon the wave’s impetuous gush
Dash’d o’er her form a crimson hue;
She blush’d—you’ve seen the rosebud blush
Beneath its morning coat of dew.
And when the lady emerges—
Quick to present her clothes I rush
And towards her stretch my longing arms;
But she repulsed me with a blush,
A blush that added to her charms.
Bage would have sparkled in her eyes;
Yet, still they twinkled lovely, sweet:
As suns in farthest skies
Emit their light without their heat.
Admirable simile ! And the incident closes most
morally. Who can estimate the educational value
of such incidents? Consider the noble stimulus
which Collingwood and Melbourne would receive
if the Vic. Govt, hired a band of ladies to have
their clothes watched at St. Kilda on Sundays
and holidays !—under the strictest ehaperonage, of
course. The Vic. Govt, teaches its children the
three R’s in a dull, mechanical fashion ; it stores
their minds ; but when does it inspire their souls?
Never, so help me Peacock ! Yet how much
more stimulating to the Better Life is the message
of Beauty than the message of Euclid and Colenso !
The Vic, democracy should sing with democratic
Gerald Massey—
Come let us worship Beauty: she hath subtle power to
start
Heroic word and deed out-flashing from the humbles
heart!
Our bliss shall richly overbrim like sunset in the west,
And we shall dream immortal dreams, and banquet with
the Blest;
Come let us worship Beauty with the knightly faith of
old,
O Chivalry of- Labour toiling for the Age of Gold !
After this, it sounds sordid to say that the clothes-
watchers would be sure voters for Govt.; but I say
it, for Govts, are sordid things. The only diffi-
culty would come in procuring, not willing ladies
(the job is just what they would like), but shapely
ones. My experience, professional and otherwise,
enables me to say that probably not one Victorian
girl in a hundred is artistically fit to have her
clothes watched. If the Turner Govt, really thinks
that an indecent zeal urges its populace to lie in
wait outside the ladies’ baths, the cure is obvious.
Don’t arrest the villains ; don’t imprison them ;
simply let them in. One glance would send mis-
guided manhood fleeing, shrieking. There is a lady
of my acquaintance, an ardent and able swimmer,
livingwithin easy distance of certain baths. She has
visited them once. “Such a nightmare, my
friend ! I shall never_ forget it, never ! Excep-
tions —oh, yes! but swallowed in the writhing
crowd of bow legs, curved spines, bulges, protru-
sions, a horror of misshapen flesh ! Oh, it haunts
me ! haunts me ! ” Doubtless enthusiastic and
inexperienced Youth will rush to combat this im-
pression. But I appeal to the sensible average
man in the words of Chris. Wren’s epitaph, Si
monumentum queer is, circumspice! Translated—
If you want an awful example, consider your own
wife. The young Spartan was warned against
drunkenness by the spectacle of the drunken
helot. The young Victorian should be warned
against curiosity by the spectacle of the average
woman without her clothes. Is not that how
Peeping Tom of Coventry was blinded ?— Brigham
Brown, M.D.. F.R.C.S.

“Work and Wages,” the new Lyceum
play, has a title that is even more irrelevant than
the average. At the beginning, the horny toiler
is on strike, and doesn’t receive any wages. He
goes to work once in Act 111., but proceedings
are suspended owing to the immediate blowing-up
of the premises. Later on Sweeney states that he
is making match-boxes at 2|d. a gross, but be isn’t
seen either working or collecting the remunera-
tion. Finally, Diver is going to receive the wages
of sin in the shape of a drop of hemp and a sudden
stoppage, but he dies prematurely in the dock,
and the event doesn’t come off. Johnson Weir,
as the hero, preaches to the strikers the doctrine
of co-operation, while Diver, the sneerful boss
with two wives, regards them like the Almighty
scoffing at a locust. But, apparently, Diver
drinks his huge factory, for presently Johnson
Weir is found owning it and running the place on
philanthropic lines, till Diver blows him up.
After that the latter spends most of his time
hustling.through dark slums to murder his two
wives so that he can rehabilitate bis wrecked for-
tunes with a third marriage. Maud Williamson
is his principal victim, and she repents all through
the story in a bare head and a long black cloak.
Why some girl doesn’t repent just for once with a
hat and feather and a pale frock with a waist to it
this paper knoweth not. The bareheaded girl re-
penting in all sorts of weather seems to be a fixed
stage tradition.
• • • •
Allowing for all discrepancies, however,
the drama is a respectable affair with a good deal ot
meat in it. There is a particularly rich streak of
humor in it when 23 Englishmen set on one solitary
Belgian with bricks, and the latter stands at bay
with a knife. The hero comes on just then, and
reproves the Belgian severely for not fighting in
fair English fashion against his 23 antagonists
armed with bricks, and tells him that Englishmen
don’t use knives. Seeing that they obviously
carry bricks instead, this seems quite true. Pro-
bably the author didn’t know that this was humor.
Diver’s sneerful capitalist is probably the best
single fact in the show, though the co. is a strong
one throughout. Diver has made one or two un-
successful attempts at playing the good man, but
as the gentlemanly ruffian with the scoff and the
cigarette he is always a brilliant success. He
hasn’t the versatility of the lamented Scot Inglis
—or at least he hasn’t developed it so far—but,
viewed as a cold evening-dress scoffer with a smoke
in his mouth, or a gambler who has aces down bis
back, or a really bad capitalist who doesn’t believe
in God, or an insolvent captain with three wives who
sells the fort to the enemy, Diver stands very
nearly alone. Frank Harcourt, King Hedley
and Sweeney especially the latter—always
give a good account of themselves, and
Stirling Whyte, when be is cast for a loath-
some, funny Jew, needs no recommendation.
Johnson Weir also seems to get a little above his
old dead level, and Phil Walsh probably wouldn’t
look quite so dreadful as he does if be wasn’t
playing Scot Inglis’ old character. As it is, be
looks very wooden, and much ashamed of himself,
and terribly embarrassed with his hat and feet.
Four nice girls, Lilian Wheeler, Maud William-
son, Noney Seabrooke, and Ethel Grey, support
the woman part of the show, and when so much
talent and beauty are thrown in for 2s. it must be
admitted that Australia is a cheap country to live
in. But if the management had any sense of
humor it would revise that part of the drama
where Johnson Weir reproves the aged Belgian
for bis un-English conduct in drawing a knife
against the 23 infuriated Britons with rocks, in-
stead of fighting them all at once with his un-
dressed fist.
35 35 35 35
Thornton’s co., at Sydney Criterion, is
rapidly getting through its last nights. “ Charley’s
Aunt ” closed up last week, and the co. has done
a brief season of “ Sweet Lavender,” and has
nearly hustled through the “ Private Secretary.”
That mournful, wrinkled curate has only two more
nights (Thursday and Friday) to go, and on
Saturday Thornton starts from scratch on a very
short race after “Miss Brown.” A special inter-
est attaches to this latter, inasmuch as there is a
perfectly new heiress, and the coffee-colored girl
from Demerara will also be in fresh hands. The
Bulletin has a sympathetic feeling for that
dusky dreamer from the tropics, partlv on
account of the womanly kindness with which she
receives the tattered Miss Brown when that lady
is brought back in handcuffs, torn, muddy, sulky,
with her hair down, and more than half inclined
to bite the spinster who runs the seminary.
So far the new Thornton co. has been quite as
successful as the old one, and up to date it hasn’t
played Lavender with flaming red hair as the old
lot did. Bessie Rignold’s Lavender, with the
sunset hair and the red tomato knob and the
fringe that was one concentrated howl for blood,
•will he long remembered in Sydney.
* 35 35 35
Eight times a week, regularly, “Babes in
the Wood ” unfurls its magnificence at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney), to payable houses. Coleman
and Burke add fresh points now and then to the
bitter altercation about the stolen florin ; and
Uncle occasionally ties himself in different kinds
of knots when he comes home, full of crime and
beer, and fights a bloodthirsty battle with the
keyhole; and sometimes he flays his dog dif-
ferently ; but, in a general way, it is the same
deserving spectacle all the time. Sydney in gene-
ral has taken the babes to its capacious heart, and
the gallery has fallen in love with the girl babe
unanimously. Any eight youths that you could
pick in the gallery would be glad and proud to
jointly boot her traducer—if she had one, and
there was only one of him, and the eight met him
at night, and could all get on top of him before he
knew they were coming, and they could raise any-
thing on his watch afterwards. Also, there isn’t
one respectable male citizen in the d.c. who
wouldn’t defend her character in strong language
anonymously through the telephone any day.
There are plenty of good points about the boy
babe, certainly, but it is the girl babe who has got
the biggest hold on the public soul, and this coun-
try would make a bonfire of its income-tax paper
in her honor any day.
35 35 35 35
The second of Sydney’s three pantomimes
begins to show signs of giving out in the feet.
Rickards announces the last nights of “Jack ” at
Sydney Royal, and though the exact date of its
foundering isn’t fixed, it is understood that that
gaudy spectacle will go down very shortly. Con-
sidering the respective prices, it might fairly
claim to be the most filling Xmas spectacle
in Sydney, and the lavish array of talent and
clothes and ballet might have ensured it a longer
existence if the Government had only put a
.£4,000,000 loan on the market at the right
moment.
