Henry’s silver coin co. is now in its 65th
week and is doing considerable destruction with
‘‘Crime in the Clouds”—a title which suggests
that someone has committed drunkenness and
assault in a balloon.
35 35 35 35
Phil. Newbury has returned to Sydney
Tivoli, and the eight Kellinos, and Austin Rudd,
and little Alma Gray, and other gems of song and
dance keep the globe revolving. Nothing abso-
lutely astonishing has eventuated since last week,
but the usual respectable level of excellence has
been maintained, and some paralysing novelties
may come along any day.
35 35 35 35
The large and varied assortment of God’s
creatures which constitutes Fitzgerald’s circus got
up its last profuse perspiration in Sydney on
Saturday night last, and immediately departed
for M.L. On the preceding Friday night the big
tent, with a bumper audience, most successfully
withstood a tremendous storm. The rain drove
down, hitting the canvas like so many million
bullets, the wind roared, the lions and tigers
roared, the thunder roared, the trainers loudly
cracked their whips, the horses galloped round
and round the circle, and the kiddies whistled
and yelled with delight.
35 35 35 35
Unless Wilson Barrett and the Firm re-
pented at the last moment of their promise to set
aside a certainty for the uncertainty, “The Manx-
man ” retired from Melb. Princess's after Tuesday
night, and “ Virginius ” is taking the floor. “ The
Manxman ” caught on to a public that had not
been laid hold of by “Claudian.” Under the
circumstances “ Virginius ”is probably a mistake
in business tactics, but, in any case, it will not do
much damage. The ancient Roman father will
knife his offspring and curse Appius Claudius for
three nights only. On Saturday the dramatic
version of “The Deemster” is to be produced.
It is handicapped with the title of “ Ben-iny-
Chree,” which sounds very like half-brother to
“ Arrah-na-Pogue, ” and “Colleen Bawn. and
“Savourneen Deelish,” and many other Irish
identities whose hair is turning grey. Therefore
the management cannot dwell too" loudly on the
point that “ Ben-my-Chree ” is another child of
Hall Caine, born on the little islaud where Pete,
Phil, and Kitty first saw the light. The plot is
the story of “ The Deemster,” with special addi-
tions by the author of the novel.
* * • »
Some of the little incidental items in
“ The Manxman ” performance are the most
effective. Kate’s musical cry of “ Pete ! ” fetches
the love-sick fiddler instantly, after maid-ser-
vant Susan has been screaming in vain, and old
man Caesar’s chunks of wood have whizzed past
him unheeded. Then there are the items of Ross
Christian, the caddish cousin, and his pugilistic
pals. Ross makes three brief appearances on the
scene, speaking only a few lines each time, but
every line bites the ear of the audience. The two
pals tell the story of their lives in- a couple
of monosyllables. Another clever bit of work
is the introduction of the choir, practising
a hymn for Caesar’s next open-air service, whilst
that steely-hearted impostor beats time with an
axe. One remembers the choir through Caesar’s
casual remark that the last time he allowed them
to help themselves to refreshment in his pub. they
mixed gin with their beer—in “ mistake ’’ for
water—and got most religiously tanked. These
minor touches of nature are capital, whereas
Wilson Barrett’s ambition for cheap realism
is apt to over-reach itself. He stages a num-
ber of hens, and picking hens are quite un-
suited to a moonlight scene, especially at
matinees. The other Saturday afternoon, when
the shades of night were darkening the first act, a
member of the rooster’s harem announced her in-
tention to lay an egg, and 500 women tittered
vigorously. Astonishing how the female sense
of humor (or is it indecency ?) gets excited at the
thought of egg-laying in public, or at any mention
or a cat s accouchement.
35 35 35 35
“ Dick Whittington ” has plugged along
into the fifth week at Melbourne Royal, and is
just now sprinting. Probably Maggie divested
herself of several pounds avoirdupois whilst the
thermometer was doing its worst, and this accounts
for the name-part having put on much pace lately.
Harry Roberts is likewise travelling faster, not
that he is any thinner than usual, but because the
pantomime track has been rolled, and banked, and
the roar of the multitude is more encouraging
than at first. Also, the fall in temperature agrees
with the Flower Pot Ballet, and the clown doesn’t
suffer from a sudden exodus of perspiring audience
when the comic scenes are about to begin. “ Dick
Whittington ” is rooted in the rich soil of favor-
able circumstances, and the advt. announces that
a second edition will blossom soon with fragrant
beauty. The Bulletin’s nose has never yet de-
tected new fragrance in the second chapter of a
pantomine, but, perhaps, this will prove an excep-
tional case.
** * *
The Rickards show booms along its ever-
lasting way at the Opera house, where the three
Hegelmanns are still alive and doing well. Miss
Lilian Tree’s fine, robust warble accounts for some
of the extra crowding on Saturday nights, and
Michael Nolan, the man who wrote “ Annie
Rooney,” appears to be rather popular despite
his connection with that distressing ballad.
** * *
The M‘Gregors, assisted by Dan Barry,
continue to defeat Ranjitsinhji with great slaugh-
ter at the Alexandra. On the sacred word of
Daniel aforesaid, “ The Relief of Lucknow ” is
guaranteed to act like a tonic to the soul of the
Briton ; also it is good for rheumatism, sore feet,
and many other diseases too numerous to men-
tion. Any drama that can last for a fortnight at
the Alexandra must have a startling feature of one
sort or other. The feature in this production is
Ranji’s rolling eye. It is the most bloodthirsty
and remorseless optic ever staged in this country,
and The Bulletin cordially recommends its
claim to an advance in salary dating from the
15th instant, when it started to roll. On Friday
there is to be a grand military command night at
the Haleck, under the patronage of Lord Wolseley
and the rest of the Salvation Army.

Harmston’s Circus was to conclude a
highly-respectable and well-conducted season in
Melbourne with a couple of performances on
Anniversary Day, but t e season may possibly be
persuaded to last a day or two longer on the old
spot. Before the end of this week, however, the
tent will fold itself up like the Arabs, and steal
silently away to Tasmania.
35 35 35 35
Some men have all the bad luck. The
kindly attempt to provide a handsome purse of
sovereigns for Henry Burton through a benefit
performance at Harmston’s Circus (Melb.) was
considerably cruelled by the weather last Friday
night. Burton, the first of our old time circus
proprietors, is now, in his 75th year, a pensioner
upon the Distressed Actors’ Fund in one of the
cottage homes supported by it. His benefit, with
veterans G. B. W. Lewis and Pollock acting as
ringmasters, had promised to be a much bigger
success—but, alas ! the wind and the rain !

Wybert Reeve, lessee of Adelaide Royal,
is still humping a cinemat. through Maoriiand
with but medium results. Meanwhile, Adelaide,
with a population of 120,000, sighs for good
shows in vain. The Royal is open intermittently,
and the Bijou—of which the travelling Hudson is
lessee —is closed eight months in the year.
At Poverty Point .
Miss Cecily Staunton, the handsome Maorilander with
the fine contralto voice, has gone to Hamburg after her
course of study under Marchesi. At this place, Herr
Spengel, a musical potentate, beamed upon the singer,
and arranged a concert for her. She sang to a colerie of
German critics, who were quite charmed with her vocalisa-
tion. Miss Staunton, however, does not mean to confine
herself to German and French music, but will visit Santley
for lessons in English ballad singing.
Miss Maud Jeffries, the tall, handsome (and i/otrag)lead-
ing lady of the Wilson Barrett co., would make a charm-
ing Rosalind—down to the waist. Her one troublesome
defect is a rolling gait, which, however, comes natural
enough to the character of Kitty in “ The Manxman.”
Ethel Levien, the pretty and vivacious daughter of Vic.
M.L.A. Levien, the onion-king of the South, made her
ddbut in a London burlesque last month. Possessed of
the usual musical ability of the Hebrew race and a plump
and pleasing figure, the young lady should score.
An Australian theatre, usually very well conducted,
was the other night invaded bv a band of larrikins in
evening dress. The circle was'already well filled, and
when these roughs bounded in the performance was well
started. Yelling, jostling each other, jumping over the
backs of seats for no other reason than sheer vanity (for
all seemed sober), they assumed their seats, and spent
the rest of the night loudly barracking each other and
the performers. An inquiry as to why they were not
cooling their heels in a police cell elicited the explanation
that they were a “ party from Govt. House," and though
the writer, after careful inspection, could find nobody
officially connected with that edifice, the crowd comprised
several of the Naicest, and men old enough to know
better.

matinees in Sydney. One of the pantos has since been
called off.”
Reading “ Virginias, ’’ it seems odd that it should be
revived by Wilson Barrett in the year 1898. It is 78 years
since the piece was first produced ; Sheridan Knowles,
then a Glasgow schoolmaster, had scribbled it in bits upon
a schoolboy's slate in leisure moments. The rhetoric
bubbles along the lines like soapsuds down a drain. Thus
Virginius’ description of his infant daughter with her
feeding-bottle :
“ At the generous
And sympathetic fount, that, at her cry,
Sent forth a stream of liquid, living pearl
To cherish her enamell'd veins.”
It is the sort of stuff that Fitz Gibbon could write.

The silliest thing in the “Manxn)an” is Philip’s affec
tation of ignorance of the infant consequence of his
escapade with Kate Cregreen. When she informs him in
hints that are as broad as Euclid’s axioms that he, and
not her husband, is the parent of her baby, he looks
puzzled, and when she is at last forced to tell him in
plain English, he moans “Oh, my God !” and sits down
on a log, mopping' his brow as though an awful, unex-
pected blow had fallen on him. The gallery titters with
a titter that means “as if you didn’t know ! ” Philip is a
dramatised Hall Caine himself, who professes not to
understand (and not to have meant) the obvious meaning
of the big scene in his book, “The Christian.”
Manager : “ You can take some hints for this character
from Hall Caine’s ‘Christian.’” “Who’s Hall Caine?”
“ What, have you never read him ? ” “ No, I never read
anything but plays and in them I never read anything
but my own parts.” “By thunder, you are the actor
that will get on.” “Yes, I do. I have now acted the
villain in * A Day of Reckoning’ for 150 nights, but have
not yet the faintest idea what it’s all about. A splendid
man for my cues.’ t.

Ruth Vincent, daughter of H. H. Vincent, of Austra-
lian repute, has been understudy to Florence St. John.
One night the latter had a headache or something, and
Ruth had to come to the rescue. So effective was her
appearance that Florence made a phenomenal recovery,
and now the pair are such dear friends.

Was Amory Sullivan’s death mentioned in. The Bulle-
tin ? Amory, the youngest son of Barry S., died
some three or four months back. Melb. Royal share-
holders will remember him sadly as the London agent for
George Coppin during the veteran’s unsuccessful syndi-
cate season at the old house in ’9O. The Laura Villiers
co. (which included Julius Knight—but womankind
didn’t “adore" Julius at that time) was one of Amory
Sullivan’s engagements, for which he hardly atoned by
sending Billee Barlow for the “ Dick Whittington ” panto.
Also, he did well by buying at second-hand a gorgeous
lot of costumes from Mrs. Langtry’s production of “Cleo-
patra.” Those costumes, or some of them, have not yet
dropped to pieces. They have appeared in Royal pantos,
every time up to the present, and the weary pressman is
always glad to see them again. Such visions of the past
make one feel younger.

Burton, the veteran Australian circus-man, at flarm-
ston’s, Melb., all hands foregoing their pa y, is a creditable
tribute, more especially as the manageress is only a bird
of passage. Mr. Burton gleans this, perhaps, most because
of his unblemished character as a paymaster." o
Spangler : “ G. B. W. Lewis figured in Harmston’s Melb.
circus as ring-master for Burton's ben., after an absence
of, the bills say, 40 years from the sawdust. He was
instrumental in building the old Melb. Princess’s, as
Astley’s Amphitheatre, when he brougnt a co. from Lon-
don, with Tom Barry as clown. Worth at one time
£30,000, he has been boomed. Lewis performed as an
acrobat, in the Risley business, with Lilliputian Tom, at
Rowe’s circus, Melb., 1854. Tom Lewis, afterwards
familiar as a pantomime clown, has been • long dead.
G.B.W.L. became the most influential theatrical manager
of Calcutta and Bombay, later of the Melb. Bijou, asso-
ciated with his smart wife, the Miss Rose Edouin; of the
profession.”
_ Paradis : “ I observed an actress wearing her wedding-
ring on the boards last night. This indicates a position
which can defy the manager, whose sine qua non is
habitually that the circlet must off. I will not score-under
particularly those few bad men who fancy their own
designs are thus forwarded. They exist most, though
not exclusively, in novels. The manager in geneial is
only actuated by considerations of ‘ business.’ The ring
excites sympathy, but drives away money. It is with a
great sigh that every actress draws off her wedding-ring
for the first time after putting it on. No people are so
superstitious. The funny thing is that you hardly ever
see the wedding-ring on a slage-wife. Stage-manager
says : ‘ Put your ring on.’ Actress says : ‘ No, I’ll not
make a joke of it.’”
“ How old is George Coppin ?”asks a Bulletin corres-
pondent. “ Bar gout he does not look over 60. Fresh,
rosy, plump, with never a wrinkle on his shrewd, good-
tempered visage. Much better-looking now than when
he kept the Auction Mart Tavern, in Hindley-street,
Adelaide, ever so many years ago. He ran the theatre
there at that timp. As a younker then, I can remember
a performance he improvised, called ‘ A Peep into
Coppin’s Auction Mart Tavern.’ All the notables of the
little town were on the stage—the Fishers, Dr. Meymott,
the brothers Williams, Gemmell, and a host whose names
escape me. And there was Coppin, as landlord, waiting
on each and taking his orders, and making him laugh,
the while he wore that grotesque, serious look which was
half his capital as a comedian. How many of these
young men live now? But there is George Coppin, any-
how, who looks as though he were good for another 30
years. He sang ‘Vil'ikins and his Dinah,’ the other
night, with all his old go and gusto. Confound that
gout! But for that we might once again revive the past
in the present, and enjoy, as then, those touches of
quaint, dry humor such as no comedian living now is
capable of illustrating so well as Coppin. Toole isn’t in
the same atmosphere with him.”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Commercial Bank of Australia reports that
the half-year’s profit is £53,222 as compared with £52,529
last half and £41,636 at December, 1896. This covers the
whole period since the new reconstruction, so that thing’s
have improved very steadily with the Commercial since
then. The earnings are now over 31 per cent, on the
shareholders’ money—which is very well up to the
present Australian average. Preference shareholders
get 2 per cent, dividend (£21,171), and £30,000 is carried
to the “ special assets trust reserve account ” to cover any
further possible losses on the old assets of the pre-recon-
struction days. This reserve now stands at £83,000, and
what it will have to amount-to before the old trouble is
finally wiped out is a gamble. If the revival goes on con-
tinuously, the reserve may not be needed at all; and, on
the other hand, the best part of five or ten or twenty
years’ profits may have to go into it before the ordinary
shareholder can get a dividend. Everything depends on
the dreadfully uncertain “ revival.”
During the last half-year the Commercial’s
new deposits have increased by over £400,000. The
profits, as shown above, have increased £7OO. Cash
and liquid securities (£1,903,088) show an increase of
£643,000. The reconstructed deposits still owing have
been reduced by £41,000 ; and £30,000, as above men-
tioned, Iras been added to the reserve which is being ac-
cumulated for the further protection of the reconstructed
depositors, if necessary. Taking all things into account,
the Commercial of Australia appears to be making very
steady progress in every respect, and, though it must
needs be a long time before it is again the overwhelming
institution it was, there is no visible reason why it
shouldn’t get there in time.
• * • *
The steady repayment of the burdensome 4J per
cent, reconstructed deposits (about 80 per cent, of which
will be paid off when the next release is effected in April)
is at last producing an effect on the profits of the Bank of
Victoria. The net takings for the half-year just ended
(£22,729) are the best since June, 1894. Following are
eight half-yearly returns
June,’94 ... £29,921 June,’96 _. £15,068
Dec.,’94 .. 21,087 Dec.,’96 ... 17,928
June,’9s .. 19,863 June,’97 ... 17,877
Dec.,’9s ... 20,631 Dec.,’97 22,729
None of these profits are made, either, by buying up
depreciated deposits, for the Victoria’s deposits are above
par, and can’t be purchased except at a loss. The profits
this half-year run a little over 3 per cent, per annum on
capital. A dividend of 5 per cent. (£10,419) is paid on
preference shares, and the other £12,310 is carried for-
ward. As this makes £58,689 at credit of profit and loss,
The Bulletin again asks why the board doesn’t apply
£50,000 of it towards starting a new reserve-fund ? A
bank without a reserve-fund never amounts to much, and
never will.
During the last 12 months nearly £73,000 of
capital has been written off on account of forfeited shares,
and this sum has gone unobtrusively into the bottomless
gulf of depreciation. It would be interesting to know if
this is all the depreciation that the directors know of;
there wasn’t even the slightest hint that these losses re-
quired to be provided for till the above w T riting-off was
effected. The general appearance of things with the Vic-
toria is very good. Despite repayments of old deposits
the total deposits have only declined £50,000 during the
half-year and amount to considerably over £5,000,000.
Cash and liquid securities (£1,709,547) are £40,000 more
than they were six months ago ; and the large increase in
profits is a notable feature. Still—is there any more de-
preciation to be provided for ?'
* * # «
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney shows
£40,150 profit for the half-year, which is just a shade
under 4 per cent, on the shareholders’ funds (capital
£1,000,000 and reserve £1,010,000). Four per cent, seems
about the Australian level nowadays. But the desperate
accuracy with which this big bank always earns just
enough to keep up its 8 per cent. div. begins to get on
The Bulletin’s nerves For instance :
Profit. 8 p.c. dividend.
Dec., ’94 ... £36,599 _. £36,498
June, ’95 ; ... 38,037 „ 37,984
Dec., ’95 _. 39,474 _. 39,227
June, ’96 ; ... 40,303 _. 39,965
Dec.,’96 ; ... 40,067 _. 40,000
June, ’97 j ... 40,119 ... 40,000
Dec., ’97 1 ~ 40,150 ... 40,000
£274,749 £273,674
This intense exactness in always show-ing just enough
profit to pay the 8 per cent, dividend, and an infinitesimal
fraction over, is very remarkable. In almost all respects,
however, the big bank shows a steady improvement.
Deposits have increased in the half-year by £200,000.
Note circulation has gone up £76,000.. There is a ma°-
mficent reserve of £2,263,000 cash, and in addition the
holding of Govt. Securities has gone up from £712,000 to
£1,009,000. Cash and liquid securities in all equal 35J
per cent, of all liabilities to the public, which is about
the most solid line of defence in Australia. The practice
of holding a solid reserve of State securities is especially
commendable, as they are easily realisable, and yet yield
a return which the coin peacefully festering in the cellar
doesn’t.
* * * *
The Bank of North Queensland, for the half-
3’ear ended 31st December, ’97, has a net profit of £1559.
The following are the profits for the last seven half-years :
Dec., ’94 .. £1674 Dec., ’96 .. £1717
June, ’95 .. 1736 June, ’97 .. 2003
Dec., ’95 .. 1182 Dec., ’97 .. 1559
June, ’96 .. 1703
The net takings for the half-year equal a little over 1$ per
cent, on capital; for the year they run to a shade over If
per cent., and though the results for ’97 are better than
for ’96, while those for ’96 are better than for ’95, the im-
provement is very slow. During the last half-year
deposits have dropped from £260,000 to £233,000, and cash
on hand from £91,500 to £44,500, which are very con-
siderable drops ; still, the 8.N.Q., having a callable capital
that equals the w-hole of its liabilities to the public, must
be regarded as solvent far beyond the Australian banking
average. Even such a solid concern as the Commercial
Banking Co. of Sydney is only backed by unpaid capital
to the extent of one-ninth of its liabilities.
Shareholders’ prospects, however, don’t improve
much. The North Queensland was the first reconstruct
to pay up in full, but seemingly it gained little by the
advt. There is no dividend—there hasn’t been one for
five years—and the accumulation at credit of profit and
loss is only £10,927. At last year’s rate of progress it
will be 45 years before the bank earns 5 per cent, dividend
annually, and, according: to present results, the share-
holders would do a lot better to sell their bank and put
their money in 3 per cent. State securities. The B.N.Q.
apparently hasn’t enough deposits to make the business
pay, and it possibly buys some of them too dear— £so,ooo
of them are fixed for 10 years at 4£ per cent. It exists for
the purpose of lending out £408,000, of which £200,000 is
the shareholders’ own capital, and no bank can make a
decent crust now by lending out its own capital. The
present arrangement is vastly safer for the depositors, no
doubt, but as a means of making dividends it is a great
deal better for a bank to lend out £2,000,000 and for
£1,800,000 of it to be the depositors’ money. Perhaps the
best thing the B.N Q. could do would be to transfer
£150,000 of its capital to a reserve-fund, and then it could
have a fair prospect of paying 5 per cent, dividends on
the other £50,000. The situation would be really the
same, but it would look better.
» * * *
The half-year’s profit of the Royal Bank of
Queensland is £7440, equal to a copper or two over 3£ per
cent, on the shareholders’ funds. The profits for the last
eight half-yearly periods show as follows :
June,’94 .. £6360 June,’96 .. £6445
Dec., ’94 .. 6477 Dec., ’96 .. 6515
June,’9s .. 5946 June,’97 .. 7463
Dec., ’95 .. 6033 Dec., ’97 .. 7440
These figures show a reasonably steady improvement for
four years past. The Royal now declares its usual 2J
per cent, dividend (£5075, including dividend tax), carries
the usual £2OOO to reserve, making it £37,000, and the
remaining £365, added to previous balance, leaves £3685
to credit of profit and loss. The policy of always carry-
ing something to reserve, no matter how small the divi-
dend may be, is a variety of wisdom which should have
more imitators. The business shows a further expansion,
and the cash reserve (£239,000) is very strong, equalling
30 per cent of the liabilities to the public. The only
feature which isn’t wholly favorable concerns the
capital account. During the half-year the Royal of
Queensland has written off £19,483 on account of shares
forfeited for non-payment of calls. This sum hasn’t gone
to reserve nor intoprofit-and-loss—in fact, it hasn’t visibly
gone anywhere, so the presumption is that it has gone to
cover depreciation of the securities. And the absolutely
ideal bank (the kind of bank that never existed) would
always keep its securities written down to their bedrock
value out of the regular profits, and wouldn’t need to use
casual windfalls like this for the purpose. If there was
£19,483 of depreciation that hadn’t been previously men-
tioned, and which is now unobtrusively provided for, it
naturally raises the question : Is there any more deprecia-
tion which isn’t mentioned ; and, if so, how much ?
* * * *
The City Bank of Sydney, with a half-year’s
profit of £8395 (equal to £3 7s. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ funds—£4oo,ooo capital and £100,244
reserve), shows a slight improvement on recent figures.
Profits for the last nine half-years have been as follows :
Dec.,’93 .. „. £10,736 June,’96 .. £B,lOB
June,’94 .. .. 11,468 Dec.,’96 ... ... 8,071
Dec.,’94 .. .. 10,004 June,’97 ... ... 8,048
June,’9s .. .. 10,030 Dec.,’97 .. ... 8,395
Dec.,’9s .. .. 10,020
The usual 4 per cent, dividend absorbs £BOOO, and the re-
maining £395 the largest margin which the City Bank
has shown since ’93, — added to previous balance, makes
£8703 to carry forward. During the half-year, note-circu-
lation has increased £BOOO ; deposits have diminished only
£2OO ; and cash (£292,678) has increased £23,000, and now
represent 25 per cent, of all liabilities to the public—a very
solid reserve.
The present is the fifth half-year in succession
when the City Bank has abstained from the practice of
writing-up premises and furniture, so presumably that
habit is definitely abandoned. Since December, 1895,
these items have stood at exactly the same figure, no allow-
ance being apparently made for depreciation of the chairs
and oil-cloth. How far the same dangerous policy is pur-
sued as regards other depreciation it is impossible to say,
in view of the exceedingly scanty information in the
balance-sheet. The share-market, at all events, insists on
taking a very low-spirited view of the City Bank of
Sydney. J ust before the present balance-sheet came out
its shares (new issue) were being vainly offered at less
than one-third their paid-up value, which is something
most unusual for a steady dividend-paying bank stock,
and the buyer could get 124 per cent, on his money. At
time of writing, after a sharp rise, they are still offered at
much less than half their paid-up price, and yield 9j per
cent. And the next most depressed dividend-paying bank
stock in the Sydney quotations only yields £5 ss. 6d. per
cent.
« * * *
The Federal Bank has so far paid its creditors
Bs. 6d. in the £. If the remaining assets realise their
paper value—which is about as improbable as a dead
prawn walking upstairs—there should be another ss. in
the £ for distribution. Probably half-a-crown will be
nearer the reality.
**• - *
The big and still remunerative National Trus-
tees, Executors and Agency Co. (Melb.) continues to
sacrifice its fine prospects in the rush after huge divi-
dends. The following is an epitome of its last five years’
career. The profit is less allowance made for deprecia-
tion, whereas the co., in its report, shows what it calls the
year’s profit, and then takes the depreciation off it: —
Net profit. Dividend paid. Deficit.
1893 .. £4442 .. 15 per cent. = £4500 .. £5B
1894 ... 2813 .. 10 „ „ = 3000 .. 187
Surplus.
1895 ... 3001 .. 10 „ „ = 3000 .. 1
Deficit.
1896 .. 2986 .. 10 „ „ = 3000 .. 14
1897 .. 2388 .. 8 „ „ = 2400 .. 12
Therefore, in four out of five successive years, the National
Trustees has paid away more than its' earnings, and it is
now £270 poorer than it was in 1893. Between 1893 and
1898 it has earned over 10 per cent, on the average, and
the management wanted bloated dividends so very badly
that it wasn’t satisfied even with that. It has added
nothing to its reserves in these awful five years,
when the necessity for strong reserves was very
great, and its accumulated fund of' £23,400 is
a very weak line of defence, even though it may be rather
stronger than that of some other similar institutions.
And even now, with the profit getting down to very little
more than one-half of what it was in ’93, and nearly half
the capital represented by foreclosed mortgages, the
National Trustees goes on its fine old promiscuous way
just the same. Whether the fall in the profits arises
through a decline of business, or through a very great
drop in the returns from investments, this paper isn’t
positive, but when £13,726 out of the £53,400 share-
holders’ funds are represented by foreclosed mortgages
the position cannot be any too good. And the manage-
ment, judging by the policy pursued with regard to divi-
dends, seems to be awful.
* ♦ * »
The Australian Widows’ Fund Society will get
a healthful shake-up at its annual meeting on the 31st.
For the first time since the Society started business one of
the retiring head-office directors—A. Dick, to wit—has an
opponent in the field. Barrister Ernest Joske, shep-
herded by Attorney-General Isaacs, is appealing for mem-
ber’s proxies on the ground that “ the stimulus of fresh
minds is already needed,” and Mr. A. Dick is by inference
pointed out as the stale mind who ought to be rejected at
the poll. Dick has the advantage of running in double
harness with his old colleague Simon Fraser ; but, on the
other hand, Isaac Isaacs is a powerful name to conjure
with, and the betting is a shade of odds on the New
Blood candidate.
» * * *
Some interesting Bananaland figures for ’97:
Gold escort beat that of ’96 by £454,435 ; meat export
shows an increase of £109,146 ; pearlshell has increased by
£30,794 ; but sugar export has decreased by £182,715, and
wool by £509,821. Brisbane imports are about four times
those of Townsville and five times those of Rockhampton.
In the matter of exports, Rockhampton leads by £12,271,
while Townsville’s exports are £139,459 less than Bris-
bane’s.
• * * *
Two more furnaces have been blown in at the
B.H. Prop, works at Pt. Pirie ; and the volume of smoke
and fumes from the giant stacks on the mine dwindles.
By the way, according to official returns supplied by the
mines under the Lead-poisoning Prevention Act, only 17
cases of “leading” were reported during 1897. There
used to be as many cases in a fortnight in the old car-
bonate days.
* * • *
For the new School of Mines at Broken Hill
the choice of the Public Service Board has been given to
Alfred Rutter, a Fellow of the Chemical Society, trained
under the English technical education system, and lately
experimental chemist to the B.H. Prop. Co.
* * • *
“ A Curlewis Miner * to The Bulletin :—“ At
the Curlewis colliery, 11 miles from Gunnedah, N.S.W.,
where some 30 miners are employed, the ventilation is re-
membersome. The air never reaches the working face —
there is no canvas or other brattice to carry it in. The
thing is just slow killing. The mine people have got a
fire-lamp stuck in a hole for a furnace. An inspector
visits us, but apparently does not see anything wrong
in making men toil in foul air for a bare existence. There
is no inspection of the working places before the miner
sets-to in the morning ; in fact the Mining Act is a dead-
letter here, and no one seems to care a rap. The owners
have the Govt, railways’ contract at 7s. 6d. per ton ; the
miners are paid 2s. 9d. per ton for clean coal, which
should leave a clear profit of at least 3s. per ton.” Then
follow a number of ugly allegations, which The Bulletin
doesn’t see its way to print. Perhaps the Mines Dept,
will take a steady look at the Curlewis colliery aforesaid,
which seems to be a bit out-of-the-way.
* * * •
The recent chopping about of Girilambones is
averred to be merely the result of market, and not mining,
operations.

According to the Lake George (N.S.W.) mines
figures for December, there were smelted during the
month 5174$ tons of sulphide ore and 1199 tons of gossan
ore ; total, 6373 tons—for per ton of ore : 1.78 per cent, of
copper, 2.2dwt. gold, and 4.50 z. of silver. The original
estimate of the value of the metallic contents of the ore
was from 38s. to 40s. per ton. The estimated profit, after
all due allowances, is from 12s. to 18s. per ton, or from
£3BOO to £5700 for the month.
* * * *
The manager of an English co.’s purchase down
south, who had gassed irrationally about the thousands
of tons of 9oz. stuff that he had ready to shove through
the mill, found himself in a tight place recently after
sending into the boxes every rock he could rake out of
the levels, and discovering about enough amalgam on his
plates to keep an active dentistry going for a month. In
this extremity he set all hands to work, raking ton and
half-ton patches out of the little pot-holes and bits of
reef all over the country side, and by this means kept the
mill going a week or two, and secured a retorted cake
about as big as a saucer. That dirty cake, an empty
mine, and a heap of fine machinery represent the avail-
able assets after the expenditure of a great mass of Cock-
ney coin. Does the British investor ever investigate ?
• • • *
Amongst miners companies are known by the
managers they keep, and there is one (not uncommon)
type of manager who uses his position as a means of
securing a cheap complete spree once a fortnight and an
unfailing stream of beer throughout the weeks. He is
the manager who has only room for the man who shouts,
and who sends down the track the miner who neglects to
liquor him at every opportunity. Two or three Bull co’s
in Gippsland have mine-managers of this stamp—known
among the men as “ Suckers.”
• * * *
Still John Bull’s agents nibble at the Great
Southern, Bendigo. Two years ago £40,000 cash was re-
fused. As many months back the price went to £130,000 ;
and George Lansell, who holds a commanding interest,
said no. Now the owners are tempted with £66,000 cash
and £66,000 shares in a £200,000 co. in £1 shares, £20,000
to go to the co.’s credit. The new offer has also been
declined.
* • • •
Vic. Minister of Mines, in reply to a deputation
of Mount Deddick speculators, promised that Govt, would
consider the matter of providing a tramway, or narrow
gauge railway, from the mines to the coast on receipt of
a statement showing the prospects of the mysterious
Deddick lodes, and the amount of stuff likely to be
trucked. Thus far the principal Deddick co., with 1000
acres of leases, employs only 18 men, and, as the Minister
mildly observed, “ when shares are quoted at £lO or £l2
it is only fair to say that a reasonable amount of labor
should be put on the mine.” Mt. Deddicks have been
rushed as high as £l6. Just now they are quoted at
about half that figure for “ small lots.” Very small lots.
* * * *
What has become of the Central Deddicks mine,
anyway? The Central was boomed in a hole-and-corner
manner at the time of its birth, and a few shares were
placed by Melbourne jobbers at a heavy premium before
the show got a quotation. Within a week of being quoted
the shares were knocked clean off the market—no buyers
at any price. Strong suspicion of Scented Dedcats in the
case.
• • • •
W. J. Koehler, B.Sc., successively assistant-
metallurgist, metallurgist, and assistant-general-manager
of the B- H. Prop., is transferring his services to the Westralia
Smelting Co., whose works are now going up at Fremantle.
The £2500 salary offered is the attraction. Mr. Koehler,
who was imported 10 years ago from America as under-
study to Mr. Schlapp, has the further distinction of being
about the most rapid speaker in Australia. Fortunately
for the peace of reporters, his public utterances are few.

“ Although the W.A. goldfields contains many
Jews,” remarks a Bulletin correspondent, “ you see none
on the new rushes—and the writer has been at every one,
up to Lake Darlot. Pioneering is apparently right out of
their line. Is it horror of inconvenience, or lack of en-
durance, or merely disinclination—-or is it their fixed rule
always to allow the cat to pull the chestnuts out of the
fire for them ?”

“The late Captain Glass,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “ did not die at the Gordon. He expired
just over a month ago on ‘ Tindal’s ’. Cause of death, an
affection of the heart. The pig-dog crowd who run the
* show ’ are enough alone to break any man’s heart. Pro-
bably he was worried to death after an eight-months’ up-
hill fight on perhaps the most messed-up ‘ show’ in W.A.
Up-to-date, it is purely a jobber’s mine, but I’ve an- idea
that the old man put them on the right track at last, the
result of which you will very likely hear of shortly.”

The palpable attempt to check a fall in Crown
Lyells defeated its purpose. As soon as the stock was
showing a downward tendency at Melb." Stock Exchange
stimulating reports appeared. “ Mine assured; lode over
40ft. wide, opening up splendidly. . . . Proving
width of lode. . . . Full force of men pushing develop-
ment work with most satisfactory results.” The publica-
tion of this joyful news had the immediate effect of send-
ing the shares down—about three shillings.
* * * •
At a gold-mine in S.A. a tunnel from a shaft
was being put in to meet one driven from the other side
of the hill—and they missed each other by only 60ft.
• • * *
From a single Alaskan mine has already been
taken more gold than equals the price—£2,ooo,ooo—for
which U.S. bought all Alaska from Russia in ’67.

The Whitaker Wright crowd of exploiters is
operating sensationally in the Klondyke region ; its
“ British America Corporation ” was far over-subscribed.
Lord Dufferin and Whitaker Wright each applied for
50,000 shares, and Lord Loch for 25,000—His ex-Ex. must
have struck it rich in South Africa. The Alaska Commer-
cial Co., which has been taken over by the new corpora-
tion at £600,000, is said to have made in 21 years ovei
£4,000,000 profits. The company’s engineers in British
Columbia report that several of the properties acquired
there will pay £lOO,OOO per annum each, to say nothing
of the results from the Mondyke claims.
* • * •
Higby, who designed and built the statue made
of solid Montana silver, for which Ada Rehan, the actress,
posed, now proposes to build one of solid gold to repre
sent President M'Kinley. It will cost £250,000 sterlirg.
will first be exhibited at the American Exposition of ’! 7,
and then in Paris the following year. Not a bad scheme,
as the President can always be melted down for all he is
worth.
“ Always try to get killed.”—Wolseley.
Prof. Agassiz and party are reported as
having lunched at a South Sea island “ upon the
site of the cannibal feasts in days gone by ”; and
as a further example of the fitness of things, they
were shown in the church the stone upon which
the victims used to be brained—now a pedestal for
the baptismal basin. “ Lest we forget! ”
Goose-and-bottle clubs are popular in Mel-
bourne. Members pay in every week, and at
Xmas get a goose and so much drink to take home.
The affair is held at a pub. Brown (small and fat,
no hat, bald-headed, very drunk and limp, drag-
ging dead, plucked goose after him). Big 6ft.
policeman, holding Brown by the arm and walk-
ing him along; policeman with bottle in the other
hand. Brown (to policeman): “Stick to the
goosh, old man ; musht take him—hie —home, you
know.” .
A tale called “Bridget Souchong” looks
original. She comes to Melb. in an immigrant
ship, from the bogs of Hoganland. Then green
and orange unite—she marries a Chinaman with
lots of money, and their children, boy and girl,
are respectively educated, on the highest scale, at
St. Patrick’s College and the Loretto Convent;
but there is the familiar difficulty over getting the
almondy girl married. Monetary affairs take
Bridget and her husband to her native Tipperary.

London Referee on police prosecutions
of fortune-tellers :
It is curiosity far more than credulity that has to do
with the prevalence of fortune-telling, and the gratifica-
tion derived from a “reading” is usually worth all it
costs. Take the case of a couple of servant-girls visiting
in secret some poor old woman who “lays out the cards. ”
It is a delicious experience for them. They know all
about the dark man and the fair man, the rivalry, the
money, and the other episodes of the well-worn tale, but
they like to hear it all over again, and it is cheap at six-
pence. If the State is concerned to prevent people from
being swindled, why does it strain at gnats and swallow
camels? For every person who is done out of sixpence
by a fortune-teller there are thousands rooked in busi-
ness, the State meanwhile standing by and even keeping
a ring for the operators. It is not to learn the future
that people go to a fortune-teller —that’s where the
police theory is faulty ; it is to procure a sensation, and
they get it, so that the bargain is perfectly fair.
O.P. tells The Bulletin a South Sea
story : —Several' years ago, when I was on a
’Frisco whaler, we called at three small islands
—Guam, Sipan, Sension—6oo miles due west
of Honolulu, and we hadn’t time to let go the
mudhooks before two boat-loads of native
women, dressed in a quarter of a yard of rag apiece,
shoved off towards us. They got half-way, then
turned and skedaddled back to shore. Then we
sighted a third boat coming from the opposite
island with a benevolent-looking old white gentle-
man in the stern-sheets. He came aboard,
had a few minutes’ secret conclave with
the skipper, and went back. The follow-
ing morning two boatloads of sable beauties
came aboard and stood bashfully in line to be
picked and wooed, and we duly picked and wooed
’em. When the ship was ready to leave again,
donations in the form of print, cotton, needles,
and matches had to be paid to the ladies. So we
rolled up aft to get them.. “ Oh, it’s all right,” said
the skipper, “ I’ll pay the women.” He then said
something to them in their own tongue, and they
went away. That afternoon the white-bearded
old gentleman came aboard and carted off a boat-
load of print, needles, cotton, and matches. That
was all the women were to see of it, unless they
repurchased with native produce, so one of the
chiefs told me ; and, judging by appearances, the
missionaries were doing a roaring trade in this
line. When we were paid off at ’Frisco I
shipped for Liverpool, and in the shipping-
office at the latter place they used to have
a habit of shoving all sorts of collection-boxes
under the noses of incoming sailors. They
introduced to me the green one, with the
nigger painted on it. Of course, I “performed,”
and told the whole story. Next day, I had three
or four parsons and flat-footed females chasing me
about the Sailors’ Home for details. And I gave
them all the details I had. At length, a long-
tailed gentleman came down, and assured me that
the London Missionary Society had made the
fullest investigation, and found that they had no
connection with the islands I had named, and that
these isles must belong to the American mission.

Society
Evidently the only real remedy for the
Adelaide hospital trouble is to burn the hospital
to the ground, and sack everybody connected with
it, and dissolve Parliament, and have a new
Government, and shift the S.A. capital to Port
Augusta. If all these things are done, and a law
is passed that any allusion to the old heartrending
scuffle shall be visited by a fine of =£loo and flog-
gings, there might be peace in the land. Other-
wise the grandchildren of the present hospital
crowd will be keeping up the row as gaily as ever
in 1950.

Girls of the Sydney Telephone Bureau are
not allowed to favor rude man with even “ Good
morning” or “Good evening.” An insulting
person employed at the G.P.O. was in the habit
of saying “ Good morning” to one of the young
ladies, but never received an answer until the
matron explained to him that the young lady
did not wish to be discourteous, but that
the rules prohibited her from replying to his
salutation. Saint Joseph the Second is appar-
ently determined that the young ladies in his
immediate charge shall not be led astray by any
designing wretch who conceals some deep-laid plot
beneath an apparently harmless “Good morning.”

At a recent fashionable wedding the people
in church were supplied with leaflets on which the
hymns-and-order-of-service were printed in gold.
One of the hymns was the inevitable “ Voice that
breathed o’er Eden,” but the truly awful printer
had printed the first line, “ The vice that breathed
o’er Eden.” Good old printer’s devil!
N. : “ The Thorpdale bushfire, Gippsland,
is eminently providential for Fat Man, who is
looking it up. He will unload a bit of subscrip-
tion, but get it back with severe interest. Thorp-
dale, a flourishing township, is simply gone. But
there can never be another bushfire in this locale,
which is now bald as a baby. An already rich
territory is incalculably refreshed by the ashes.
The subscriptions cannot by any possibility be
enough to set up the long array of Burnt-outs
again. They must sell. So a fine body of inde-
pendent farmers will be shifted into a parcel of
dependents on a syndicate, or individual. Poor
Thrift!”

WEDDED.
[For Tub Bulletin.]
You rose before the day was done;
I watched your shadow flit.
The heart that stirred at setting sun
Throbbed when the lamp was lit.
You spoke : and stilled my pulse’s play.
You looked : that calm was vain.
You whispered all my fears away,
And kissed them back again.
Last week my light commands I gave,
With careless glances such
As queens bestow on favored slave,
And now'—the Master’s touch.
Still lower yet the love-light burned,
Until my terror grew
To peace, as, fearing you, I turned
For comfort unto you.
And what to you was passion blind
To me meant sanctuary—
O, arms encircling close ! O, kind,
Strong shoulders sheltering me !
Maoriland. Constance Clyde.

At time of writing, some £BOO had been
collected for the Tas. bushfire sufferers, “ Tatter-
sall ” Adams and M.L.C. Crossby heading the list
with £lOO each. The Govt, persists in calling the
fire “a national calamity,” but only accepts
responsibility for dealing with it as a national
affair by promising a £ for £ subsidy and appoint-
ing a private committee to disburse the funds.
As the biggest end of the committee is composed
of persons to whom many of the burnt-out settlers
are mortgaged, and, as the public are suspicious
of mortgagees, the latest Braddon-Fysh blunder
is not likely to help sweet Charity’s chariot along
very much.

Ponte : “ Spoke to an adherent of the
Seventh Day Adventist colony located at Coorom-
bong (N.S.W.). Seems a communistic affair.
Full-blown members ‘pool’ all property and
earnings. Outside adherents pay a tithe of their
gross earnings. Local idea of the faith is summed
up thus : —They have two Sundays a week ; they
find you a job if you join, and they anticipate the
millennium very shortly—and still you ask what
the Faith has to recommend it ? ”
N. : “An in-
terviewer tackles,
first, a young lady
engaged teaching
the Chinese, atthe
City Missions,
and, secondly, one
of the Chinamen
operated upon. In
Melb., the Angli-
can and Wesleyan
Missions have
about 30 of these
girls at work, all
volunteers, and
each teaching one
Chinaman to read
English, through
the. New Testa-
ment. The suc-
cess is this way :
Formerly, the
Mission Churches could only get a few inferior
Chinkies to attend the humdrum of services in
the Chinese language ; now, they get the very
pick of the community, the swell Chinamen,
in dandy cutaway black pagets, welly nice new
hard hats, and it is proper form among them
to twiddle a compact umbrella. The Chinese
explanation of this assiduity is that they are
desirous of learning to read the newspapers
and books. This accounts for the vogue of
mission-schools in India, China, and Japan.
In India, the youths lust to get atthe English
novels, while the Japs are all for the papers.”
Yic. Government declines to entertain a
proposal to utilise the cellars of Parliament House
to store wines of the province for a term of years.
Our oath ! How much of that wine would there
be left on the recurrence of “ the boom ” —with a
new Speaker, and a select assortment of New
Women to dally with the mace in the small hours
of the morning ? The experiment was tried about
25 years ago, when Rev. Dr. Bleasdale put down a
lot of wine under Melb. Public Library. Six
months afterwards the worthy divine entered the
cellar to find a beggarly array of empty casks.

It was at the ball marking the termination
of certain protracted festivities that one of the
chief potentates —who in private life looms large
in the softgoods—swelled with honor as he stood
beside the wife of a real live Gov. for the first
quadrille. Opening the conversation with an
observation about the beauty of Australian
women, the lady was met by the glorified bagman
remarking, “ Yes, but their figures are very bad,
especially in this hot climate.” The subject ap-
pealed to his professional instincts, and, growing
eloquent, he continued : “I have often remarked
that Australian women, young and old, should
always have their corsets made to order, as it is
impossible to fit them out of stock. Still,” he per-
sisted, holding his partner out at arm’s length,
“ we have endless makes, and I’ll guarantee we
have at least three shapes in stock that I could
fit you perfectly with.” Here the lady began to
feel tired.
Hermes: “As to lady-medicoes having
more nerve and more sympathy than males:
When writer was in Sydney Hospital, his gash,
being near the equator, was dressed by a man,
while that of the next-bed patient, being in his
face, was fixed up by a nurse. The way that nurse
used to wade in, regardless of pain inflicted,
seemed to prove that she had ten times the nerve
of the male dresser, who used to stop at the slightest
gasp. One day a cockroach took a hand in the
game, and the nurse’s splendid nerve turned out
to be only ghastly indifference to suffering on the
part of the patient.”

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos your remarks
on Indian evidence. Some time ago I paid a
round of visits to the prisons of Co-
lombo. While going over the
female side of the big prison (not
the Hullsdorf, the other one), I
saw a number of Indian women,
some young, some old, and asked
their crimes. I was told, to my
amazement and horror, that
their crime was murder. “ But
you know,” said the superin-
tendent, “ they didn’t actually
commit the murders. The crimes
were committed by male relatives,
and as males are capitally punished,
and females are not, the women
take over the crimes and suffer in
order to save their murderer friends.’*
It appeared that the
authorities of justice
in the island of the
“ spicy breezes ” are
quite well aware that
the women are inno-
cent ; but the evi-
dence is always so
clear and conclusive,
the witnesses so una-
nimous, the details
so well corroborated,
and the prisoners so unanimous in confess-
ing their crimes, that nothing is left for
it but to find them guilty and make a
pretence of punishing them.—Cornstalk.

start with, a cleverer eye for detail, a quicker mind, and
gentler touch, and more sympathy. . . .

WE WANT A CHANGE!

suggestions, followed. by idiotic laughter. In the streets

“ In an Australian cemetery, not long ago,”
writes a Bulletin correspondent, “ a comely
weed-clad young widow was observed —after taste-
fully arranging some flowers on the departed’s
tomb and remaining for some time in silent
prayer—to solace herself with a light luncheon of
bananas, the peels of which she then placed care-
fully amongst the flowers, and departed.” Well,
even Sorrow has tissues to be recuperated, and,
after all, why shouldn’t we eat to a memory as
well as drink to it ! Solomon struck it both
ways when he said: ‘‘Stay me with flagons —
comfort me with apples ! ” There is really, never-
theless, nothing keener amongst the many ironies
of life than the brutal unromanticism of Nature.
“ Your dead are nothing to me,” she says ; “ but
you are alive, and my business is with the living.
So, grieve if you will —but grub you must ! ”
* * * *
A white man and a Kanaka had a scuffle
recently at Port Douglas (Q.), and the cheap
harmless nigger promptly gathered up three or
four hundred strong, and threatened to loot Port
Douglas and carry away its wives and daughters
to be his white Mary. The inhabitants kept off
the mild toiler with revolvers. The only reason
why half-a-dozen N.Q. towns haven’t been looted
by the gentle brown agriculturist is that he is
always short of a leader. If ever a Napoleonic
nigger arises to sool him on, there should be a
picturesque breakage up North.

Brisbane Street prints a yarn which,
slightly varied, has reached The Bulletin, to
effect that when Gov. Lamington came away from
Eulo, W.Q., some of the boys asked him : “ How
did you leave the Queen of Eulo, your Ex-
cellency?” “I don’t know the lady,” said his
Ex. “ Not know the Queen of Eulo ? ” “ I have
not that honor.” “ You better git, mate. I guess
you’re an impostor. You’re not the Guv’ner we
hear those yarns about. He not know the Queen !
Garn ! ” And Gov. Lamington garned.
NEIGHBOUR O' MINE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a pretty brown aottage just over tho
way
Begirt with a garden of roses,
And a virginal vision as fair as the day
The door standing open discloses;
Ah, me! As she daintily glances and trips,
I warrant me little she guesses
Whose soul she hath limed with the lure of her
lips—
Whose heart she hath snared with her
tresses ;
She recks not what riot and ruin take place
In the breast where the thoughts entwine
Of the gentle grace of her sweet young fac&—
This beautiful Neighbor of Mine!
I care not for collect or sermon or creed,
I smile at the words of the preacher;
But a worshipper true is my spirit indeed
When I see the Divine in the Creature;
I know that the benders of orthodox knee
Might cry the confession should shame me—
But give them the glimpse that is granted to
me,
By Heaven! what mortal would blame me 7
For I venture to hold that a heart that is
cold
To the fervour of topics divine,
May yet worship a goddess of mortal mould—
This beautiful Neighbor of Mine!
And I dream, and dream, till the garden swells
To a region of joyaunce Elysian,
Till the silvery tinkle of temple bells
I fancy I hear in a vision;
Till a vot'ry of old for the moment I seem
Whosei orisons rise up and falter,
Till pillar and portico glisten and gleam
Bound fane with its goddess and altar;
Till a weird and wonderfid change takes place ,
And the cottage expands to a shrine,
Through the spell in the grace of her gentle face —
This beautiful Neighbor of Mine!
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
Truly we are a fighting people. In Hyde
Park (Sydney), the other day, a terrific combat
took place between a misguided boy of seven
years, fighting one hand only, and a belligerent
infant of five, who fought all over. Each had his
second, and a tall gent in a shiny hat acted as
timekeeper and referee. About 200 heathens
stood round enjoying the fun and making en-
couraging remarks. Among the crowd were a
well-known medico, a hospital nurse, an individual
garbed as a minister of religion (all presumably in
case of accident only), and two detectives ; a big
majority of the remainder were well-dressed
“ladies and gentlemen,” evidently with a stake
in the country. The bruisers were very ragged
urchins indeed. Had either of them worn the
white collar of Respectability, that well-dressed
crowd would, no doubt, at the outset have
squelched the battle as disgraceful.
* « * *
Commandant Holled Smith, of the Yic.
army, still nurses his resentment against Colonel
Templeton, the paunchy accountant who onetime
marched into the local War Office and hung his
umbrella on Holled Smith’s peg, when that modern
majah-general was outside the beastly kawliny.
Templeton, as officer in charge of the Kolapore
Cup winners, doesn’t seem to have been compli-
mented from head-quarters, neither did head-
quarters condescend to work a knife and fork at
the little Melb. banquet in honor of the rifle
team’s success, possibly because head-quarters
couldn’t have very well stoked its official interior
under the circumstances without putting some
melted butter upon Tem.’s plate. This scornful
attitude of Smith towards the more commercial
warrior will seem very ridiculous in the event of
any European power going to war with Melbourne.
Templeton is, undoubtedly, good at figures, and
if, perchance, headquarters should want to pub-
lish a balance-sheet of the first battle his services
would be absolutely indispensable.
* ♦ * *
A Melb. Argus report speaks with evident
admiration of the “motherly instinct” which
saved a child’s life the other day. Lady of the
house placed a full bottle of chlorodyne on the
table, and went into another room for a few
minutes. Upon her return, she noticed that the
bottle was nearly empty. Instinctively she went
in search of her small daughter, “whom she
found in great pain,” and the doctor was fetched
in time to avert a funeral. How wonderful is a
mother’s love ! Now, a father, or a casual visitor,
noticing the sudden removal of chlorodyne from
that bottle, Would have put it down to the cat, no
doubt.

Another
strange death :
Evans and Key,
day-shift in the

Hope on a Tas.
field, sinking in
the rook. Their
braceman, a young
fellow lately re-
cruited from a
local potato-mine,
whil s t fooling
around the mouth
of the shaft at crib-
time, dropped a
sharp drill. There
was nobody below
at the time—the
two miners, Evans
and Key, were
on top. indulging
in a smoke after
crib, and yet that
drill falling killed
a man. The brace-
man said nothing
abou t the accident.
That evening,
James Collier,
working below in
the Golden Hope
was struck by a
falling drill, the
point of which
went in at the
base of the skull,
and came out at
the mouth. It
was the drill drop-
ped by the brace-
man on the day-
shift nine hours
earlier. When
the drill first fell
it embedded itself
in a “green”
centre about 50ft.
down. Some tim-
ber sent below in
the bucket by the
braceman on the
four-o’clock-shift dislodged it, and it got the man
it had been waiting for.
* * * *
A fragment of orthodox morality. In the
four years, 1893-96, there were, in N.S.W., 27,309
couples who rejoiced in the birth of their first
child. In 8542 cases the said first child was born
in less than eight months after marriage, and in
6074 of these cases the birth took place in less than
sixmoiithsafter marriage. Consequently there were
probably less than 20,000 cases, out of over 27,000,
when that first child even preserved a semblance
of being above suspicion. Also, in the same
period there were 9916 infants who were openly
illegitimate ; altogether, 18,458 undesirable infants
came to light in the Mother Province within a
year. Apparently, a very large proportion of
N.S.W. marriages are not made in Heaven, but
under the gum-tree, with the laughing jackass to
give the bride away.
* ♦ * *
The lodger’s apotheosis—in a Melb.
court. Wife sued husband for threatening. Hus-
band pleaded that he was an Othello, with the
grim spectre of the lodger lago. He had come
down from the bush. His wife represented that
he could take his meals at home, but it was not
convenient for him there to doss. In other
words, he replied with St. Paul ‘ This one thing
Ido ’—doss. As for the eating of the meat and
partaking of the souchong, he would do that
without Mr. Lodger readily enough, and go any-
where, but an Englishman’s castle was his bolster.
Case dismissed.
* * * *
Loyalty in Exhibition-street, Melb. In
June last there arose in that classic thoroughfare
a Jubilee House, whereat another Jubilee residence
higher up the street waxed wrath at the infringe-
ment of copyright and erected over the door a
plate with the words, “This is the Jubilee House.”
Ask for Jubilee House, and see that you get it.
None genuine without the signature blown in the
bottle.

An unfortunate selector of Croydon (Q.)
recently got himself into trouble through over-
anxiety about the family honor. His daughter
(unmarried) became the mother of a baby, and the
poor devil registered the child as his wife’s—the
daughter (it was alleged) officiating as midwife.
Committed for trial. Such are the victims upon
whom the Law is always ready to pounce.

“ Does any lady or gentleman object to
smoking in this carriage? ” said the smoker. No
one objected. Pipes were filled, when a ladylike
gent drawled out: “ I must object to smoking in
the presence of ladies.” Argument was no use,
and pipes were put out. But one man looked
hard at the objector and growled : “ I beg pardon.
I did not know you were one of the third sex.”
Then there was a long long silence.
The non-smoking carriage was crowded with a
Sydney cricket-team, and the boys were hungry
for a smoke. One old fellow alone objected and
swore he’d report the lot. At next stopping-place
the captain called the guard and asked him to
remove the Old-’un, as he would persist in smok-
ing and annoying them. The Old-’un swore and
howled that he had never used a pipe in his life.
“ Why, he’s got a pipe in his pocket now ! ” said
one of the boys. Out it came, and it was warm.
Then out he went and was shoved into a smoker,
GIVING IT A NAME.
IRATE PARTY : “ What! do you mean to say that I have deliberately made up
a lie?”
PLACID PARTY : “ Well, we’ll call it a Financial Statement if you like! ”
and one of the villains walked out and bought
another pipe, and the train went on.

French-Canadians hold the multiplication
record. In Quebec the father of 12 lawful
children can claim an allotment of 100 a. as a State
reward. In ’95, 1742 proud fathers claimed this,
and one claimed 300 a. on the ground that he was
the father of 36 children ! The wife (still on the
sunny side of 30) of another father of his country,
has already provided him with 17 children —
including two sets of triplets and three pairs of
twins —in five years.

A NEW YEAR’S DAY EPISODE IN THE BIG SCRUB.
(The finish for the “Charcoal-burners’” stakes—last jump, rounding the home turn.)
1ST WARRIGAL : “Hold tight, Bill, he’s only judgin’ ’is steps!”
dressed —opens door to excited person, who re-
quests Mrs. Doctor’s immediate attendance upon
his wife in her confinement. “Very sorry, my
dear sir ! —but it’s quite impossible. The doctor
herself has just been confined of twins!” Tableau.
Another contribution —by a lady medico. In
this case it is a male doctor, and an excited female
tears the bell out by the roots in the dead of night.
The doctor’s wife —who also has excellent reasons
for having her skirt and blouse and boots on —
answers the bell. “ For God’s sake, send the
doctor round at once. My husband’s got the
horrors.” “Very sorry, my dear madam, but it
is quite impossible. The doctor has the horrors
himself.” Yells and loud smashing sounds up-
stairs ! More tableau—stacks of tableau, in fact!!
* * * *
Sometimes the house of Cohen comes out
underneath. A ’cute Brisbane newsboy found
one of the Chosen too hard to draw for a sixpence
for a seasonable paper during the recent silly
season, but “ Striker ” kept at his man, and drew
him (for a very hairy chuckle) when he said
“ Will yer ’ave a copy of the Xmas number of the
Jewish Chronicle, Mr. Cohen?”
* * * *
It is indeed odd how teetotal-bossed con-
cerns go bung. Soda-water appears synonymous
with mental fog. There seems to be a screw loose,
a linchpin out of the water-drinking mind for the
conduct of large affairs. Probably it is in the
lack of sociability. The abstainer is apt to be
hugful of a mean self —has no idea of the scatter-
ing that increaseth. It has been pointed out how
the whisky-corned Melb. building societies have
ridden the storm. The more teetotalism in a
concern, the worse the slough, till the lowest
financial deep is reached, with the two-M. rule of
Munro and Mirams.
The usual summer sailor from the war-
ships in the Derwent is just now providing the
chief recreation in Hobart. One jolly Jack, con-
fined in the local lock-up for absconding from his
vessel, promptly climbed out per skylight and
vanished. A score or two of the clean-shaven
beer-chewers trotted along to a big laundry fire
the other night, and, in defiance of orders, took
charge of the proceedings, to the great delight of
the larrikins and the utter disgust of the local
fire-brigade. The most noisy expression of public
opinion at the fire being apparently in their favor,
the tars performed stupendous deeds of daring, and
most successfully got in at impossible windows,
and climbed uselessly over burning roofs, until
raked fore and aft by well-directed streams from
the hoses of jealous firemen. They couldn’t stand
cold water, so beat a retreat to their vessel, and
next day their admirers wrote letters to the papers
giving the fire-brigades “ beans ” for their
cowardly assault on the gallant tars. The rever-
ence for anything British is even more pronounced
in Tasmania than on the mainland.
* * * *
The present Sir Tatton Sykes, successor
to the good old “sport” who made the name
famous of yore, seems to be suffering considerably
from a wife thirty years younger than himself.
Sir Tatton is 72. Not long ago he repudiated the
purchase of the racing mare La Fleche for .£12,600,
which investment had been made on his behalf by
Lady Sykes. And now he repudiates his ap-
parent signature to £15,000 of promissory notes,
and declares that his partner for life forged it;
a jury has found that the baronet did not sign the
bills. The case is remarkable for the name of the
money-lender who took the paper—Jay. Evi-
dently there is more than one sort of jay engaged
in sixty per shent transactions.
Dear Bulletin, —On 6fch Jan. last the
licensee of a well-known King-street (Sydney)
hotel was fined for selling liquor at 11.22 on Xmas
Eve. Now, if the testifying constable will cover
the amount, I am prepared to lodge £2 2s. in
your hands—the whole to be handed over to the
Sydney Hospital if I succeed in finding for him
at least 12 neighboring hotels in full blast all day
Sunday, and to any hour after 11 on week-nights.
—Fair Play.
* * * *
H.M. : “Paused before a city con-
fectioner’s, the other day, to admire a dainty
design in wedding-cakes. Caressing Cupids and
flower-adorned females chased each other round
this hymeneal sacrifice in all the abandon of un-
conscious nakedness. My appreciation was pre-
sently disturbed by the vicious voice of a soured-
looking woman telling her giddy spouse he ‘ought
to be ashamed of himself stopping to gaze at such
indecency.’ They were an elderly pair, and he
sneaked after her with the uncertain tread of a
guilty man.”
* * * *
In a recent Sydney Factories Act case—
thrown out of court on a technicality—a 15-year-
old girl gave evidence that she had worked from 7
a.m. until 7 p.m., or later (with brief intervals for
meals), for ss. a week. A fraction over one penny
an hour !
* * * *
The cheerfulness with which the average
person will jump forward to identify “the prisoner,
yer worship,” was never better shown than in the
so-called Collingwood (Vic.) “murder” case, the
other Saturday. First, a man found placidly
sleeping on a verandah alongside the corpse
was aroused to announce that said corpse was
that of his poor brother Pat, and with a wail
of anguish he rushed off to break the news to his
mother. He had hardly turned the corner before
another man arrived to declare the cadaver was
“ Poor old Pat,” his brother, and he too rushed
off in tears to tell the family of its misfortune.
Then an elderly female came along and swore
positively that “ she knew the dead man well, and
had paid him for a job he done for her. He was
John Smith.” Later, she changed her mind and
decided that he wasn’t John Smith. And the
corpse turned out, after all, to be John Smith’s.
4.
That popular Melb. non-producer, the
lengthy D. T. Davies, went ’Ome some weeks ago
with a couple of modest gold-mine specs., which
he hoped to “ get off ” on tempting terms, and a
new bike patent. This may, or may not, take the
wheeling world by storm. It is a chainless bicycle,
with an apparently abnormal motive power in two
steel cylinders, the works whereof are the secret
of the inventor, Mr. A. I. Madden. The speci-
men machine that Davies took away with him
seemed to offer the maximum of travel at the
minimum cost of labor to the pedallist, and if the
hidden parts can be trusted not to go wrong under
severe treatment “ Madden’s Chainless ” should
boom presently. This must be the bike which a
’Murkan paper had heard of when it ascribed the
authorship to Victoria’s Chief Justice. In the
event of D. T. Davies forming a London company
to run the patent he will find himself paragraphed
as ex-Speaker-Boomster Matthew of that ilk. One
mistake naturally suggests the other.
Great is the wisdom of the Hobart Jay-
pee. The other day, a grocer was prosecuted for
selling, as mustard, a mixture containing but a
small percentage of that condiment. The up-to-
date justices who heard the complaint dismissed
it, on the ground that informant had asked for the
cheapest mustard, and that, anyhow, the adulterant
was not injurious to health. The board appealed,
when it was decided that a buyer who asked and
paid for mustard ought to get it, whatever the
price charged; and that whether or not the
adulterant was injurious to health was beside the
question. The stuff might even be beneficial to
the health of the consumer, but it was an adul-
terant all the same. So the Jay pees had to take
back their judgment, and fine the grocer. Apropos,
mustard and pepper appear to be as apocryphal
just now in Melbourne as the Book of Esdras.
According to the Govt, analyst, the general public
consumes a great deal more rice-starch than
genuine mustard condiment. Same with pepper,
which is chiefly peas. The manufacturers plead
that everybody knows that the genuine articles
couldn’t be sold at the price charged, and that the
practice complained of is universal in the old
country. So that, for clean, unadulterated front,
you can’t beat the Vic. representative of native
industry in the pepper and mustard line.
OPPOSITE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some men, they say, have got a knack
Of going to the dogs,
And end their days away out-back
On sun-baked plains and bogs.
But with poor me it’s opposite—
Though why, I cannot see ;
No matter where I stand or it.
The doggies come to me !
N.S.W.
R.H.

PRESS NOTICES.
Gisborne (M.L.) Telephone, Jan. 8 : “ A perusal of the
evidence by any unprejudiced person must be convincing
that there was no evidence upon which to convict the
accused, and there is much reason to believe that a hor-
rible mistake has been made. Things would probably
have gone differently with Suffield if he had not been a
working man.”
Grenfell Vedette : “ The trial was undoubtedly
another one of those monstrous proceedings over which
Judge Windeyer so often presided, in which he formed
predilections from the beginning against the prisoner,
and almost forced an adverse verdict down the jury’s
throat.”
Wingham (N.S.W.) Chronicle: “We cannot help
agreeing with what seems to be almost a universal idea,
that Suffield is suffering punishment for a crime he did
not commit.”
Tatura (Vic.) Guardian: “An impartial reader of the
evidence must admit that a re-hearing of the case cannot
be denied the accused.”
Before leaving for the Federal Convention
N.S.W. Lands Minister Carruthers federated —
got married again. So there !
When William the mountebank topped-off
his Kiel deliverance with “Long live his Royal
Highness Prince Henry. Hurrah, hurrah,
hurrah ! ” that ecstatic young man arose, in
slavering adoration, and got off his chest the
following astounding loathsomeness : —
Most Serene Emperor, most powerful King and Lord
illustrious Brother, — ... I am only animated by one
desire to proclaim and preach abroad to all who will hear
—as well as those who will not hear—the gospel of your
Majesty’s consecrated person. This I will have inscribed
on my banner, and will bear it wherever I may go. . .
1 raise my glass and call upon those who with me enjoy
the happy privilege of being permitted to go forth, to
remember this day, to impress the person of the Emperor
upon their minds,' and to let the cry resound far out into
the world, our Most Serene Mighty Beloved Emperor,
King, and Master, for ever and ever. Hurrah, hurrah
hurrah !
All which, if not the raving of an actual lunatic,
is sufficiently like thereunto to suggest tha.t, after
all, specific brain-disorder may be responsible for
the insensate caperings of this unspeakable pair
of imperial bounders.
