A WOMAN’S LETTER.
, Sydney, January 25,1898.
Mr Dear Myee—
A very floppy, tired-looking collection of cricket
enthusiasts decorated the grand-stand at the
match between N.S. W. and Victoria on Saturday.
Whatever the women went for it wasn’t the game,
for they only occasionally threw a languid glance
on the field, and passed the rest of the time in
chatter. One girl was reading a novel with deep
interest. The dressing ran mostly to washed-out
cotton frocks tied with wilted wisps of waist-
ribbon. A yellow muslin with a bilious green
streak suggested eggs-and-spinauh, and was the
vividest creation of the afternoon. From the
back of the stand the tawdry show of shabby hats
looked like a vegetable garden wilted by a heat-
wave. His Ex. made his first appearance since
his return from Geyser-land, but didn’t seem to
court inquiry—he and the new Admiral kept
quietly in the background most of the time, with-
out God-save or any other demonstration of grovel.
To women, cricket is certainly most tedious to
watch—this whether you understand the game,
from having played it in your early girlhood, or
not. men seem to retain the instincts of
babyhood longer than women do. If anybody
doubts this let him or her take note of the veteran
of the N.S.W. v. Vic. match. To see him saunter-
ing up and down the lawn, with his hands in his
pockets and a beautiful 10-year-old simper on his
50-year-old face, suggests to me the necessity of
putting the gentleman in knickerbockers, with
one pocket full of marbles and the other full of
cake, in order to complete the figure. But no
doubt he looks much more formidable when sign-
ing the receipt for his salary.
Ihe lady cricketers of Sydney propose working
up their little agony by having a match with the
theatrical gentlemen in March; the excuse for
airing their calves in public being— sweet Charity.
If that is so, much more gate would result from
rounders, interspersed with kiss-in-the-ring and
find-the-slipper. Then the ladies and the styge
gentlemen would meet an common ground and
thoroughly understand each other; and every-
body, including the forgotten crowd, would have
a real good time. What a draw it would be:
the prospect of George Rignold in Henry V.
costume dropping the handkerchief and being run
to earth by some thin slab of a girl !
The summer sales now in full blast at most of
the big draperies are a harvest for the shop-lifter.
Crowds of bargain-hunting women who line up in
front of Mark Foy’s, and grumble loudly at hav-
ing to wait till the doors are opened that they may
be drafted in like sheep, don’t all know the real
reason why they are let in by one door and out in
single file by another—where a zealous (and ex-
perienced) door-keeper stands and eyes them nar-
rowly over. This cautious treatment of the
shopping battalions aim two bricks at one fowl:
A/r S a h -£ ge at * vt ‘ > also > it keeps a check on looters.
Mark Foy says that in every hundred customers
who rush in when the doors are opened five are
plainly “on the make.” These angels filch all
kinds of things, can stow away a large mantle as
well aB a bottle of perfume. They are dressed for
the part by wearing under their frocks black
calico bloomers, tied tightly round their ankles,
and into these bags they slip their spoil through
long slits purposely made in their skirts. The
proprietor once ran to earth, in a near-hand hotel,
a looter who was stocking-stuffed as if by a
pillaging Santa Claus, and made her unload a silk
cape, a case of scents, odds and ends «f lace and
frillery, and thirteen pairs of boots !
Invitations for a recent Northern wedding m
parvenu high life were restricted to eminent
officials, the very Naicest, and those members of
the wealthy lower orders who could be depended
"P™ 1 for a fair-priced present in exchange for the
thrill of seeing their names figuring at the big
shivoo in the sassiety papers. When the cere-
mony and reception were over, a lordly flunkey,
evidently hired at enormous expense, stepped for-
ward and loudly yelled the names of the prin-
cipals whom he escorted to another room, where a
lavish gorge had been laid out. The guests natu-
rally prepared to follow, but after the “big”
people had begun to vigorously stoke themselves,
and the Naicest were grabbing at the edibles, the
wealthy lower orders, who had set up the young
couple in furniture, found themselves blocked by
the aforementioned flunkey, who referred them to
a buffet in another room, where sandwiches and
“colonial” awaited them. This is the first
instance of the sort since the historic occasion
when a Southern Mayor divided his guests into
two batches at the annual ball.
History repeats itself. Minister of Lands Car-
ruthers, in marrying fresh on the heels of a sensa-
tional court-case, follows the illustrious example of
Sir Chas. Dilke, who was accepted by Mrs. Mark
Patti son immediately after his private life had
been dissected by the world. Gordon-Cumming
wedded his gilded true-love and fell on his feet
financially while the whole world was ringing with
the baccarat scandal. And now Miss Burnett
courageously steps into the shoes vacated by Mrs.
Joe No. 1. To work a woman up into a frenzy of
devotion the man has only to pose before his ador-
ing one as a maligned and suffering hero and she
rushes to his side to console her darling for the
misjudgments of a base and slanderous world;
Anybody who goes to the Criterion expecting
to see dressy drama in the revival of “ Sweet
Lavender,” and other frocky plays, draws a
blank. The only actress of the co. who calculates
sartorial effect is Miss Carew. Her Mrs. Gil-
fillian was Mrs. Grundy Dersonified; her curls
were as rigid as little telescopes all the time, and'
she swept a purple velvet train about like a pall of
all the proprieties. But all the other gowns had!
an inartistic hitch in front which revealed toe
much foot—as if the wearers were ignorant of the
effect of stage slope on the front view of a gown.
This defect makes the performers’ feet the great
solid facts of the play. And from the stalls the
view of boots of all sizes blots out much of the
pathos proceeding on the stage.
A theatrical rumpus has been the talk of the
town. A well-known Blockite was treating a
theatrical lady to an oyster supper, when a male
theatrical arrived on the scene and objected bub
couldn’t knock the other man down because he,
the male theatrical aforesaid, is such a little one.
But there was a vulgar row, and the other man
Eassed the night in a dungeon cell. The story has
een blown up to enormous dimensions, but the
above simple tale contains all the essential facts.
All the time Her Ex. has been away the various
goody little organisations headed by her patron-
age have been languishing to inanition. All the
Friendly Girls and Praying Mothers and Needle-
work Guilds and Dorcas coteries and other charity
fads have been unchronicled while their titled
backer was absent, for the dailies don’t give space
except to the philanthropic palavers of the high and
mighty, Self-advt. is the very soul of charity.
The pars, announcing the return of the Governor’s
wife say nothing about the Hon. Alice, the daugh-
ter who accompanied her and shared the memor-
able weepful send-off from Margaret-street pier ;
and her other chick, Douglas, has been left behind
at school. So the diminished family will probably
obtrude itself less than ever on public notice.
A select contingent of the bong-tong went to
Redfern to welcome the Vic. Governor’s return
from Jenolan Caves. One waiting personage was
arrayed in a Knowlesian garb of black tailcoat,
tall hat and white trousers. The women's eyes
were gladdened by a sight of Lady B.’s travelling
dress, which was accurately cut. Like most
Englishwomen, she looks better in tailor-built
gowns than in fussy evening confections, with
elaborate fixings.
The process of cleaning-up is going on vigorously
at Admiralty House, which is going to have, in
the near future, a missis residing there, when Mrs.
Pearson takes up her abode, Sassiety is anxiously
looking forward to a good time, and expects to
be entertained at more festive functions than the
late regime spread itself over. Garden-parties
were the most frequent form of social agony on
the rockyjledge where the house is perched, and
guests scrambled like goats about the precipitous
and breezy grass-patch. Mrs. Bridge didn’t live
here, because she is a member of an exclusive
little social and intellectual coterie at ’ome, and
she feared, if she left it for long, she would drop
out of the procession altogether. Besides, the
admiral’s wife has a lop-sided kind of position
here ; she must play second-fiddle to the Governor’s
wife, and, as she can’t reckon herself on a level
with ordinary grovellers, she has to exercise much
tactfulness to balance herself aright on her tight
rope.
A blessed relief from the eternal “ pretty wed-
ding ” indoors was given by a bold innovation at
a Vic. country town. To vary the monotony the
alleged happy couple were made one under the
shade of two blackwood trees garlanded for the
occasion with white flower-festoons. This pas-
toral rite is evidently the improved, up-to-date
Australian form of being hitched under a hedge ;
but it’s a risky experiment to make here : the im-
pressiveness of the ceremony would be almost cer-
tain to be marred by a ’skeeter alighting on the
parson’s nose, or by the bridegroom jumping away
with a wild yell from an adjacent bull-ant’s nest,
or by the bride having to shoot flies off herself all
the time.
After 13 years’ of hard work with the Lieder-
tafel, conductor Delaney has resigned his post, to
the regret of musical circles. It was his grit and
zeal which kept the above society together during
the bank-smashing time when the Metrop. Lieder-
tafel had to go under, but he now finds it neces-
sary to quit, as, besides his organ-playing at St.
Mary’s, ne has much teaching work. Also, his
late illness has told upon him.
A matrimonial engagement which is convulsing
sassiety with interest just now is that of a Melb.
girl, Ethel Greene, daughter of the ex-Commis-
sioner of Railways, who by her own pluck and
ability has made a name and a big business as a
fashionable dressmaker. Some timid snobs turned
their backs on her at first when her career as a
young woman in business began, and afterwards
when they saw that Govt. House and other holy
places were still open to the “person in trade,”
veered round and tried to rush her socially. Now
that the lady is actually going to be married
to (think hard here !) the brother of the husband
of the sister of the Victorian bart. the small fry
are gasping in astonishment. Nothing succeeds
like success.
Our specially-imported nobility has a way nowa-
days of arriving and departing as unostentatiously
as the bad boy who leaves the family mansion for
a night out by climbing quietly over the back
fence so as not to wake the old man and the slum-
bering Towser in the kennel. A certain earl
glided away lately like morning dew. He was
the highest-priced aide ever on an Australian vice-
regal staff. Gov. Brassey gave him .£lOOO, which
was all the scion of Hingland’s aristocracy had to
keep the wolf from the door. For a time he
dangled temptingly before marriageable moneyed
maidens, and reports were published that he was
actually engaged to a local baronet’s sister (after-
wards emphatically denied). But society damsels
cooled off latterly. Racecourses and theatres were
his chief haunts. He left this sorrowing conti-
nent per Orizaba, with the intention of eventually
joining his regiment after a little pasear on the
way.
A certain Sydney stage-manager never swears
at rehearsals, but is bitterly and freezingly sarcas-
tic to the chorus-girls, who are mostly too chuckle-
headed to catch the point of his innuendoes, and
refer to him as a “nice polite man.” Of the
sledge-hammer type of swearing managers, the
brothers Harris (of Drury Lane) were shining
examples (so say pros, who have been directed by
them). Rehearsing a panto. Chas. Harris would
scatter lurid language about till 2 a.m., and then
|brotber Sir Augustus and croak hoarsely,
Gus, just go down and cuss those girls, my voice
has gone.” Lately a local stage-director rehears-
ing a ballet which had got itself badly tangled,
collared a small danseuse with the intention of
putting her into place, and inquired irately,
Now, my child, who the deuce are you ? ” And
the terrified fairy lisped, “ Please, sir, I’m a ‘ nut ’
in the wedding-breakfast ballet! ”
I he latest move in fashionable charity is for
drawing-room tenors and baritones to warble
gratis at philanthropic bun-parties for sailors, un-
employed and other down-trodden persons who
can be coaxed by the prospect of getting out of
the rain for a while to listen to them. A certain
Judge addicted to gospel-meetings and charitable
concerts usually has half-a-dozen singing young
men in his train at these slummy shivoos.
The English cricketers keep on filling their
united w.p.b. with the shoals of invitation cards
which still steadily rain in on the “amachures,”
in spite of the lofty scorn with which the gentle-
men players regard all such courtesies. When at
Bendigo a tea-party was wrecked owing to the
non-arrival of Maclaren & Co. at the festival
specially-arranged, by worshippers in their honor.
The professionals all turned up.
A recently-married Sydney sassiety maiden
wrote a long account of her own wedding and sent
it to a>city paper. Being of an extremely modest
disposition, she naturally did not care to say much
about herself, so she merely wrote that ‘‘The
bride looked a dream of loveliness in a beautiful
and charmingly-made,” &c.
Madame Henry, who has successfully carried on
for some years the only school for the study of
French comedy which N.S. W. possesses, has un-
fortunately suffered a breakdown of health ; while
summering at Bowral she became seriously ill.
Until her recovery her Cercle will be kept to-
gether by Mine. Verner.
Silver is on top at the moment as a fashionable
metal. Every high-toned family owns at least
one “ silver-table,” and no dressing-room is con-
sidered up-to-date unless the owner can afford to
have her hair-brush bristles backed by silver,
while her luxurious lord and master must jerk his
razor-strop out of a new patent silver strop-case,
or the very servants would sneer at him as a hope-
less plebeian. Also the woman who heats her
curling-tongs at the kitchen gas-stove can’t expect
to be on visiting terms with the superior person
who boasts a silver curling-stove in a hammered
metal velvet-lined box. This is where the newly-
married score heavily, because all these things are
generally added to them in the wedding-present
grab. If the silver craze continues and spreads
into the guest-chamber, prudent hosts will have
to chain down all small portable articles in the
spare bedrooms.
That vulgar vegetable, the onion, is the sort of
bulb that no really Naice woman would own up to
eating, but it is much devoured in private by
youth and beauty, as it has quite a reputation as
a complexion-clearer. Boiled onions and a glass
of Btout have formed for years the prosaic mid-
night meal of a bewitching comic-opera soprano,
who would disenchantingly swallow her savory
supper after capturing all the male hearts in her
audience by warbling, in her alluring way, come-
and-kiss-me ditties. The handsome Gaiety Girl,
Maud Hi bson, takes her nightly onion raw and
sliced with vinegar.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“A rather sickening development in the Ranji
craze at Melbourne just now is the rush of small
and giddy girls to get hold of his dusky phiz on a
‘ Cameo * button and pin it outside their padded
breasts. Button portraits of the real Hinglish
cricketers are a drug on the market. The average
damsel who has a ‘full collection’ will gladly
swap away all the white men for another Ranji to
pin elsewhere upon her form divine. In hard
cash the ‘ Prince ’ is worth sixpence, and the stock
has a rising tendency.
“Ranji’s diamond bolt seems to be quite a
popular myth among Australian girls. The value
of this glittering fabrication is estimated at two
million pounds sterling, or thereabouts, and the
dear creatures believe that their own sweet Prince
will keep it in reserve for the last test-match, as a
special device for dazzling the Australian bowlers.
Nobody that I know of has ever seen Ranji’s belt,
and if he possesses such an article he had better
produce it at once and shine accordingly.
“ The Indian prince, I hear, was almost mobbed
in Adelaide on the second day of the test-match.
A large crowd of women crowded outside his
hotel after the day’s play, and waited patiently
tor a glimpse at their dusky idol. Strange that
whilst Adelaide society women called him ‘ the
dear prince,’ ‘the lower ordahs ’ simply dubbed
him— 1 Liquorice ! ’
“President Zeal and Speaker Mason are between
them running an afternoon tea kiosk in the
grounds of Parliament House whereat tea and
trimmings are served out to the female belongings
of the delegates two afternoons a week. ‘ The
first commoner ’ looms very large at all conven-
tional affairs, and his confused-looking whiskers
and inevitable velvet collar are beauty spots at
every gathering. A walk down Collins-street,
however, makes it a little difficult to take Our
Speaker seriously. Blatantly displayed in a
photographer’s show-case there is a picture of this
Charles I-looking worthy in a fearful and wonder-
ful get-up. The lower half of his anatomy sug-
gests a harlequin in reduced circumstances. It
takes a ballet dancer to show how spangled stock-
ings can be worn convincingly and, for the dignity
of the Chair it would be as well to consign the
aforementioned photo, to oblivion.
“ The Mayoress is like the princess in the fairy
tale—young and fair and wealthy. She is the
ideal entertainer, and on Thursday evening turned
what might have proved but a dreary function to
the women who gathered to see the lions feed, into
a very pleasant shivoo for all concerned. As the
gushing “ Fedora ” of the Akgus remarks in her
inimitable way, ‘the view of the banquet from
the gallery was really very beautiful,’ and the
speeches, until Toby Barton passed the twentieth
lap in his oration and folks began to yawn, were
considered most interesting even to the feeblest
feminine intellect present. The one blank in the
expensive programme was the music. The grand
organ was absurdly out of place when it came to
‘ musically honor ’ the toasts, and papa Plock and
his excellent string musicians were the most missed
men at the whole affair.
“Thrilling annals of bush-fire horrors provide
interesting reading for those at a safe distance.
Think of the cremated corpse at Thorpdale, for
instance, or the Neerim baby, planted up to its
neck in moist earth, and watered all day like a
flower by a sentinel brother, while Dad anil Mums
beat the flames back from the cottage. With the
danger over and the furnace-blast gone by, Babs,
dug up damp and smiling, provided a compara-
tively cool oasis for a hysterical done-brown family
to water still further with thankful tears. Also,
from Thorpdale side, comes word of a plucky
mother, who fought the fire all day, until the
children seemed to be literally toasting before her
eyes. Then she gathered the crying bairns in her
arms, and determined to drown herself, with them,
in the well, so as to save them from the agony of
roasting alive. Happily, the wind changed in the
nick of time, and ‘ the pretty chickens and their
dam ’ were saved—after all.
“ Like all the elements, fire is no respecter of
persons. Here, there, and everywhere, Judges,
fashionable surgeons, and all sorts and conditions
of ‘Nicest’ have been kept busy ‘beating out’
their summer residences. The sole male scion of
a toney old Gisborne (Vic.) clan had enough to do
to save his hearth and home. And he was just
married, the other week, to the dashing only
daughter of an equally well-known local family.
But his married life has been stormy from the
start. On the eve of his wedding, some insect
‘ bunged ’ his eye. His sisters, cousins, and aunts
did the ministering angel by the injured optic all
night. In the morning, however, he arrived at
the altar beaming with happiness, but facially
suggestive of a damaged pug.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
To Thomas Suffield.
Meeting of the Executive Council.—A meeting 01
N.S.W. Executive Council was held yesterday, when the
Lieutenant-Governor (Sir Frederick Parley) presided
The business transacted was of an official and routine
character.
Drink a bumper, Thomas Suffield, water’s cheap
with you, I ween,
To the business that’s “ official ” and the glory of
'* Routine.”
Take no breath of comfort, hoping that the “busi-
ness ” may be yours,
For the heart that beats “ official ” human suffer-
ing never stirs.
But though walls and bars are round you, shutting
out God’s light and air,
You are freer still than they are who are leagued
to keep you there ;
For the flaccid brains that flounder in a cold,
official sea
Are more cramped and bound than you are, and
no key can set them free.
Take this grain of comfort to you, may it help to
heal your soars,
When you feel the sunlight warm you as it filters
through the bars,
Just thank God you are a pris’ner, prison’d MAN,
but not machine,
With a soul for ever handcuffed by Officialdom’s
Routine !
Sydney.
B.E.

Personal Items.
Stated that editor Finlayson, now in Eng-
land, is to succeed Astley Cooper as S.A. Regis-
ter’s London correspondent.
The late father of the Earl of Seafield,
just come of age in Dunedin, occupied at one time
the position of a bailiff at Oamaru, and his
mother is a Salvationist. The young fellow in-
tends to stick to Maoriland.
The trail of the Jubilee. A loyal N.S.
Wales pair had twins born unto them last June.
They christened one Irene Victoria, and the other
Albert Record. Both died a few days ago. l
“ Infantile debility,” the doctor said. Not strong
enough to carry those names.
Every American isn’t a humorist. Some
smart Yanks who run in Melbourne a somewhat
mystic medical institute, where all diseases are
healed “on modern principles,” proudly advertise
that they now occupy the fine oJd house of the
lately-deceased coroner, Youl.
Not one out of ten persons manages to hit
Melb. Mayor MacEacharn’s name at first try.
He relates that when he first hung out his shingle
nobody dared to ask for him by name, but on
and all would point to the brass plate and blankly
inquire “ Is that gentleman in ? ”
It is proposed in S.A. to establish some
sort of memorial of the late P. P. Gillen, King-
ston’s well-beloved Commissioner of Lands. A
start has been made, though it does not seem as if
more than enough to put a sign-board on the
grave will be forthcoming in the end.
The Baron Pierre de Coubertin, who gives,
in the Fortnightly, a Fcench view of the British
Empire, makes some funny blunders about Aus-
tralia. He calls Deakin “the Prime Minister of
Victoria,” and attributes to him a belief that the
Pacifio Ocean will become an English lake. The
cruiser Ringarooma he calls the “ Kangarooma,”
and says, truly enough, that the name (as he gives
it) has an Australian character.
Don’t trust promises made regarding a fund
for “ perpetuating the memory of a good man.” In
the awful heat-wave of two years ago one of the
most experienced and universally-respected of
N.S.W. Land Board chairmen—a man with a
clean record of about 40 years’ service in the back
country-died beside the track while travelling on
duty. Big promises flowed in for a mighty monu-
ment—yet the widow has done all in that way
that has actually been accomplished.
DEPUTY O'BRIEN, Melb . Fire Brigade .
Photo., Ch[?]mont.
A fine, bullet-headed, thick-set hero is
Melb. Fire Brigade Deputy O’Brien. In a pro-
fession which demands pluck, strength and deter-
mination as a matter of ordinary routine, O’Brien’s
distinction, in these respects, is all the more
notable for the unostentatious manner in which
it has been acquired. No “ gallery-work ” in
O’Brien’s exploits; about which, generally, no-
body hears anything until rescued persons or eye-
witnesses come forward to testify. It is O’Brien’s
behaviour after,he has done anything particularly
plucky that make% him so unpopular with
Melb. pressmen. A couple of years ago O’B.
got the Humane Society’s gold-medal for
rescuing Deputy Andy M‘Do wall from the
hold of the Hilaria, burning at Port Melb. pier.
M‘Dowall subsequently died from the effects of
the smoke, and O’Brien, who was then super.,
was promoted to the vacancy. During the big
fire of Flinders-street O’Brien had several narrow
escapes, but his most striking adventure of late
was his determined daring attempt to rescue the
sufferers in the South Yarra sewer disaster.
O’Brien penetrated the drive for some distance
again and again, only to be driven back by the
Sas, and at last he fell insensible himself and was
rought to the surface apparently dead. Upon
examination he was not expected to have a chance,
but he pulled round, and after a fortnight’s holiday
at Sorrento, during which he assisted to save the
life of a drowning boy, he went back to his work
as good as new. He has a son in the brigade,
and quiet and suave as the “ old man ” generally
is, he proved himself as good a man at “ language”
as his chief officer, when he heard erroneously
that the boy had been cut off on a roof in the big
fire. O’Brien is extremely popular with his men,
because he never asks anybody to take a risk he
won’t take himself.
Justice Owen, now away on leave, was
designated for N.S.W. Chief Justiceship on the
death of Martin, C. J. The Premier of the day
cabled the offer, but Judge Owen, who was then
also enjoying a holiday, did not receive the mes-
sage, and the post was otherwise filled. The Judge
is a Trinity College man, but has been nearly 40
years in Australia. He lately left the Equity
Bench to afford free scope to his son in that
division of law, the public mind showing signs of
irritation at sons pleading in their fathers’ courts.

The wife of H. M. Addison, the Adelaide
State Bank chairman who was prominent in the
recent Nixon attempted murder case, died this
month.
Chas. Norman, of the Age reporting
staff, has been appointed night sub editor of
Sydney Daily Telegraph. He is an accom-
plished musician—was in Maoriland a profes-
sional organist—and possesses a head of fluffy
golden hair that only needs coaxing to rival
Paderewski’s mop.
Dr. O'Connell, who died suddenly at
Goolwa (S.A.) on Jan. 15, was a member of the
honorary surgical staff whick struck work at Ade-
laide Hospital, and he was also on the Board
which the Govt, declined to reappoint. He was
a prominent Catholic, and the late Archbishop
died under his auspices.
Stated that trouble is likely to arise re
S.A. Chief Justice Way’s position as Judge on
the Privy Council. Way’s last trip cost him
much money, and the S.A. Govt, claims that the
other provinces ought to contribute to future
salary and expenses. Way is due to return to
Britain in April next, but he wants to know his
financial position first.
Leopold Fane de Salis, who resigned from
N.S.W. Leg. Council the other day, owing to
financial misfortunes, belongs to a fine old adven-
turing and pioneering stock. The head of the
family is a German count, but sons and daughters
have ramified through half the titles of England.
William, a brother of Leopold’s, who died last
year, was chairman of the P. and O. Co. and of
the London Chartered Bank, and in his day was a
competent authority on South Sea Island
affairs. His collection of curios, picked up
in the South Seas and in China, was unique.
He and Leopold gave years of study to
early Australian history and the manners
and customs of the aborigines. Leopold in-
herited .£3OO a-year from his brother and an in-
terest in .£20,000 worth of English property. Wil-
liam died worth about £ 140,000, a great portion
of which he left to hospitals and almshouses in
England and Australia. Maitland and Tam worth
hospitals both benefit under his will. Leopold and
William, though enterprising and energetic, were
reticent men, and averse to company. Leopold
is noted for being able to listen with apparent in-
terest to people talking ignorantly on early Aus-
tralian topics, about which his own knowledge is
full and accurate.
At Auckland (M.L.), the other day, a fire
occurred, by which, among other matters, copies
of old treaties connected with Tahiti were con-
sumed. Which is a reminder that, at one time,
Britain and France were perilously near to war
over rival claims upon the Society group. At
that time, a French frigate sailed into the harbor
at Hobart, during the night, and anchored off the
old battery. Captain Moriarty, R.M., was then
port-officer. When the old chap arose in the
morning, and looked out of his bedroom-window,
he beheld the Frenchman flying the tricolor over
the British ensign at the peak of the spanker-yard.
He called for his uniform, cocked hat and sword,
and commanded that his barge should be ready.
Thus he boarded the French frigate, where he was
received by the officer of the watch, with many
bows and compliments. But he paid no heed.
He mounted to Che quarter-deck, drew his sword,
and with one sweep severed the halyards. Down
came the flags. Then the old chap marched back
to the gangway, took off his cocked hat, made a
prodigious bow, and departed, leaving the French-
man agape. When he got ashore he went to Govt.
House, reported to Governor Wilmott, and then—
and not till then—called for his morning dram.
The “ incident ” led to reams of correspondence,
and a leader in the Times upon the loyal temper
of Van Diemen’s Land.
Melb. : “ Demise—auctioneer Walstab,
aged 88, sire of George Arthur Walstab, the
brilliant and universally esteemed journalist and
author. Mr. Walstab was one of those elect with
the genuine brand of the English gentleman.
Nobody could imagine his deflection, in a hair’s
breadth, from the line of truth and honor. Was
in Melb. business since 1852. Demerara sugar-
planting had failed to microbe him, as such things
almost invariably do, and he was sound as a slim
wheat-stalk.”
The father of Judge Forbes,
now retiring from N.S.W. District
Court Bench, was in the days of his
Chief-Justiceship frequently taken to
task for his John Norton-like expres-
sions of Republican faith. On one
occasion a fire-eating Royalist named
Moodie assaulted the Judge, and for
months the country was almost in a
state of war. The Chief-Justice w'as
a native of Bermuda, and although
Bermuda was, and is, a British de-
pendency, the young lawyer appears
to have taken his politics from the
Pacific Slope. He was the first to
introduce trial by jury in Australia,
and to defend the institution when a
dead set was made to abolish it. The
father ascended the Bench in 1824,
the son descends in 1808.
About time that Peacock’s steam laugh
was remorselessly suppressed. He burst out,
apropos of nothing, immediately the Convention
was opened in Melbourne—just to get his laugh
paragraphed. And par’d it was accordingly, as
an event of moment. He does not do it because
he is amused at anything, but because he thinks it
is expected of him. It is not a genuine laugh. It
is simply a habit he has of making a large hole in
his face and belching forth a hideous sound. And
as the people are tired, Peacock’s laugh ought to
be Put Down.
George Throssell, W.A. Lands-Minister,
is a large-sized original. The other day Fre-
mantle council sought to honor one of its num
ber, a gentleman from the distressful country, by
naming a street “ Doonan-street.” Having no
new street to christen, the council decided to re-
name one of the two Queen-streets in which the
town rejoiced, but the Lands Department objected.
Representations were made to Throssell, with the
result that he approved of the change, and, when
noting his approval, wrote the following rhymed
minute :
“ What’s in a name ? ” The rose by any other name
would smell as sweet.
And “Doonan” is as good as “Queen” — when it only
means a street.
Which utterly paralysed the permanent heads*
and at once established the reputation of the
Minister as a poet.
Michael Flurscheim, the Swiss economist,
land reformer and socialist, pays Adelaide and
Melbourne a flying visit on his way to M.L. about
the end of this month. He is the author of
“ Rent, Interest and Wages,” and the “ True
History of Money Island. ’ ’ He is on board the
German boat Barbarnssa, and is accompanied by
his daughter. Flurscheim must be an old man
now, but is still fit for the fray, and impatient
with the slow headway his ideas on land and cur-
rency reform make in Europe ; to use his own
phrase, he wants to try whether it -won’t be easier
to accelerate a moving ball than to set in motion
a still one. With him are Ignatius Singer and
family. Singer is the co-scribe with Behrens of a
book (“Some Unrecognised Laws of Nature”)
which attracted much attention in scientific circles
last year by its robust reasoning and the boldness
of its attack on some assumptions as to matter and
energy. The latter happen both to be old
Adelaide men, and are foemen worthy of very
good steel.
Tom Finney, M.P., a big Brisbane (Q.)
draper, and very ordinary politician, is once more
taking a “ tour to Europe,” and is being adulated
all round. A stranger would take him to be a
little Christmas tin-god, with his in’ards stuffed
with chocolates. He has just paid 20s. in the <£
to the liquidation creditors of the old firm of
Finney, Isles & Co., who had given the firm
clean receipts for Bs. in the £; but the resultant
advts. have been worth it. Every local rag
(barring the Worker) has found it necessary to
spread on the butter with a shovel. Why ?
Because, firstly, ordinary honesty’s rare in Bris-
bane ; and because, secondly, Finney is the
hunkiest advertiser in Brisbane’s little business
world. Floods may overwhelm, fires may con-
sume, bankruptcies may bleed him, but the big
advertiser plunges around boss of the maelstrom,
like a porpoise circling round and always ahead
of the ship of public patronage. And Finney was
born with an advertising caul.
“Someday ” (writes “ Hiram ”) “you will
want to look up the antecedents of noble families.
Here is something. T. J. Lipton, the great tea-
merchant, has been.made a Knight Bachelor ! I
In 1870 Tom Lipton was driving a mule-car from
New Orleans to Jefferson City for lOdol. a week.
In company with Phil Mowbrey and Jack Ross, he
got arrested on a charge of arson in Poydras
Market. The three were suspected because they
had been having a jollification with a shopkeeper
(a Jew), one J. White, the night before his shop
was burnt. All were discharged, of course. In
’7l Tom opened a branch of his father’s butter-
and-egg shop (11 Stockwell-st.) in Stobcross-st.,
Anderston, Glasgow, where I often used to call on
him. After I came to Australia he printed imita-
tion .£l-notes, promising a pound’s worth of
groceries for a pound ! So many were passed
about Glasgow that the police blocked them.
Next he blossomed into tea-and-eoffee, and is
now the greatest dealer in the world, owning his
own plantations. He was tall, pfoo&y-faced, in-
clined to jewellery and green neckties.”

A story about Lieut. Aupen, one-time
torpedo - lieutenant on H.M.S. Orlando here.
Years ago, when he was a middy, his ship was
stationed at Vancouver, and Aupen being seized
with a sudden illness was invalided just prior to
the departure of his vessel on a cruise. The only
other officer then left in port was the chief car-
penter, also invalided. The youngster had deeply
studied the Queen's na\al regulations, and the
time for his examination coming round, he applied
—seizing the golden hour —to the only senior
officer in the port, the chief carpenter, to be
examined for lieutenant. This he or any officer
is entitled to do whenever or i.'berever he may be,
if the requisite term of his service be fulfilled.
The chief carpenter accordingly examined the
lad, and passed him with flying colors. Aupen
immediately thereby became the senior officer on
the station, and his first act of authority was to
order himself to England with despatches, an
order he obeyed with consummate address and
with all the haste possible, seeing his ship might
return any minute, and Xmas was approaching.
He arrived one evening at Portsmouth and found
he would have to wait eight hours for a train to
London. Again the regulations came to his aid.
Reg. No. So-and-so directs all officers carrying
despatches to take every available means to
expedite the arrival of same at headquarters.
Aupen decided that the Admiralty ought to
have his despatches forthwith. He ordered a
special train to London, and presented himself
before the First Lord. The great man, who had
heard nothing about either Aupen or his de-
spatches before, requested an explanation, which
the lad respectfully gave. He escaped without
even a reprimand ; he had complied to the letter
with the Queen’s regulations, and the whole affair
quickly became the laugh of London. The cream
of the joke was that Aupen’s despatches contained
nothing but a narrative of his own illness, and a
glowing account of the brilliant manner in which
he had passed his exam.—written by himself.
His next ship received him as lieutenant.

LASER B€€R.
Aboriginalities .
Grief in the camp of the Murrumbidgee
whalers. Their only friend and shade on Hay’s
famous One Tree Plain was lately blown down.
With that historical tree disappears one of
southern N.S. W.’s best-known land-marks. The
trunk of the well-known gum was literally covered
with carvings of names, initials and dates—it
boro dates as far back as 1850. The surprising
thing is that, so far as is known, no one ever hung
himself on that tree. What an advt. it would
have been : “Hung himself on the one tree on
the One Tree Plain ! ” One advantage of the
tree’s downfall is that buggies, drays and waggons
can now safely cross the big plain without fear of
Funning against its one tree. It seems hardly
credible ; but, in true accordance with the general
eussedness of things, many vehicles have, at one
time or other, collided with it.
» * * •
Jack the Bullocky, Charley the Ringer,
Marsupial Bill and others, in a northern shed, are
discussing the carrying capacity of the P. and O.
boats. After an argument as to whether the
Perthshire, which took from Sydney some 9000
odd tons of coal, was the largest vessel that has
loaded in Australian waters, the Bullocky
paralysed the company with the information that
when he was down in Rockhampton he saw the
Fitzroy (the vessel that trades up the Fitzroy
River), and that she had a house on deck so big
that he could walk in at the doorway without
“ bending ” his head ; that the mast was thicker
than his waggon-pole and three times as long, and
that they loaded her down chock-full of bananas
and put three horses on board of her, and then she
didrvt sink l
ANOTHER COINCIDENCE.
Beneath the window of the W.C.T.U. building.,
Little Flinders-street, Melbourne.
Kangaroo pathology, according to the
Medical Press and Circular :
The deprivation of active exercise, which is entailed by
board and lodging in the Zoological Gardens [London],
afflicts the kangaroos in a marked manner. They develop
enormous corns, several ounces in weight, which are
very troublesome to remove, and, on the approach of
damp weather—and damp weather seeing to he the rule in
the purlieus of Regent’s Park—they are prone to gouty
manifestations, usually affecting one hand, or paw, and
then the opposite foot.

W.D., from Mulgerrie (W.A.) :—On New
Year’s Eve the police here had to go round to all
the groggeries begging the publicans not to supply
any more fire-water to the diggers, because the
lock-up was stuffed to its utmost capacity with
“ drunks,” and, as the logs for chaining purposes
had been sneaked by the mill-owners, they, the
police, had to cry a go.

“ Scotty the Wrinkler ” :
I should like to know your M.L. correspondent (on
the Derrink). He is a man who views cause and effect as
I do, and, although I have not yet tried Kola Nut, I can
substantiate the facts about the other drinks. “Cobbler’s
punch ” is the best of all mouth-vvetters when you are
spitting cotton-wool through cracked lips. Any acid
(with water and sugar) composes the beverage—vinegar
for choice. All the Urana drunks used to come to me for
a recovery on rest, cobbler’s punch and reading. The
puhlicans would send them off with a nip in a soda-water
bottle, just to carry them my distance. When vinegar is
scarce, jam will do, also cream-of-tartar, tartaric acid, or
weak vitriol. The acid is necessary to give it a “ bite,”
but, as your Maoriland brother boozikin says, it is the
sugar that does the trick, being a food and therefore
satisfying. Having soothed the innards with this punch
exhibit beef-tea, soup, milk (with lime or soda to prevent
curdling) then meat, and when you can stand that you’re
ready to “ go on the tank ” again.

Dear Bulletin, — The man in the far bush,
besides being a tailor in a small way, and many
other things, is an adept at mending old boots.
There is no call for a tradesman to set up business
in his neighborhood, and his absence has caused
bushy to think and act for himself until he has
become expert. Finding his leathers going,
he first appropriates all the sprigs or tacks that
come in his way, and steals a piece of good sole
leather; then he takes the loan of somebody’s
hammer and goes to work. If chance permits,
when the job’s done he “sells out” for a bob or
two and goes serenely on his way. He’s not far
out when he says repairing costs nothing. I knew
one man though, whose repairing cost him three
months. But he was only an apprentice.—
Werrimee.

Writes “ A.L.” :
I was listening to the reminiscences of an ancient ex-
policeman at a town on one of the Northern rivers, and
the name of a well-known medico in the early Sixties was
brought up. “ Know him ? ” said the venerable cop ; “ I
do, the divil ! He was afther getting me iift.o trouble
once. There was a reward of LlOO out for a blackfellow
who had murdered a boundary-rider, and we were all
eager to get him —and the money. Well, I came across
him and arrested him, but when taking him in he died
about 10 miles from the police-station. There was an
enquiry, of course, and the doctor said the black was
over-driven, and died from fear and fatigue. Fear and
fatigue,” and the old man’s eyes blinked viciously, while
a grin crept over his face. “ The devil a bit of fear and
fatigue it was. I had a collar on my horse’s neck and
another on the darkey’s, with a chain betwixt them, and
we were jogging along easy, not distressing him in the
least, when the black devil buried his teeth in the calf of
my leg. With that I dug the spurs in my horse, and the
plunge he gave landed that darkey clean off his feet into
the middle of the road, and another plunge sent him fly-
. ing six feet in the air head downwards. Then I did a
half-mile gallop with the nigger sometimes on his feet
and mostly taking jumps in the air and the rest of the
time trailing. I guess he must have died through getting
his neck broken in five or six places. And the old saw-
bones said it was fear and fatigue. Perhaps it was after
all; the sudden jump might have made him feel tired.
Anyhow, I never got the £100.”

Thus a Westralian to a Melbourne man :
“ I travelled from Mullewa to Cue on the same
coach with a police-escort. One said he liked the
nor’-west better than the Murchison, and explained
why. ‘ You see we are allowed 2s. per day for
the keep of nigger prisoners up there, and we
. take good care to have as many as we can always
in the logs, and we take ’em out and make them
catch their tucker, and we pocket the cash. My
word I did well up there.’” The great and
glorious West!

Dear Bulletin,— Re “R.H.’s” doubt as
to whether bung-eye is produced by a small black
fly or the ordinary house-fly, after feeding on
putrid matter. The latter alternative is, in my
opinion, out of the question, except where actual
blood-poisoning sets in, which often means death
—not merely a closed eye for three days. I have
been bitten on the eyelid by what we call up
North the “sandfly,” and have had a bunged eye
with the consequences described. No perception
of the actual bite, but gradually increasing
itch, and then swelling. The fly is a very
small grey one—sometimes nearly black. I found
I could cure this itch, and prevent the swell-
ing by bathing the eye in water as hot as I could
bear it; keep the water hot, and keep on bathing
until you find the swelling reduced. When I com-
menced beekeeping, I found that a sting on the
forehead, between the eyes, or near either eye,
meant one or both eyes bunged up. But, several
times, I averted this result by the above method
of hot fomentation. I am used to bee-stings now;
they cause no swelling, even when on the eyelid,
and do not seem so painful as of old, except, curi-
ously, when delivered by an odd Italian who
seems to be extra powerful at the business end.
But to the point. If “ R.H.” will try the hot-
water fomentation, I almost believe he will find
it a preventative of bunged eye, and, though I
don’t expect the fortune he mentions, I would be
glad to learn the result.—J., Tweed River.
* » * *
Ed. Bulletin,—We decided to build a
bark humpy ; it was a bit cold living under calico.
The bark did not strip well, and, when taken off
the tree, it was hard to open without placing it
over a fire to heat the sap. We stripped a few
sheets, and started to carry them down the moun-
tain to our camp. To carry a sheet of bark pro-
perly you stand it on end, place your back to the
sap-side, half sit down, keeping the back straight,
put the hands low down, catch a grip each side,
and give a couple of hitches as you straighten up’
shooting the bark well out over the head. Dave,
the green-hand, managed all right, but, in the
hitching process, he shoved his hat partly over his
eyes, and when he let go with one hand to shove
his hat back, like a spring-trap the bark closed
round him, and there he was, cased in a cylinder
of stringybark, and rolling down the mountain
side—bounding over rocks, bumping against trees,
and making straight for where the descent ended
in a perpendicular fall of 200 ft. into the Blue
Mountain Creek. We followed as fast as we
could, until, luckily, a peppermint-tree brought
the cylinder up, and we soon had the occupant
out, not much the worse for his shaking. “ Dave,
old man,” said Sam, afterwards, “I reckon some
of them Bulletin tight places ain’t a patch on the
fix you were in to-day, and, if you had gone over
that bluff you would have been the first man on
record to be killed in his coffin ! ” —R.F.

Dear B.—l remember it so hot on the
Damlie River in N.Q. once that the fish were
coming out of the water dripping with perspira-
tion to lie on the rocks and fan themselves with
their tails. But the heat was so overpowering
that they couldn’t keep it up, and were all duly
turned into broiled fish.—S.E.O’B.

Dear Bulletin,— Did you ever see a rain-
bow at night ? Was watching cattle one showery
night (full moon) when I saw a perfect rainbow,
sa?is colors. It was quite white. I have told
several people, but they do not seem to believe me.
It was on the same trip, dodging behind the cattle
one day in some thick mulga, I sighted a scrub
turkey, as white as the “ driven snow.” (Have
never seen snow, but am told it’s white). -I had
seen white kangaroos, and heard tell of white
crows, white “ grey ” parrots, &c., but am inclined
to think that white bush turkeys are extremely
rare.— The Wanderer.

• * * *
You see some strange sights out West
during a drought. An iron tank was sunk a few
months ago, on a certain station, to a level of
about 15in. below the surface, and used ■ r water-
ing horses. During the water-famine clouds of
parrots used to fly to this tank in the evening,
crowd into the water, and fight furiously until
numbers of them were drowned. Rabbits met
their death in the same manner, and things got so
bad that the tank had to be cleaned out daily to
purify the water from its holocaust. Writer has
known teal flying over a station in search of water
to drop suddenly on a gal vanised-iron roof, the
sheen of which in the moonlight had, by its sem-
blance of liquid, deceived the birds and caused
them to make a descent.

Dear Bulletin, —“ W.O.R.’s ” uilcle’s
ram’s butting exploit is good for a sheep, but I
knew an old Tas. prospector named Yates who
was a better butter for a man than “ Billy ” was
for a ram At the age of f»8 I saw him knock out,
in quick time, a young six-foot Victorian native—
and the young ’un was sober and the old ’un
drunk. Mr. Yates could hit hard enough for
ordinary people with his hands, but harder with
his head. I saw him, for the wager of a bottle of
rum, run at a solid four-railed fence and break the
top rail in two with his head. Yates is dead
now, but it wasn’t the fence-rail that wiped him
out —it was the rum.—C.R.

Dear Bulletin, —A study of your columns
tends to convince one that this is a bank-burdened
country, and that the nation is pretty well run by
the dummies of “our financial institutions.” Per-
sonally, I do not know the squatter, and have little
knowledge of how he squats, but I do know the
bush-township, particularly in Victoria, and find
that it is almost invariably owned by the bank—
from the new, red hotel, managed by an equally
red, drowsy man, in last month’s shirt, and
ancient, down-trodden carpet slippers, to the
gable-ended humpy, inhabited by Smith’s spotted
cattle-“ da wg.” The fact that the bank owns the
township is largely due to the boom and the sub-
sequent burst. During the boom, Ragan the pub-
lican, and Ryan the smith, and Hogan the grocer,
and Mooney the butcher, grew suddenly too vast
for their low, cheap, weatherboard premises, and
instantly borrowed and built. Then there was an
explosion, and presently Ragan, and Ryan, and
Hogan, and Mooney found themselves and theirs
in the hands of a decrepit and despairing bank,
and were glad to escape with a shirt to their backs.
And, where formerly a few ricketty wooden sheds
faced the station, and were called Budgerygubbery,
or something of the kind, a line of blatant brick
establishments now juts out of the wilderness and
frowns down the wretched little dog-box of a
station ; and they arc called Seaton, or Beacons-
field, or Frogmore. And there is only one man
worth mentioning in the place, the manager of the
“local branch,” an elegant young person, who
habitually wears a cigarette and a cummerbund,
and is lord of all he surveys.—S.

Dear Bulletin,— H. J. M‘Cooey asserts
that the laughing-jackass “never killed, nor is
ever likely to kill a full-grown snake.” Per
contra, —During one summer a year or two ago I
saw jackasses on four different occasions flying
with snakes in their bills, the reptile in one case
being 4ft. long—about as much as the bird could
wobble along with. Mr. M’Cooey had in future
better stick to what he has seen for himself.—
Wombat.
The Giant of Medicines.
Warner’s Safe Cure is not a “cure all.” It is a
specific for the diseases resulting from a weakness
of two or. ans—two only— the liver (known by
jaundice, flatulency, nausea, bilious headaches,
spasmodic pains in the stomach, and the “ tired ”
“ played-out ” feeling) and the kidneys.
The Kidneys, unlike other organs in the body,
when diseased, may themselves be free from" pain,
and this fact leads many to believe that they are
not diseased. But medical authorities agree that
they can be far gone with disease, and yet give forth
no pain, because they have but few nerves of
sensation.
We do not open a watch to see if it is going or
in good order; we merely look at the hands, or
note the accuracy of its time. So we need not
open the kidneys to see if they are diseased. We
must study the condition of the system. Kidney
disease produces any of the following symptoms :
Backache, flatulency and pain in the heart, irregu-
larity or excess in urinating, sour stomach, heart-
burn with dyspepsia, pains in the small of the
back, headache, neuralgia, loss of memory,
rheumatism, chills and fever, red or white brick-
dust deposits, shortness of breath, and a pallor
or puffiness of the skin.
Theseare thechief disorders, or symptoms, caused
by a diseased condition of the kidneys. If the
kidneys are restored to health by the great specific
—Warner’s Safe Cure —these ailments mentioned
above will disappear. THERE IS NC MYS-
TERY ABOUT If, Warner’s Safe Cure does
cure. When the kidneys are diseased, the albu-
men, the life-property of the blood, escapes in the
water, while the urea, the “ kidney-poison,” re-
mains, and it is this “ kidney-poison'' in the blood
that, circulating through the entire body, affects
every organ, and produces all the above symptoms.

In the early thirties it was a moot point as
to whether Brisbane or Cleveland Point was to be
the head-quarters of the penal settlement at
Moreton Bay. A few hundred yards of mud at
Cleveland sent' Governor Brisbane up the river.
Now the water comes down and swamps most
of the Q. metropolis every year or so, and silts up
the tortuous waterway a few feet. But Brisbane
doesn’t pay for the drainage or the dredging—the
whole province is saddled with the cost of the
awful blunder. Change hopeless, owing to vested
interests—and flood-prevention doubtful even if
inillions are spent.

“ SURPLUS."
The term “ surplus ” is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate;
due provision being made in the valuation for the
rights of future participants. The natural ten-
dency of distribution is to diminish the surplus
for the moment; but in the case of a prosperous
company, the decrease is speedily more than
covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. It requires
but a moment’s consideration, however, to per-
ceive that it is easy to keep in hand more than
the future will require under any possible circum-
stances ; and, when that course is followed, in-
justice to present participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they are entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was J 26,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance—of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
24 millions sterling 1 This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
=£44,445,244, which is more, than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid intere ts of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
this course, the Mutual Life of" New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,” “ the Co. which does the most
good 1 ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in exces; of
any possible needs of the generations to come.*

Mr. T C. Allen, Consulting Mechanical
Engineer, has joined Mr. Arthur Griffith Patent

At a recent English fashionable marriage
the bridegroom presented the bride with the bullet
which had cost him his arm in South Africa. It
was mounted in gold, fashioned into a double-
headed serpent.
The hydrophobia scare in England is very
bad just now, and the dog-muzzling question rose
to political importance at Deptford election,
where the Unionist candidate was defeated on
account of having originated the regulation under
which sporting dogs are allowed to go unmuzzled,
- ~ . , “
Mr. J. Button, organist, and Rev. I homas
Davies, preacher, officiated in the same church in
Harrington (Eng.) in 1877. Without any know-
ledge of each other’s movements they met again,
a Sunday or two ago, in the same relative posi-
tions in a church at Parramatta, N.S.W.
Seven years ago the dead-and-gone Parkes
laid the foundation - stone of a trades hall at
Broken Hill. Not another stroke was done—
except by somebody who presently capsized the
stone and collared the coins. Now Mr. B. Back-
house, M.L.C., has presented the local unions
with complete plans of a building, and a start is to
be made with it at once.


THOSE ART ENVELOPES.
Joe Cook , the N.S. W. Postmaster-General , proposes to issue a series of “ Art Envelopes” to advertise the province. Our
artist herewith submits a few designs.
No. 1.—An official envelope for use abroad, intended to convey some idea of N.S.W's prosperity under
Freetrade. No. 2.—Is intended as a sort of pleasantry on the part of the P.M.-General at the expense of the Attorney-
General, and may have some bearing upon Alien Exclusion. The pun is just what one might expect from Joe Cook.
No. 3.—For use in legal correspondence. No. 4 and 5.—Self-explanatory. No. 6.—A very re-assuring envelope
indeed. No. 7.—Introduced for picturesque, rather than for moral effect. No. 8.—How to bring George Reid within
the domain of “practical” politics. No. 9.—An encouragement of youthful Australians. No. 10,—Social.

CEYLON TEA.
g
CAPTAIN FERGUSON, P.S. to HIS EXCELLENCY THE
GOVERNOR, writes as follows :
“ I beg to inform you that the sample packet of Nirvana Tea you were good enough to send
me was prepared for His Excellency, and the Tea seemed to be of excellent quality.”
Mr. C. R. BLACKE IT, Govt. Analyst of Victoria, Bays, September 21st, ’97:—
“I have analysed the Nirvana Tea, and am able to affirm that it is of Eirst-class Quality.
Rich in extract, it is of pleasant aroma and flavour, and is absolutely pure.”
Dr. J. E. ELLIOTT (Elliott Bros., Ltd., Sydney), says :
« This is to certify that I have examined several Samples of Nirvana Tea taken from stock.
lam satisfied that it is an unusually fine, pure Ceylon Tea, consisting of young leaves. It has
a very pleasant aroma, is rich in extractive, and contains little tanin or astringent matter.
Owing to its being packed in lead at Colombo, its freshness is preserved, and it is a pleasure to
recommend such a high-class tea.”
fd
CEYLON TEA is put up in Lead in Colombo
in order that it may reach the public in the ori-
ginal packets, pure, fresh, untampered with.

an antidote for stubborn prickly heat and itchings and chafings of all kinds. Of a peculiar,
healthy aroma, which lasts right to the end, it is an elegant-looking article, and is, morever, the
greatest cleansing soap in the market.
Mr. W. A. DIXON, Registered Analytical and Consulting Chemist, Laboratory, 97 Pitt-
street, Sydney, says :—“I have analysed several samples of Minerva Soap, and have to report
that it is a superfatted, milled Soap, impregnated with soothing and antiseptic medicaments. It
is evidently made of the Purest Materials, and is Absolutely Neutral—that is, it does not contain
any free alkali such as is found in cheap soaps, and which, whilst it promotes the formation of a
lather, renders the skin hard and dry. For this reason Minerva Soap may be ÜBed with safetv
on the most delicate skin. It contains a very low percentage of water, and, therefore, a high
percentage of actual soap—higher than any other I have examined.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Heard on the stump, in Hobart: “ Since
‘Tattersall ’ Adams assumed the reins of govern-
ment in this island, an appropriate name for the
province would be Tattersmania.”
The only M.L.A. on Tomjoe Byrnes’ re-
ception committee, in Brisbane, the other day;
was Labor-member Dawson, who, rumor holds;
is likely to perform a sudden acrobatic feat.
Ricardo : “ The most important of the
rings within rings of the China question is a pro-
spective fight between Russia and Britain for
the tea-trade, in which the Siberian railway is ex-
pected to beat the British ships.”
The Germans of S.A., with one consent,
decline to have anything to do with the petition
framed by Sydney German-Hebrew South Sea
traders, asking the Reichstag to strengthen the
German fleet in Australasian waters.
Not all the great many licenses issued to
Syrian hawkers in Sydney, to which The Bulletin
referred the other day, remain there. A good
many, it is said, are transferred to country dis-
tricts, where a little more difficulty is sometimes
experienced in getting them in the regular way.
Three members of N.S.W. Parliament
have, since prorogation, executed what are practi-
cally assignments of their estates. Trustees col-
lect their incomes, Parliamentary salaries even
going into the pools. The difference between such
assignments and insolvency is merely a legal one.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott, who admits to
being “not a violent Protectionist ” just now, is
supposed to be “ not over-anxious ” for the down-
fall of Reid at the coming elections. “ I don’t
agree with everything the Protectionists say
against Reid,” he observed lately—which is a pro-
per enough frame of mind to be in with a vacant
Agent-Generalship looming ahead.
T.D. : “ Hinted that, through giving
legislators too much rope, Chairman of Committees
M'Court has effectually debarred himself from
N.S.W. Speakership. When the House goes into
committee it is the immediate signal for an out-
burst of conversation, and visitors to the galleries
are astonished to hear luridities of language for
which an ordinary nerson outside would spend a
month in gaol.”
An Englishman
died, and by some mis-
chance got past the
keeper of the Great
Gate. He strolled up
the main avenue, much
astonished and a trifle
annoyed. It wasn’t
named High-street, nor
were there any hotels to
be seen, nor yet any
churches; and the
cherubs seemed far too
familiar—to a stranger.
At last he reached the
Great White Throne,
and was about to grovel
before the Presence,
when—glancing at one
of the pedestals —some-
thing familiar caught his
eye. Going closer, he
observed carefully, and
when at last the awful
truth struck him he fell
dazed—and was lost.
For the pedestal carried
a label bearing the words
—“ Made in Germany.’

F. B. Suttor, who now definitely announces
his candidature for Bathurst in the Protectionist
interest, was Minister of Justice in the Parkes
Government of 21 years ago. Of the eight
Ministers who then composed the Government,
five are dead, and the rest (except Suttor) politi-
cally extinct. Suttor figured again as Minister of
Justice in the Parkes-Robertson batch of ’7B.
Estimated that the population
of N.S.W. increased in ’97 by 25,820
This is 100 more than the increase in
’93 and 6000 more than the inorease in
’96, but otherwise it is the smallest
record for very many years past. The
province loses 1434 inhabitants by ex-
cess of departures overland to Vic-
toria, S.A. and Queensland, but it
gains a lot by arrivals oversea, and
the net result is an increase of 2852
by immigration. The remainder of the
increase (22,968) is through excess of
births over deaths. Barring ’96 and
’B9, last year was the worst for births
within the memory of this generation.
People really haven’t time to be born in Aus-
tralia now.
According to an apparently inspired state-
ment in the Sydney Daily Telegraph, Premier
Reid is going back on his definite promise, which
appears in Hansard, that a referendum on the
fiscal question shall be taken at next N.S.W.
general election. It is now proposed that the
fiscal vote shall be taken at some indefinite date
in the dim hereafter. Did G. Reid ever make a
promise that he didn’t go back on, more or less ?
Suppose Premier Reid makes one-fifth of
N.S.W. Leg. Council retire every five years, he,
as it were, pours (at the best) one-fifth of whisky
into four-fifths of water —and then allows the
mixture to stand and evaporate for 60 months;
then he renews the operation. Let the whisky
stand for reform and the water for Upper House
Toryism. How many thousand years would it
take to bring about a reasonably-strong mixture ?
N. “ ‘You and I are wise fools, able to
take care of No. 2, but not of No. 1.’ —Charles
Reade to Mrs. Seymour. Isn’t this just the case
of such as Parkes, Graham Berry, and Dibbs?
Nay, one applies it to Pitt. Familiar cant is
about a man who cannot manage his own affairs
not being able to manage a nation, but these men
are ruined by their absorption in the public good.
The hunks would be nowhere against a Graham
Berry, if such a man could bend his mind to his
own concerns. This for thechuckly concentrative
crawlers to Gorgius Midasity and Thriftability.”
Melb. : “ Superfluous to remark that the
Federal Convention excites infinitely less public
notice than a boys’ pi ay-game of cricket. The
great National Concern, meaning Federalism, is
too much in the hands of the lawyers, a class in
whom it is impossible to rouse general confidence.
When the French Constituent Assembly was
formed, in 1789, some philosopher remarked, ‘ A
mass of lawyers. No good can come of it.’
Finance is a great question, but what is needed in
the beginning is the Blast Furnace —the orator
who can really make the Australian kangaroo sit
up and drive on the nation to a conviction that
Federation is an indispensable step towards Inde-
pendence. He must be racy of the soil, Australia’s
Patrick Henry or O’Connell. Here’s the Hour —
Where’s the Man ? ”

Under Westralia’s new cheap foreign nig-
ger-labor law, one colored heathen only can be
imported for every 500 tons of a ship’s capacity ;
when his contract expires he must either enter
into a new one, or leave; and the foreign cheap
blackfellow is not'to be employed in any mine, or
to go south of latitude 27. This latter provision
ought to be a great deal of use. The Bulletin
has a vision just here of 60,000 policemen guarding
the blazing wastes of latitude 27, to see that the
cheap foreign nigger doesn’t come across.

Suppose the problem of reforming the
N.S.W. Leg. Council is submitted to a popular
vote, as now proposed, how will the question be
put ? Presumably it will simply read : “ Are you
in favor of reforming the Leg. Council ? ” Nearly
everybody will answer “ Yes! ” And then the
Government will presumably allege that its
lunatic scheme of appointing Councillors for 25
years is a reform, and that the people have en-
dorsed it by a huge majority.
Pity the Adelaide W.C.T.U. ! Three can-
didates ran for the seat of “ poor M'Pherson,” as
somehow or other everybody calls the deceased
Labor-leader. One is a good fellow, who “ shouts ’ ’
for everybody ; another is interested in a paper
which has a “bung” column, and has sadly sati-
rised the good dames ; and the third looks very
kindly upon the man that lays the odds. It isn’t
all beer and skittles — i.e., tonic ale and parlor-
quoits—to spot the “good men” for whom all
‘ ‘ true women ” should vote.
N.S.W. Labor-party corrobborees at New-
castle on 26th instant, and will thereafter issue an
electoral manifesto. The party favors the aboli-
tion of the Upper House, but evidently regards
that scheme as impracticable for the present, and
so will discuss what attitude should be assumed
in regard to reforming the Council as it exists
to-day. It is pretty safe to assume that
M'Gowan and co. assent to Premier
Reid’s scheme of watering (or whiskying) the
Council to the extent of one-fifth every five years.
If they do, they may, as a Labor-party, just
as well jump off South Head.
Next year will produce the best crop of
industries yet manufactured in New South
Wales. The police in each district have got in-
structions to ascertain the number of farmers who
manufacture butter, and the number of hands em-
ployed in such work. It will pan out this way :
The old man milks the cows. The wife makes,
say, 10lb. butter a day, ai d two boys drive the
cows from the paddock with much blanky and an
old cattle-dog. Premier Reid calls this an in-
dustry becauses it has four hands employed and
produces 36501 b. of butter a year. N.S. Wales
will next year be one vast industry, and almost
every one in it will be employed in a factory.
Yic. : “Railway Commissioner Mathieson
has thrust his hand into the gauntlet. Even
Premier Turner finds, and says, that Mathieson
may be broken, but not bent. Taking it myself
for a good thing, I must say Responsible Govern-
ment is wholly done away with in our railway
department, and a beneficial David Syme substi-
tuted, the Minister of Railways being the veriest
puppet in the country. No man with any self-
respect would hold the post. Mathieson is nearly as
popular as Speight with the employees, and if he
would but draw on the velvet glove over the wrist
of steel would do yet better. He is over-brusque,
Scotch-bristly, almost hedge-hoggy. But he is at
home.”
G. Republican Dibbs, it appears, refuses
to listen to the voice of the charmer. He will not
re-enter political life, but will lay to his book,
and, in the interests of his family, rest uneasily
in the more or less uncomfortable security of his
official -01300 a year. Dibbs is buoyant beyond
his years, but what sane politician can, at 65, look
back without a shudder on the dying days of the
ever-neoessitous Parkes, or the last years of poor,
hard-up, old Jack Robertson, or on the bone
contemptuously flung by Parliament to the septua-
genarian Berry—flung with the injunction that
his penniless wife is on no account to be allowed
to gnaw it after he is dead ? The rewards of
politics !
Note the tendency in municipalities to
refer disputed matters of local policy to a plebiscite
of the ratepayers. But in N.S.W., at any rate,
there is no provision for it in the unwieldy and
indefinite Municipalities Act; at any rate, no
special provision. Thereupon the further dispute
invariably arises whether the vote should be taken
on the cumulative plan, as the Act provides with
regard to the election of aldermen, or whether it
should be on the one-ratepayer-one-vote system.
Until that is settled (and there seems to be no way
of settling it except by still another amending Act)
the local Referendum will be valueless, and,
besides, the Referendum itself will be brought into
disrepute. Yet what a valuable bit of education
it might be !
N. : “ It is impossible to rouse the alarm
of ‘ wolf ’ in the public mind of Australia anyhow
over the China divide. People are convinced that
such things are settled up beforehand by huge
vultures like Britain, Russia, Germany and
France, and then a sham is made of sparring and
turkey-cockery, just to delude the commoners of
each nation. The Charles Marvin business showed
up this kind of useful dodge. But for the disclo-
sures of this tenpenoe-an-hour copying-clerk, what
a delusion the globe would yet be under re the
brilliant triumph achieved by Dizzy and Salisbury
at Berlin ! All rehearsed like a common panto-
mine. The China play has been under rehearsal
since the war, and they are just doing the first act
according to the book.”

From Broad Arrow (W.A.) Standard of
Dec. 18, 1897
Mr. George Webb, the enterprising landlord of the
Exchange Hotel, has opened a new saloon bar, which
should speedily become a resort for those in search of &
quiet and cool spot. The bar is handsomely decorated,
that part being carried out by the genial Jim Bogie. The
walls are hung with a paper of charming design and the
floor covered with linoleum on a bed of sawdust 3in.
thick.
In the bush journal every man who has anything
to do with liquor is “ genial ” —he is either the
genial host, or the genial boots, or the genial
chucker-out, or the genial drunk lying under the
form with his head resting genially on the house
dog. So when a man gets a job to paint the inside
of the new beer-haunt he naturally becomes “ the
genial Jim Bogie.”
The boy-burgiar is becoming an institu-
tion in Melbourne. He is very small and very
ingenious, and he preys upon the grocer as a rule.
The head of the last band arrested was a reckless
desperado aged eight, and he and his subordinate
bold buccaneers had taken possession of an empty
house next to a big grocery, furnished it with
paling fixtures, and stocked it with edibles lifted
from the neighboring emporium. During holidays
and evenings the gang would push in the bottom
half of the back-door of the provision depot until
the small but desperate leader could crawl through;
then the banditti looted biscuit-barrel and lolly-
tin, and humped away chocolate, and cocoa, and
tinned fish, and fruits, and many cakes, and
stowed them in their den. The captain locked
up before leaving, and was let out as he entered,
and while the police were scooting far and wide,
and galloping up and down, hunting clues, the
burglars were making themselves beastly ill with
raspberry vinegar and sardines next door. A
traitor “blew the gaff,” and the burglars are to
be sent to a State establishment to be reformed by
steam.

After three years’ wanderings in the mazes
of the law, and after enjoying the ponderous con-
sideration of twenty-one Judges, the great indus-
trial case of Allen v. Flood has been decided in
favor of the appellant, Allen, by the House of
Lords. Two shipwrights, Flood and Taylor, were
employed by a shipbuilding company to do iron-
work on an iron ship—a work which the boiler-
makers consider their own. The Boilermakers'
Union, through Allen, protested, and threatened
to withdraw their men, and the company dis-
charged the shipwrights. Flood brought an action
against Allen and union officials for maliciously
and wrongfully procuring his dismissal. A Judge
and common jury decided in favor of Flood,
the Court of Appeal confirmed verdict, and the
case went to the Lords. The Tory journals are
rather hysterical over the judgment, but it is as
much in favor of their friends, the employers,
as it is of the unions, because no employer or union
of employers (they always call it “ federation ”)
could induce any other employer to sack a man—a
thing very popular amongst them if the man is a
prominent union official —if the decision went the
other way. And the ruling seems fair enough.
An employer has a perfect right to discharge a
man with whom he has no contract, and the
decision goes that a third person has a perfect
legal right to use lawful means to induce him. to
commit that lawful act. Lord Watson, in giving
judgment, laid down that malice, only comes in
when the act to which it leads is an actionable
wrong.
