Evenin’ ’Erald (Melb.) takes a quiet shot
at Minister Carruthers:—“The hon. gentleman
appeared to be recognised in the galleries, in
which there were about 20 ladies.” Why, of
course. Carruthers is an interesting personage.
Fitzroy (Vic.) councillors fell out and
fought in the Council Chamber one day last week.
Unfortunately, nobody was injured. The Bulle-
tin is in favor of compelling councillors to learn
pugilism in order that they may hurt each other
sufficiently when they clash. In the multitude of
councillors there is wisdom, but no science.
Election for Thursday Island Hospital-
committee took place the other day, and out of a
total of eight members elected three were colored
men—one a Manilla man, one a Jap., and the
third a Hindoo. Significant! Also, at an alien
wedding m that same cheerful little island a Malay
man came along with a gun and shot three of the
guests. You see, he hadn’t been invited. Signi-
ficant also.
The election to fill the vacancy for East
Adelaide in S.A. Assembly vacated by the death
of M‘Pherson, resulted in the return of Hutchin-
son, nominee of the united Labor-party. Voting:
Hutchinson (Labor), 1002 ; Shaw (Liberal, i.e.,
Tory), 890 ; Salmon (independent Labor), 60. But
Salmon was neither fish nor flesh—he was only a
red herring, but the dodge didn’t work as well as
was expected.
The widows and orphans of the men killed
in the South Yarra sewer, the other day, will, in
all probability, be the greatest sufferers by the
Gippsland bush-fires, since the later sensation has
completely “ knocked ” the earlier one kite-high,
and an emotional public can only squander sym-
pathy on one horror at a time. The Gippsland
calamity has tapped the flow of contributions, and
the public (as always) overlooks the sewer disaster
and forks out for the big show only.
A derivation: “Sholto.’' An insulting
epithet, indigenous to southern Maoriland. Years
ago, the Government of the day settled a number
of Shetland crofters at “The Neck,” Stewart’s
Island, furnishing them with boats, nets, and
quarters in a large building known as “ The Bar-
racks.” “ The Barracks ” was still standing about
12 years ago, and was then used as a dancing-hall
by the brown maidens of the vicinity and their
admirers from the Bluff oystering-fleet. It was
then being steadily converted into firewood by the
oystermen, so it has probably disappeared
by now. The crofters were expected by their
sanguine importers to catch fish, but they
soon found out that things were livelier on the
mainland. So they removed there, over-running
the country districts of Southland. Fishing, even
when combined with the unlawful manufacture of
whisky, did not provide in those primitive days
enough oil for the rag upon the smell of which the
Shetlanders were popularly supposed to exist, so
they invaded every local branch of industry.
Owing to the low wages for which they worked
they became extremely unpopular, and as two out
of every three of them answered to the name of
“ Sholto,” anyone who was mouldy for want of a
fight had only to enter the first country pub. he
came to and remark to the first man he ran against,
“ Hullo, Sholto, when did you come over ? ” to be
instantly obliged. “Sholto” was an insult that
shrieked for blood.
The recent Chinese New Year’s Day passed
off in Sydney without the customary tremendous
firework racket, because “the Emperor has de-
cided, on account of the eclipse of the sun, to
postpone all festivities.” Very natural and proper
on the part of the “ Brother of the Sun.”
Bananaland editors poke endless fun at
Clement Wragge because he always writes in the
first person plural and dubs himself “We.”
Surely Clement has as much right to do so as
“ the dirty man in the back office” who runs the
Banner of Bugaboo, or the Clarion of Dead
Dog. Besides, Wragge is a very plural sort of
individual—prophet, poet, and meteorologist all
in one. Also, tee adoption of the “We ” business
may possibly signify that Clement is inclined to
admit that Providence has some little say in the
weather question—occupying, probably, the r6le
of junior partner. Probably “We” is a meteoro-
logical abbreviation of “me and the Almighty.”
Melb. Argus, commenting on the cable
news of a suicide epidemic among New York
society damsels, refers quite seriously to the
Suicide Club (for gentlemen only) which was said
to have been discovered in the ’Murkan city some
months ago. No such club ever existed. All the
newspaper stories of that cheerful institution and
the orgies perpetrated therein, were merely works
of fiction based upon some facts which haven’t, so
far as The Bulletin knows, been published here-
abouts. These are the facts : An R.C. mission is
carried on among the prostitutes of New York,
and if a sister happens to die during one of her
intermittent bursts of penitence, she gets
an impressive R.C. funeral. After witness-
ing one of these theatrical interments, a
number of low-spirited “gay’’-girls, feeling that
the only thing worth living for was a solemn, pic-
turesque removal to the cemetery, formed them-
selves into a Suicide Syndicate, and drew lots for
the privilege of taking a dose of poison on the Ist
of next month. Tickets were drawn from a hand-
bag, and the one fatal ticket decorated with a
crude representation of a skull fell to the girl
who had proposed the scheme. Whereupon this
bold spirit warned her lady friends that if
any of them failed to carry out the con-
tract when their turns came round, she (the
pioneeress of the movement) would arise from her
grave and haunt ’em —haunt ’em “ something
horrible.” A couple of weeks later she took a
dose of reclamation from the R.C. missionaries,
previous to taking a dose of poison on her own,
and her “ funeral came up to the best expectations
of the syndicators.” Then came another drawing
and another suicide, but this time the girl
didn’t get any funeral to speak of. She was
buried as a pauper, and the enthusiasm of the
surviving girls slumped considerably. The next
one to draw the winning ticket was not eager for
death. In her distress of mind she took to drink-
ing more than usual, until at last she drank
herself into a fervent belief that girl No. 1
was chasing her around the Bowery with
a view to burying her alive at midnight.
The shrieks and gruesome remarks of the unsober
sister led to an enquiry, and the nuns came
down in force and broke up the Suicide Syndicate.
The association was, by this time, quite ready to
be broken up. When the girls learned that no
more cases of suicide would get buried with
musical honors, and wreaths and crosses, they lost
their craving for the Hereafter. As for No. 1
Girl, who was going to haunt ’em something
horrible —well, they got over the difficulty by
praying hard for the repose of her soul.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
«Toe Darling, the man who laid the foundation
©1 the big Australian score against England in Adelaide,
must be glad that he gave up farming for cricket. Joe
was a great cricketer at school—he made 252 for his col-
lege on Adelaide oval—but his father, a prominent S.A.
politician, and, in business, a keen wheat-buyer, appren-
ticed him to a farmer in the dry northern areas of S.A.
Joe, hovvever, after about two years of farming, rebelled,
returned to Adelaide and started a cricket-depot, much to
the parental disgust. Joe’s cricket prowess put' him into
the S.A. eleven against Stoddart’s last team and he
thumped the English bowling mercilesslj - , making a
century in one innings. Gradually the old man relented,
and he much astonished his family by happening down to
cricket matches when his lad was batting. By-and-by
there came a reconciliation. On top of a big score the
Hon. John D. walked into his son’s dressing-room. “ Joe,
ma boy, 1 think you’re best at cricket. Here s a gold
watch and a cheque to ye representing one poon’ per run
of your score to-day.” Joe was speechless. That
year the old man followed his offspring to Melb., and
when begot there he said—“l don’t keer aboot beezness
so much noo,” and he went frae there to Sidney. And
now the Hon. John—who five years ago had never seen
a cricket-match—is always there, provided that Joe is
there too; and the score of 178 against England on
Friday week brought a cheque for c7B. The old man
only makes it k ;1 a run over the hundred noo—“ One has
to be canny wi’ Joe, who bats better when the siller is
up.”
* * * *
Adelaide test-match was an eye-opener to the
great Wardill. The attendance beat all previous S.A.
records, and over £lOOO was taken on the first two days.
When Harry Trott won the toss, someone asked him
what the Australians ought to make. “We ought to
make quite 600,” he said, “but I’ll be satisfied with 500.”
And they made 573.
Over 45,000 people witnessed the match in Ade-
laide. The gate totalled £2174 3s. 9d., far and away an
Adelaide record, and, in proportion to population, an
Australian record also.
The best innings of the match were Darling’s
178, Clem Hill’s 81, Hayward’s 70, and Iredale’s 84.
Darling's big hit out of the Oval—the biggest ground in
all Australia—went not only over the outer fence, but
over the barbed-wire netting as well. When Darling and
Hill were together—they put on 170 runs—the English
bowling seemed helpless, indeed. But they had a run of
luck next day. Darling just touched a ball and was
caught by Storer; then Gregory was bluffed out by
Storer with an appeal for a catch which Phillips allowed.
Syd. says he never went near the ball. Then “Noss”
Iredale played a ball on to his wicket just when he was
commencing to clout the bowling.
M’Laren’s 124 was the one decent display of
batting in England's second innings, and he once more
proved that he is a better man than Ranji. The Lancas-
trian amateur is the brilliant man of the English team.
* » » *
The versatility of Australian bowling was illus-
trated in England’s second innings in Adelaide. M'Leod,
who had not bowled in the first essay of England, cap-
tured 5 wickets for 66. But Noble and Howell were the
bowlers of the match. The former bowled quite up to
Giffenian form, and bids fair to take champion George's
place in Australian cricket. Howell proved that Aus-
tralia has at last secured a reliable length bowler. Like
Hearne, England’s crack, he kept down the runs even
when the best men were in.
• * * *
The unexpected usually happens, so perhaps
Trott will win the toss in the fourth test-match. All
Melbourne seems to have made up its mind that the
Englishmen are to take first innings next time, and a
considerable portion of Melbourne expects them to win.
In fact, Young Australia seems dissatisfied with the all-
roundness of the local men’s superiority in the two last
matches, and (without considering that they made 400
runs in their follow-on innings at Sydney) nurses a sus-
picion that “ the other fellows have been having the loan
of ’em.” Than which nobody knows better than the
Other Fellows, seven of whom collapsed for 17 runs in the
attempt to avoid a one-innings licking at Adelaide. But
stay. Briggs didn't collapse. He carried out his bat,
with a blob to his credit, after putting in 35 minutes at
the wicket.
* * • *
The British Lion, with his tail between his legs,
has roared “wretched and paltry!" at the batting of
Stoddart’s team in the third test-match, and, notwith-
standing the fact that the Australians played a portion of
their winning game almost as slowly as the visitors scored
in a losing game, the roar of the indignant animal afore-
said was justifiable. No English Eleven has made such a
poor display, on paper, as the men who tried to keep
their wickets up (and couldn’t do- it) at Adelaide last
Monday week. Lord Sheffield’s lot, of yore, lost a couple
of test-matcht-s in a respectable, slap-dash manner. They
at least tried to bat brilliantly. The latest failure of a re-
presentative English Eleven wasprobably more pronounced
than it might have been if they had batted after the
devices of their own hearts instead of attempting to graft
the Australian “safe” method upon their ’Ome-made
style. Maclaren ranks as a rapid scorer when “set.”
Going in after lunch Mac. had made only 43 at the
4 o’clock adjournment, and when stumps were drawn his
score was 70. If the “ brilliant ” Britisher’s slow rate of
scoring (20 per hour) was due to the quality of the bowl-
ing there is no more to be said. Stoddart’s team, in that
case, haven’t a ghost of a show to win either of the re-
maining test-matches on their merits.
Hinglishinen in Australia seem quite unable to
realise the truth about Stoddart’s team up-to-date. Of
their six recognised bowlers, one (Wain wright) hasn’t taken
a wicket in elevtn-a-side matches. Hayward, Hirst, and
Briggs have captured 26 wickets among them, at an
average of 50 runs, and although Hayward is a much
better bowler, on figures, than the two nuggety little men,
he is apparently unfit for hard work. Richardson and
Hearne are the only English bowlers of importance, and
they, on hard wickets, are dangerous only by com-
parison with Briggs and Hirst. If we have already seen
the best that the English bowlers can do, we are likely to
see the Australians make 800 runs next time they win the
toss. Give Richardson a strain in his arm and Hearne a
cold in his head, and what is there to prevent the looal
talent from scoring 1000 runs for one innings?
Australian bowling against the Englishmen is
quite another story. Only two of the visitors have taken
10 wickets apiece, whereas five Australians have done it.
A sixth man, Howell, has the fine average of five for 66 in
one innings of a test-match. Outside of these, there is
Trott, always good enough to do a certain amount of
execution within a reasonable period, if he had no better
bowling at command. A gigantic All-England innings—
i.e., 600 runs—is barely possible, as averages stand at pre-
sent.
Batting returns, thus far, give no solid indica-
tions of English supremacy. The visitors’ play has been
absurdly uneven. Seven times out of twelve, in first-
class matches, the crack Mason has failed to put up
double figures, and one of his double figures was a score
of ,11, anyhow. Ranji and Druce are the only batsmen
who have made double figures every time. Four Austra-
lians have done as much as that —Olein. Hill, M‘Leod,
Gregory and Iredale. They have each defeated six
attempts by the enemy to get rid of them for a unit.
Wainwright, the disappointing Yorkshireman, has
managed to score double figures in seven out of eight
tries, although his highest score was only 37. Stoddart
will, presumably, give Wainwright a show in place of Mason
or Briggs this wc'ek.
Allowing that they have lost the last two tosses,
and have had one doubtful wicket to bat upon, the
Englishmen’s chance of winning the fourth test-match is
a poor one, on paper. But not much poorer than the
chance that Stoddart’s previous team had (on paper) of
winning the rubber after they had suffered donkey-lick-
ings at Adelaide and Sydney. The Kangaroo will most
likely knock the Lion into small bits, but the time for
dancing among his fragments is not yet
* * * *
Ranji’s declaration that he will play in Adelaide
no more show's his childishness, writes The Bulletin’s
local correspondent. Barring a few spectators who
made references to ice-cream and bananas, the crowd
cheered the Injun far more than he deserved. He
drop ed two catches and batted only fairly. What must
warriors like Giffen and Trott think of Ranji’s attitude!
The other day a Melb. man sang out to Trott, “ Why
don’t, yer put Worrall on, ’Arry? Are yer jealous?”
This has been Trott’s favorite joke ever since. Then
what about George Giffen and little brother Walter ?
Milk-and-w'ater cricketers make a mistake in coming to
Australia, the home of the “ blankv ” barracker.
* * •* ■ •
Ranjitsinhji has been at great pains to explain
to the Argus man that he receives not a penny of pay-
ment for his Australian trip beyond his expenses By the
way, didn’t W. G. Grace’s “expenses” amount to £2OO0 —
while the sordid, money-grasping professional Englishman
got in wages and exes, about £'sooall told? Ranji., if he
says anything on the expenses question, should go into
details.
* * * *
It would have been more adroit had Ranji said
little or nothing about the unseemly barracking of the
Adelaide crowd. The sensitiveness of our dark guest is
due to his not having played football in Australia for a
while and got used to hearing the opposing team en-
treated to tear his blanky liver out, and to chew him up
and spit him out—and other little courtesies of the sort.
The Indian batsman's plaint was injudicious in that it
has given the Australian barracker the tip that Ranji’s
cricket is badly influenced by remarks from the fence.
Remarks from the fence in the succeeding test matches
may be consequently expected in large quantities.
* * * »
When Ranjitsinhji made a batting slip in the
Adelaide match the crowd yelled : “ Huh ! read Ranji’s
book on batting!” When he dropped the ball in the
field : “ Hi! see what Ranji says about fielding." The
Adelaide revulsion against the black no-prince was all the
stronger because, previous to his chattering, he had been
a strong favorite in the holy city. A local brewer named
one of his tonics Ranjitsinhji ginger-beer; a boot-man
honored him by advertising a special line of Ranji shoes ;
another tradesman promptly produced a brand of Ranji
complexion soap; and in return the dark enslaver gave
them hot chutney, so to speak.
* * * *
As compared with the test-matches, this week’s
little-cricket (even an interprovincial match is small-beer
when an English ■ Eleven is around) could only raise a
simmer of excitement. The Englishmen have been lan-
guidly playing a Hamilton (Vic.) twenty-two, who got
the visitors out for 179, and made nearly as many them-
selves in the first innings. Tasmania played a Vic. second
eleven at Melb. and started with 154 to the Victorians’
107—Eady’s speedy bowling taking seven wickets (four
clean) for 96. At, Sydney the 60th N.S.W.-Vic. match
commenced with the local men batting ; and Saturday’s
play left four wickets down for 177. On Monday rain
commenced to fall, and the match had to be postponed.
Iredale’s 13, last Saturday, was the result of
very sick work, and his innings, when compared with
Donnan s slow but sure 56, awakens the query as to
which gentleman should stand out of the next test-
match. Perhaps both should stand out: Iredale for bad
batting, and Donnan for slow batting.
• • * *
Dear Bulletin, —He test - matches and
“ Britishah’s ” assertion that in the first one the English-
men scored faster than the Australians. “ Britishah "
has evidently taken a leaf from the book of the astute
financial jugsrler who runs N.S.W.— i.e., he presents one
set of figures bearing out his view, and carefully suppresses
any others. Why not pick out the last two matches in
Melb. and Adelaide, in both of which the Australians
scored considerably faster than the Englishmen? Let B.
look up the scores and see. “ You pay your money and
you have your choice,” and my choice is the last two
matches—two to one, you know ! Fancy talking of the
brilliancy of the English attack after 520 and 573 in two
consecutive innings !— The Swimmer.
• * * *
After Giffen’s indifferent exhibition in the inter-
provincial, the test-match selectors were in a difficulty
about the great man. But genius found a way out—he
had not heard from the promoters with regard to the
higher pay, and, therefore, it was useless to pick him!
So his reputation was saved — and so was theirs.

From Grey River Argus (West Coast, M.L.): —
“I, the undersigned, challenge R. Mundy to kill more
rats than him by teeth work for £25 a-side.—T. Baggs.”
* * * •
Even betting that Tasmania, finding the
“blind ” tote unworkable because of punters’ opposition,
and being too tired to invent any plan likely to meet the
wishes of owners and punters, will shortly repeal the law
against Ikey Mo. The T.R C. has just arranged to run a
machine for investors of £2 or over, but, as it will be
open to public gaze, it will serve no purpose beyond
giving £2 investors a chance to get their bit on without
having to rub shoulders w ith the common herd. The
“ outside push ” is quite agreeable for owners to have a
“ blind ” tote so long as the moneys are kept separate,
but as the owners want the “beans” of the “outside
push ” more than anything else in this weary world the
suggestion falls flat. Meantime the “ sport ” flourishes
like a lily of the valley in drought-time.
• * * *
“ Tattersall,” replying to an Otago grumbler
last year re the blackmailing of ticket-holders by horse-
owners, said : “ We think our assurance that the utmost
secrecy is preserved by us as to names and addresses of
drawers should be sufficient.” But it isn’t sufficient.
Neither is “ Tatt.” justified in asserting, as a fact beyond
contradiction, that the large majority of subscribers to
his sweeps are quite sitisfied with the “ early-draw ” sys-
tem, which places the drawer of a probable starter at the
mercy of a secretly-informed owner who may refuse to
run his nag unless he is allowed a 30 to 50 per cent,
share in the ticket. The public are dissatisfied wdth the
original system, now that they understand how' it admits
of blackmail. For every one horsey customer content to
sacrifice a portion of his prize-money chance, for the sake
of the chance of “ hedging” on a valuable ticket, there
are 50 simple speculators, who would prefer to get their
ticket valuable or worthless —at the last moment
before the race, or even after the event is decided.
To them a “Tatt.’s” sweep is merely a lottery,
and if it were advertised to be drawn as a lot-
tery, devoid of the horse element, they would send
along their money all the same. These members of the
great majority have to run the risk of being blackmailed
in order that the “ sweep” character of the drawing may
be maintained.
Let “Tatt.,” when next Melbourne Cup time
approaches, make the experiment of advertising one
“ early-draw ” subscription for the benefit of his horsey
customers who are content to stand their chance
against the rapacity of owners, and two “ late
draw ” lotteries for such as don’t care a button
whether they get their tickets before the race, or im-
mediately afterwards. The writer, for one, would rather
be surprised to find that he had won £5OOO or so, than
experience the awful shock of seeing his “sweep” horse
beaten by a short head out of third place. If a man can
depend upon getting a fair chance for his investment—
and he can depend upon it where “ Tatt.” is concerned—
he is all the better for not getting the long-drawn-out
excitement of knowing that a certain horse is running
for him. Also, he is the better for not having to suffer
the pangs of blackmail.
* * * •
Whitemailer : “Let Tatt. still draw his sweeps
a few days before the race, and discard the horse element
from the first three prizes—the first three horses in the
race to count merely as starters. A man could then make
his first, second, or third-prize pile without interference
from owners, &c., and ‘ starters ’ money would be
divided among drawers of all starters, including the three
that happened to be placed.”
* ♦ * *
Owner and trainer G. A. Powton won £lOBO in
12 prizes at the Xmas-week Perth Cup meeting. “J. G.
Arthur” (Mr. Jenkins) came next with £BBO in the five
prizes won by Le Var and Yale Lock. Bolger, long a
N.S.W. back-country rider, working under Mr, R. B.
Pell’s eye, trained and rode both these winners.
* * • *
In a little “ out-back ” town, recently, the land-
lord of the Bullocky’s Retreat and a couple of his hench-
men made a house-to-house canvass for subs, for an after-
noon’s sport on Boxing Day, and collected some £lO odd.
The sports were duly brought off in the backyard of the
above-mentioned hotel, to the perfect satisfaction of the
landlord and his lady—which should act as an object-
lesson for other up-to-date publicans.
• * * *
In N.S.W., the other day, four horses started in
a race—A, B, C, and D. A, the winner, was ridden by the
trainer of the second horse, B. B was ridden by the
reputed owner (not the nominator) of A. C, the third
horse, was ridden by the nominator of A, while D was
on its own and had its own boy up. Needless to say, on
account of D’s presence in the race, there was the usual
bumping inside a post, running off the course, &c., &c.,
and the supposed “ dead bird ” just managed to squeeze
home. Then followed carpeting-, to the dismay and also to
the great indignation of the guilty ones ; and horse B
its rider, and trainer were given a rest for six mouths’
whilst the rider of 0 escaped with a caution.
* * * *
Pungaridgee regatta was the event of the year
in Barataria, and this year, finding the interest likely to
fail through paucity of entries, an enterprising delegate
was despatched to the capital of Barataria invested with
full powers to do his best. Accordingly he waited upon
the Barataria R.C. and instructed the members of that
club that open orders of £5 each awaited the winners of
the senior races, and could be practically had for the
picking-up. The Baratarians waited no longer; they
reached-Pungaridgee with a dozen men of mixed classes
and entering their boats they had little difficulty in
sweeping “ the card.” But the races were senior races
according to the programme, and the Baratarian Rowiiw
Association has decided that all the maiden men who
rowed are senior oarsmen. In vain the competitors
pleaded that the entries closed earlier and that, the races
were merely scratch ones. The Association was obdurate
and, like the Sunday-school teacher who threw his pupils
to the wolves, the oarsmen are now engaged in puttin'*-
all the blame on to a high official of the association who
was one of their number and who participated in the
deal.
* * « *
Although the L.V.YV. had very good attend-
ances, as times go, for its meeting on Melb. Exhibition
track, there was a considerable loss on the deal. Too
much money given away in prizes, it is said. Can any-
one say what the takings would have been if the League
hadn’t put up a liberal programme? The Melb. public-
takes little interest in wheel-racing ; the names of its
“champions” are dropping out of notice, and only the
big holiday handicaps will draw a big crowd. The excep-
tion to the rule is the Austral Wheel Race, which flour-
ishes as a social, rather than a sporting, event—an annual
show of racing under absurdly unsatisfactory conditions.
And the exception, in this case, certainly proves the rule.
A public that believed in bicycle racing l with the enthu-
siasm of a newspaper-reporter wouldn't roll up to witness
a rough-and-tumble Austral contest on a slippery grass
track, any more than it would trouble to attend an inter-
national cricket-match on a corrugated-iron wicket.

drawn the Yankee into a ring with the Jackson of yore.

Adelaide, not a cent goes in charity. Which, so
far as the public know, is eminently characteristic
of his conduct in life. Some years ago, Simms
was set down as a millionaire. He must have lost
heavily by hanging on to Broken Hills. His
death probably leaves Barr Smith at the head of
the S.A. rich-man list.

GREAT EXCITEMENT
IN MELBOURNE.
Merit, like murder, always “ will out.” Mere
assertion never proves anything. Only practical
demonstration of claims to distinction can con-
vince the public mind. The brain of man is the
harbinger of doubt. It accepts nothing that can-
not and is not supported by proof—proof founded
on truth, walled about, domed and turreted with
facts. Tacts are the offsprings of development,
and development is the material and legitimate
progeny of doubt. Doubts lead to investigation,
investigation to discovery, and discovery en-
lightens the mind, expands the intellect, and
brings man into closer affinity with nature.
Things that are commonplace enough to us at the
present age would have, less than a century ago,
subjected the projector or discoverer to imprison-
ment and torture, and perhaps death. The dis-
coverer of the earth’s rotundity and the propa-
gators of the fact groaned and suffered beneath
the rack of ignorance and superstition, and were
put to cruel and ignominious deaths. When
Herschell first swept the horoscope of Heaven
with his wonderful telescope, and proclaimed the
existence of the stellar world, the million of glit-
tering spheres whirling in space, compared with
some of which our earth is but a tiny speck, an
avalanche of malice and manevolence was hurled
against his devoted head. So on down through
the whole realm of natural science, until within a
few decades, every new discovery, every fresh
revelation of fixed natural law, was met with a
tidal wave of credulity and doubt. That word
“impossible” has stood a colossal barrier on the
highway of “ Acceptance and Belief.” It took
ages to beat it down, but thanks to modern educa-
tion, which has ungyved the hand and unfettered
the brain of man, that monarch of nature, and
permits him to go on untrammeled, solving her
great problems and interpreting her wondrous
laws.
To-day there is passing in review before our
eyes such a panorama of recent discoveries in the
world of natural phenomena and other branches
of science that we stand convulsed and amazed,
but it must be patent to all who have given the
subject attention that the advancement made in
the medical field eclipses all others, and towers
like a white shaft to the clouds above and beyond
conception.
The Polypathio system in the treatment of
chronic disease is undoubtedly the most advanced
of all medical science, and if one were to judge by
the the vaBt throng that may be seen daily at their
offices in their magnificent institute, 84 Collins-
street, Melbourne, it would seem that half the
population of the city were clamoring for their
services.
The success attending this new system is almost
miraculous, but it is in accordance with natural
and applied science, and wins its constant vic-
tories over disease because of the true following
out of that which natural ability and the highest
developments of Medical Science has shown to be
sure and true. All sufferers are welcome to the
fullest investigation of the POLYPATHIC
MEDICAL INSTITUTE, of 84 Collins Street,
MELBOURNE. Wm. HENRY GAZE, M.D.,
L.S.A. Lond., L.R.C.P. Lond., M.R.C.S. Eng.,
is Physician in charge.
Those who cannot call personally, can
write. All communications are strictly con-
fidential, and an opinion will be given in all
cases by return mail where the consultation
fee of 10s. is enclosed.
More capital invested, more physicians
employed, more cures made by the Poly-
pathic System, than by any other similar
Institution or body of Physicians in the
world.*

E. LENTHALL OLDFIELD, Principal.
N. : “ What eyesores are those three
Religious Colleges stuck on to Melb. University,
the Trinity, Ormond, and Queen’s, all with offen-
sive names, too. They ought to be severally the
schools of mining, agriculture and engineering.
In my individual opinion, these smug Anglican,
Presbyterian, and Wesleyan institutions are a blot
oil the educational system. Surely the Salva-
tionists, Muggletonians, Jews and Symists are
just as much entitled to have a show. What
beastly intolerance it was to leave the Jews out,
anyhow.”

“ Compton ” to The Bulletin, —
Don’t let the “bushed” man have all the sympathy.
What about getting sent on the wrong track? The very
familiarity of the average bushman with his surroundings
makes him the least capable, of instructors. You ask
for O’Brien’s place and you are told to “turn round by
O’Toole’s.” You ask for the township and you are told
to follow the track. The fact that there are two tracks
with—to a stranger—equal claims to a trial, is nothing.
To the local mind, one is the obvious and unmistake-
able “turn-off to “Brown’s” or to “Smith’s.”
At midnight, scarcely able to see ten yards before me, I
have been told to take a road “past the end of the
house,” and have found, on reaching “ the house,” that
there were roads on all four sides of it. Which is the end
of a house? My informant knew. There was a clear
conception in his mind when he spoke (it is marvellous
with what clearness bushmen do picture space-relations),
but he failed absolutely to realise that his words called up
no answering conception in mine. Two illustrations: 1.
Road runs w'ith the Barwon on the right. Two men
(shearers — i.e., travellers themselves) direct me. Says
the first, “Go down the river five miles, and take the
first turn-off to the left.” Second intervenes, “Don’t
go down the river road beyond the first gate.
There follow the mailman’s tracks, bearing off
to the left. It’s a straighter road.” I follow it
as after events show, accurately enough. But in
about four miles I come upon a “Warnambool,” which,
to my inexperienced eye, is the river. (The Warnambool
has never been mentioned.) Conclude I have missed the
mailman’s tracks, and am on the river road. It is late
dusk. I recall my first directions —“ Turn off to the left
from the river road.” So I ride on in the dark looking
intently for it ; and pass the station on my right, only a
couple of hundred yards off the track, without seeing it.
Find a turn-off on my left, eventually, and push along it
doggedly for eight miles before concluding that I am
helplessly astray. Observe : all the local world knew of
the Warnambool, therefore to neither of my informants
did it occur that I didn’t. Another experience : “ Follow
the track this way ’’—pointing to a certain star. In a
quarter of a mile I come on two tracks. I select
the one leading “ this way,” which ends at a tank
—trampled out by stock. Not being able to pick it
up again in the dark, I steer back by the stars. Again,
all the world (of that part) knew the road I followed was
a mere turn-off to the tank. Why shouldn’t I know it,
too? Summed-up—chief causes of misdirection are-
failure on part of director to realise mental state of
directee ; under-estimate of distances—which makes a
traveller believe himself to be on wrong road, after cover-
ing distance specified without seeing destination; and
habit of identifying a track by more or less inconspicuous
locally-known landmarks. The one safe rule is to describe
the road, saying how often it forks, and whether right
or left branch should he followed. That’s all. Scenery
and astronomical phenomena are irrelevant and confusing.

How to excel at Billiards.

JJEALTH DEPARTMENT OF NEW SOUTH WALES.
Office of the Board of Health.
Sydney, 6th January, 1898.
APPLICATIONS are invited from LEGALLY QUALI-
FIED MEDICAL PRACTITIONERS for appointment to
the following posts under the Public Health Act, 1896 :
Medical Officer of Health to the combined Metro-
politan Districts; salary, £750.
Medical Officer of Health to the combined Hunter
River Districts; salary, £7OO.
The selected candidates will be required to devote their
whole time to their duties, which will be such as are pre-
scribed by the Act, and such as may be ordered by the
Board from time to time. They will be required to reside
in such part of their districts as the Board may approve.
Applications, with statement of age, qualifications, testi-
monials, &c., should reach the undersigned not later than
Monday, 7th February, 1898.
2d4 O. A. SIMMS, Secretary.

THE KLONDYKE
ABSOLUTELY PE EE
To New Subscribers to “The Province."
The Province claims that it has the largest circulation
west of Winnipeg, and that claim has never been ques-
tioned or challenged. It is now determined, if money
and energy can do it, to increase its circulation until* it
can prove that it has The Largest Circulat icn In
Canada. To attain this object, the Directors have ar-
ranged with The Province Publishing Company, Limited
Liability, for the supi ly of One Hundred Thousand
Copies of the Famous Map of the Klondyxie.
To every NEW subscriber who sends $l.OO to The Pro-
vince, Victoria or Vancouver, Canada’s only high-class
weekly, The Province, will he sent for one year from the
date the subscription is received to any address in Canada
or the United States (United Kingdom $l.OO extra), to-
gether with one copy of the above map. The map has
never been sold for less than Fifty Cents or The Province
for less than a Dollar. By taking advantage of this un-
precedented offer, which is only possible in the case .of
New Subscribers who enclose their money with their
order, they get the two for the price of one. This offer
will be withdrawn as soon as the special edition of one
hundred thousand copies is exhausted. It is the most
generous offer ever made by any newspaper in any part of
the world to obtain new subscribers. Every subscription
booked under this arrangement means a direct loss to
the publishers, but a larger circlulation means more ad-
vertising at better rates, and in this way The Province
hopes to make good the deficit. Send $l.OO to The Pro-
vince by return mail, and get a copy of this beautiful and
accurate map and The Province for a year.

During Closing-Out Sale it is often useless to order from

placed.- In sending samples, therefore, a first, second,

best judgment would be used in selecting the nearest, both
as regards pattern and price.

H.M. : “ Surveyed a city crowd to-day,
devoting the precious lunch-hour to sampling the
Gospel. Their orator, of the fiery order, was
giving ’em Holy warmth and other pleasantries in
the good old imprecatory way. I noted the effects
of the fervid feast on his disciples’ features, and
left, reflecting that it is an icy iconoclast who would
employ Reason to deprive these mental cripples of
their crutches.”

A Sydney suburban draper lately erected
a water-fountain in the main street and presented
it to the council. At the opening the local resi-
dents rolled up in force, and proceeded to show
their gratitude for the plenteous water by wading
in the free beer that the draper had provided, and
sundry of them were locked up. The aforesaid
draper, evidently an all-round public benefactor,
again came to the fore and paid up their fines.

“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack

nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines

“She.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Wealth.
Winsome little thing,
Playing on the floor,
Tiny Nymph of Spring—
Doris, cetat. four.
Smiling pretty mite,
Happy as the day ;
Little elfin sprite,
Chasing care away.
Ev’ry wish and whim
Instantly attained,
Governess so prim,
Bound to be well-trained.
Envied she must be,
Hers the pride of place ;
Surely you'll agree
Riches make the pace.
Money oft reviled—
Why, I cannot tell;
Doris, only child,
Liked it very well.
Seminary tall,
French and German cue,
Doris beat them all,
Thumbed the banjo, too.
Little Miss in skirts
Hiding- ankles’ make—
Maze of lace, who flirts,
Causing hearts to ache.
Presently this dove
Smiling meets her fate ;
Then the verb “ to love”
She will conjugate.
Trousseau very choice,
Wedding very smart,
Happy pair rejoice,
Honeymooning start.
Nurtured, cared for, well
Brought up has she
been ;
She’s a modern belle,
Fashion’s social queen.
Envied should she be,
Hers the pride of place ;
Surely all agree
Riches make the pace.
In Poverty
Ushered into sin,
Such plebeian type,
Nose and mouth and chin—
Little guttersnipe.
Mother’s morals loose,
Father never known—
Grumble ! What’s the use?
Vice will claim its own.
School she always shirked,
Ignorance is bliss;
For her bread she worked—
Plucky little Miss.
Selling papers here,
Hawking matches there ;
Soon starts drinking beer,
Then she learns to swear.
P’r’aps she’ll lie and steal,
Hunger presses sore —
For her country’s weal
Get six months or moi e.
See, her looks are bold,
Though her feet are bare 1
Charity is cold,
So is wintry air.
Virtue bids her go,
Stares in holy dread ;
Vice, now bowing low,
Welcomes her instead.
Decked in colors rare,
Rouged and painted face,
Tinted auburn hair,
Sunshade trimmed with
lace.
Kept and clothed and fed
By the fruits of Shame ;
On chill Virtue’s head
Shall we put the blame ?
Ere we cast a stone
Let us look within,
See we do not own
Friendship with such sin.
Mother’s morals loose,
Father never known—
Preach it 1 What’s the use ?
Vice has claimed its own.
LANCE T.

Quaint crank letter from the typical hazy
correspondent— the sort who loves to write about
the Beast of Revelations and the like :
Editor Bulletin, — In 1894 there came before a certain
court of justice in N.S. Wales one of the strangest cases
that ever took place in this or any other country ; during
the hearing of the case one of the defendants hypno-
tised the presiding Judge and several of the jurymen,
and when passing sentence the Judge did so with a ft a *'
vanic bat tery between his legs to keep him awake. Last
September or October the same man was in Sydney and
hypnotised a good many people, the peculiar feature ot
the case being that all those Hypnotised declared they saw
pictures in his eyes. Still more remarkable is the fact
that neither whilst in gaol or out did they get the man s
photo. ; all that was shown on the photograph was the
man’s nose, and although many snapshots were taken in
Sydney all alike, for some unaccountable reason, failed.
The affair occasioned some little stir at the time, but
seemed to die a natural death until last week it was re-
ported he had been seen in one of the sifburbs of Sydney.
Writing on this subject brings to recollection a lot of
strange incidents connected with the affair, but I cannot
recollect them. Clearly there was something connected
with it about Deeming, the murderer, being still at large
and known as the Destroying Angel of a certain sect :
also,about the line of life in the man's hand as .described
by palmists, and a dim recollection of a peculiar minder
happening in a country gaol. If you could furnish a few
particulars you would greatly oblige me and, I daresay,
interest others.
Melb. Unitarian Church, the late Chief
Justice Higinbotham’s place qf worship, after a
shepherdless spell, has just imported a new pastor
in Rev. R. H. Lambley, M.A., said to be big
intellectually. Local Unitarians, tired of frequent
changes, hope much from the .new man.
A closely-cropped bristly head is the pride
of one Melb. pressman, whose pet theory is- that
the brains ooze through the scalp, and that the
more hair in the way the less chance the in-
tellect has of asserting itself —in support of which
he instances the average musician. The other
day the man of copy was at the .door of Melb.
City Court, and just as a stalwart constable
roared “William Blinks, on bail, assault and
robbery,” he pushed his way in to meet every eye
in the court. “My word,” audibly observed a
little man at the back, as the journalist strolled
down the passage towards the table, and, by the
way, also towards the dock, “ I wouldn’t care to
meet that bloke of a dark night.” W. B. Dailey
wasn’t far wrong when he said, “Place the most
pleasing face behind the spikes and it becomes at
once a face that expresses every evil passion.”

H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of a}l other Australian gems.*

When the World was Wide.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They wore boys, Billy and Jim, and I looked up
to them from a long way underneath. They
smoked and were happy. I smoked and got sick. ,
Smoking came natural to Billy and Jim. It
wasn’t the considerable undertaking that most
boys find it. And fishing came natural to them ;
and fish-talk came natural too. Billy had a bit of
a halt in his utterance. Evidently ISature was
afraid of him from the first, and had tried to tie
his tongue. But she didn’t succeed to any great
extent. When Billy started to embroider he was
an artist. The fish he missed were leviathans.
He always missed the big ones and caught the
small ones. It’s the way with more people than
Billy, and other things than fish.
Bill and Jim had a contempt for harbor fishing.
It was too safe, Billy reckoned ; and Jim said that
the Dagoes had cleaned the bays—scraped them
clean of all but bung. I hated the term “ bung,”
because it meant yellowtail, and yellowtail were
the only fish that I could catch. But Billy and
Jim were master fishermen, and wouldn’t allow
that yellowtail were anything but “ bung ’’—too
silly to catch except as a means to an end.
Saturday afternoons we would go down to the
wharves and catch yellowtail. They generally
caught ten to my one. Mostly I’d be undoing tan-
gles. That was how I learnt to swear. Nothing in
life teases halt so much as to sit negotiating an end-
less mess while the fish underneath are hungry and
anxious to be hooked. When the afternoon is far
gone, it’s particularly hard, because at sunset the
yellowtail close their mouths and won’t open them
for the best bait in the world.
With me yellowtail were an end. With Billy
and Jim they were only bait.
• * * •
After dark on Saturday evenings I’d meet them
trudging eastward. At other times when the
night was pretty late—they liked going when it
was pretty late because anything might happen
to them then—they’d come to the back-gate and
whistle. Then I’d go down and we’d talk.
“Whereto, Billy?”
“The R-r-ocks.”
Then there’d be silence to allow me to digest
the information, and during the pause they would
fill their pipes and smoke.
“Don’t you think it’ll rain, Billy?” Billy
would cock his eye heavenward and answer posi-
tively, either for or against, as to how the weather
would shape. He always settled things at the
start, and if they didn’t turn out as he predicted,
he would lie. his way out like a meteorologist.
“ Can’t you come ? ” Jim would say—“ heaps of
tucker.”
“ No—worse luck.”
Then another silence—sad on my part. Billy’s
“ old man ” was invariably drunk and didn’t care
what became of Billy ; and Jim’s people lived out
West. Billy and Jim had better times than any
other boys I knew. Just about this time a wind
would whiz down the street, picking up a handful
if dust and flinging it against the lamp.
«It’s going to blow, Billy,” Jim would say.
“My oath—big guns!” was Billy s answer; and
he’d draw himself up and look as though a gale
or two were little things.
“ Where will you camp, Billy ?.
“Mar-owbra.”
“A tent?” . .
“ No,” from Billy. A t-t-tent in this wind !
not m-much—a cave.” , ,
Then he’d puff disdainfully a while and hitch
up his bacrs. He always wore two one in front,
and the other behind. “There’s a cl-lipper
coming up the c-coast, Jim. She 11 be beating on:
Marowbra about three o’clock; keep your eye
open for her.’ ,
“ Yes, Billy ; anyway, we re bound to spot the
Governor Blackall heading up from Melbourne.
“ That’ll be about f-four o’clock, eh ? ’
“ About,” from Jim.
Then, before they went, Jim would be sure to
ask Billy if he had his sheath-knife; and Billy
would shake his head wisely, and remark that
sharks were plentiful off the “Rocks. ’ And per-
haps he’d hint at other uses for the knife—of
land-sharks that lay in wait for men m lonely
places on coast roads.
It happened sometimes that the wind rose
came up in a great gale from the south. AVhen
my window rattled I would wake up in bed to
lean on an elbow and dream of the two
boys—who had left their friends of the street
corner and the gas-lamp to lie all nigh t in a smoke-
blackened cave and mate with wild winds and tall
seas.
• • • *
I had one expedition with them ; I managed it
at a great sacrifice of veracity. We went away
early in the afternoon, and reached a wild spot
under the cliffs, Bondi-way, just before night. _ It
teemed with fish in those days, but now, alas, it is
fished no more. The great sewer of the city
empties there. ,
We made a fire of honeysuckle, the wood that
is red as blood and pretty as a painted thing.
Towards night Jim found a skeleton-key in a rock
hole. Billy said that it must have belonged to a
convict who ran away in the early days and used
the cave that we were camped in for a refuge.
Jim agreed with him, and my hair rose.
It was thrilling afterwards when darkness had
come and Jim and I lay at one side of the fire,
and Billy stood at the other with his hands in his
belt, smoking and stuttering out lies. He was in
great humor that night—rose to the occasion and
excelled himself. , ,Y
He talked of caves first. Said they had a habit
of coming down. They were so nicely fixed up
that the least noise would sometimes shift them.
Where he cams from —the South Coast—a man
just whispered and down the cave came on top of
him. No one ever knew when they were going
to fall. They just did it when they liked. Water
helped at times. The water ate and ate and ate
away at the foundations, and then perhaps a
fellow started to sing, and before he knew where
he was—where was he? “Head and buried,”said
Billy. He took a pull at his pipe and went on. A
lot of people were killed in that way every year.
Lord ! if we only knew how many were lying dead
in broken-down caves along the coast, we'd just
rise and go on the moment. Bellows like us, too,
who were out for a bit of fishing, and were lying
Siiet when it happened ! He concluded by saying
at if he was the Government he’d have every
cave in the country propped. Then he paused
and' took a careful survey of our camping-ground.
He conducted the operation like a general,
tapped the roof and listened, tapped it again and
shook his head doubtfully, stamped his feet on the
floor, and then got down on all-fours to look under
a crevice. In th at position he said “ Pooh ! ” and
called for a stick. Then he raked out a dead
leather-jacket and an oyster-shell. And now he
began to wonder how these came there. It was
a funny thing to find a dead fish and an oyster-
shell so far above sea. Jim said that they might
have been left there by fellows like ourselves.
Put Billy inclined to the opinion that they were
there from the beginning, remained there when
the sea backed away from the land in the early
days.
■’****
There would have been much more argument
only that Billy thought he saw something. He
climbed a rock to get a better view, and
pointed to a white patch that palpitated in the
dark sea. Billy said it was a blue-pointer, and
began to talk of sharks. He asked me had I ever
heard tell of Big Ben. I answered, “ Of course —
who hasn’t?” Then he asked had I ever seen
him. I said “ No,’’and he smiled wisely and shook
his head. Which meant that Billy had seen
him—and that he was just trying to think when.
Big Ben was the biggest shark on the coast.
He was supposed to be more than 20ft. long—
some said 36ft., but no one ever yet heard of a
shark that size—except the white shark of the
Mediterranean —the brute that swallowed Jonah.
Big Ben was the best-known shark in Australia.
If ever anyone happened to be taken under in the
harbor, or on the beaches outside the harbor, it
was always credited to Big Ben. There was
hardly a fisherman in Sydney that hadn’t had
him on one time or another. He was said to be
just one mass of hooks and gaffs and harpoons,
and his jaws were festooned with fishing lines. It
was generally believed that he was full of watches
and rings and other valuables enough to make a
man rich. Bellows always talked of Big Ben
when they were out as we were, and if one got his
line snapped it was ten to one that a week or so
after he’d suppose it was Big Ben that did it.
Billy took his pipe out and told a story. He
and the old man, he said, were fishing at Long
Bay one night, and hadn’t any luck. Every fish
they hooked was taken from them before they
could land it. They got a head now and again—
but that was all. The old man knew at once that
it was a shark, and said that he’d get him. He
put out a line that’d hold a horse, with a whole
mullet for bait. In a couple of minutes the shark
took the bait, and was nearly taking Billy’s old
man, too, because the old man wouldn’t give an
inch of line away. Then Billy took the old man
round the waist, and they dug their heels in rock-
holes and braced themselves back. In a little
while the shark sickened. “He didn’t want to
get away,” said Billy. Billy supposed the hook
in his jaw hurt him too much. They hauled him
in, and— —
“ Cri-i-ikey! it’s Big B-ben !” said Billy’s old
man, as it lay on the beach looking up at them with
small white eyes. The old man got his sheath-
knife and put it into the shark. But Big Ben
gave a roll, and stood up on his tail like a penguin.
Then he began to waddle forward, and Billy
never saw anything so funny as the way that the
old man turned tail and cleared out. Billy ran
too, and—but enough of the boy’s talk. The sea
was beating on the rocks, sluicing and swishing;
and in the rock-holes the crabs were crawling to
and fro like spiders all on fire.
• •- *. * .
After that, I saw much of Billy, and heard him
often. But, alas, a girl came. Ah, why did the
little girl move into our street; and why did she
part us so daintily-a smile for one and a dis-
dainful toss of brown curls for the other ? Could
she not have left us boys a little longer .

Viator : “ Re Parisian Pompes Fun fibres.
There are seven classes of funerals, the most ex-
pensive .£2OO, and the cheapest .£3 10s. When
the next of kin certify they are unable to pay. the
funeral company has to give free and respectable
burial, on the .£3 10s. fashion. Notwithstanding
that one-third of the burials are gratuitous, the
company pays 40 per cent, di vidend —a commentary
on the profits of undertakery. It has a guaranteed
monopoly from the Paris Corporation, and no
private undertakers are allowed.”
Widgeree: “ A post-officS dodge. Should
a letter-carrier be curious as to the contents of a
certain letter, he will deliver it to someone of a
name similar to that of the addressee. For
instance, a letter addressed to T. B. Smith will be
handed over to one of the numerous other Smiths,
say T. F Aforesaid T. F. opens, reads, marks on
it ‘ Opened by mistake- not for me,’ and returns it.
The letter official then has it at his mercy, and,
after perusal, delivers it to the correct person.”

The Crosscut at the Hundred 'n
Seventy-Nine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, good-bye, graphite bars an’ schists an’ diorites,
We’re goin’ west and south and east to see the
Melbourne lights.
Good-night to good old 1.0. U., and all the Maggie
line,
For we’ve struck it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine !
Last week when they were sayin’ that the stock
would have a fall
I laughs because I taps and hears the lode behind
the wall ;
Oh, bless your heart! I hears that iron vein be-
hind the wall!
Good-bye ! So long !
For we’ve hit it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine.
For don’t you hear the East a-callin’ ,
Singin’ on the salt lake — singin’ on the camel-pad,
Callin’ on the sleeper—welcome whether good or
bad!
Calling from the hundred ’n seventy-nine l
They wanted for to cross-cut west—my little voice
was last,
But I said, “East; for Melbourne’s called that lode
so loud she’s swung round fast.”
So they cross-cut east for eighty feet and never
any sign,
But —we struck it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine !
For I says to them “ Why don’t you drive the way
the voices call ? ’
And, driving just a foot, I hears the lode behind
the wall.
Oh, I hears the two-ounce vein-stuff soundin’ dull
behind the wall—
So, Good-bye ! So-long !
For we’ve hit it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine.
And noiv you'll hear the East a-callin’ —
Calling South to Albany, calling westward to the
Cross —
Calling from the loco.—good though it be gain or
loss —
Calling from the hundred 'n seventy-nine.
There’s the voices of the streets we knew a-callin’
loud and strong,
There’s a cry for us uplift from Melbourne stones ;
There’s a call from ali our women—half a wail and
half a song—•
But there’s little use to call the dead men’s bones.
Sing hushy! for the graveyard’s peg a peg that
marks our claim.
Sing hushy ! for good men lie there asleep,
Who played the silent tragedy which never knows
a name,
Who hid their home-tugged heart-strings deep t
They kept their grit—they never shed their home-
sick tears till dusk,
Soft as breeding women—steadfast as the stars ;
They have left us to the joys of life—the music
and the musk—
It’s our luck we’re warm ; our luck I’ve struck
then; bars.
Sing hushy ! for the mates I leave who’ve pegged
their final claim,
Whose women folk are waiting east like mine ;
And now by Luck and Blessed Lode I’m chuckin’
up the game
For We’ve struck it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine.
Ahd I'm full of every Stinkin’ pride, for I heard
the voices call,
/ heard the two-ounee Vein-stuff soundin’ dull
behind the wall,
And driving just a foot we found the lode behind
the wall!
So Good-bye ! So-long !
For we’ve hit it at the hundred ’n seventy-nine.
And now we hear the East a-callin’,
Callin' on the iron range — callin’ on the rattling
train,
Callin’ on the claypan —“ Never leave your love
again 1 ”
Calling from the hundred ’n seventy-nine.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
White Feather (W.A.).
Te Wiria's Potatoes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Villiers was on good terms with the dispossessed
lords of the soil. He had a sort of romantic re-
gard for them. He considered they were an
ill-treated, down-trodden race—he used to tell his
pakeha friends so. And when men met him in
Auckland they wcmld ask him how his proUg£s
the Ngati-Ata were getting on. Of course he
spoke the Maori lingo. He doctored the members
of his pet tribe when they fell ill ; bought their
kumera at exorbitant prices ; helped them in their
land transactions with the grasping pakeha-. gave
them fche use of his out . houses for s i eep ing in, and
o{ his paddooks for their horses .
So far, the Ngati-Ata had done nothing for
Viiliers in return, beyond warning him of the
approach of pack-horses from the interior—bony,
pi'mh-bellied pack-horses were the bane of his life
and a menace, to his clover-paddocks
Villiers lived m an old pa close by the sea. Its
earthern walls stood twenty feet high, and were
surrounded by a ditch fifteen feet deep. The
whole earth-work was overgrown with maiden-hair
fern and lycopodium—in token of the return of
peace. In the middle of the pa, commanding a
view of the sea, Viiliers had built his bouse ; and
his farm stretched its rich acres all round.
He had grown a phenomenal crop of potatoes,
but the question was, who would dig them up?
He himself was turning sixty ; his sons had gone
to the Thames to dig gold; all the able-bodied
men of the district had caught the gold-fever too
—worn-out old-soldier Saunders and one-armed
constabulary-man Murphy were the only men left.
• • • *
Viiliers stood on the pa bank and pondered.
The bay stretched glittering before him ; a gentle
breeze stirred the trees in the orchard below
and rippled the surface of the sea. Two big
canoes came in sight round the nearest point and
made for the shore in front of the house. Villiers
anticipated a day spent in kororo and eating.
Two score Maoris came straggling up the beach,
through the gap in the pa bank, and stood -in
picturesque groups ahout Villiers’ verandah.
“ We come from Tohitapu, our chief,” said the
spokesman of the party, a huge fellow of fifteen
stone. “ Tohitapu loves the pakeha people, but,
most of all, he loves Te Wiria, his great friend.
Tohitapu has stored up in his heart all the good
things Te Wiria has done for the Maori, especially
for the Ngati-Ata hapu. Nothing can ever make
Tohitapu forget the kindness Te Wiria has shown
to him and his kainga. Therefore Tohi’ has said
to us: ‘ What can we do to show Te Wiria our
thanks ? How can we return this great ranrjatira's
many services ? How can we preserve the regard
of our pakeha friend for a long time? I will tell
you. Te Wiria has a fine crop of potatoes, just
ready for digging-Hakiri has seen them, .and so
has Titore. But how will To Wiria dig up his
crop at this time, when all the pakeha have gone
to the gold-fields, and men are scarce? Now you
men of Ngati-Ata, I will tell you what you must
do. You must take the largest canoes you have,
the biggest of all, and go over to Te Rapa, to Te
Wiria, and dig up his riwai crop for him. Then
we shall show that the Maori people love Te
Wiria, and there will be great friendship between
him and us.”
Villiers replied almost with tears. He was
overjoyed to know that the Ngati-Ata had his
welfare so near their heart. He had tried to show
that he looked on them as friends : they had come
to prove that such they were. They were good
men--they thought of the needs of others. They
deserved to enjoy plenty all their lives. Might
they never lack kurnera and potatoes, pigs and
tobacco. As for his riwai crop, it was a good
crop, as they had guessed, and ready for digging.
He would be glad to accept their generous offer.
He considered Hakiri and Titore and Haneke and
the rest of them had shown a proper spirit in
coming over to help him just in the nick of time.
It was what he might have expected of such
generous follows.
Forks and spades were taken down to the potato-
field. The Maoris—men and women—began to
dig for all they were worth.
• * * •
Towards mid-day, Villiers’ women-folk took
down to the workers large quantities of pork and
a huge iron cauldron. The Maori women, with
many smiles, fixed up a cooking-place, and soon
the koh.ua was sizzling over a bright fire. The
pork was boiled with thistles in the same pot.
r i he Maoris gathered round; squatted on the
ground ; dipped their fingers in the stew, and ate
till they were full. Then they stretched them-
selves. -They praised Te Wiria, his house, his
horses, his pork, his potatoes. And Villiers’ little
son looked on and watched them feed, and coveted
their eating capacities.
In the evening the work was finished; fifty
sacks of potatoes stood heaped in Villiers’ sheds.
With many flattering speeches, and laughter and
chattering, the Maoris got into their canoes, and
disappeared round the cape.
Villiers went to sleep with a light heart that
night his endeavours to maintain friendly rela-
tions with the Ngati-Ata were not in vain.
At one o’clock, his big kangaroo-hound began to
bark with all its might, and tugged furiously at
the bullock-chain that held -it; »■ The dog-often
barked at night-—usually at wild cats or the moon.
Villiers put his head out of the window, but
could see nothing—so he went to bed again. But
the dog barked on for hours.
* * * *
Next morning, Villiers went to the sheds, to
feast his eyes on his wealth of potatoes. He
opened the door of shed No. I.—not a sack was to
be seen. He went to shed No. 2. —that also was
empty. His potatoes were gone.
Villiers did not go for the police—there were no
police to fetch. He saddled his horse, and rode
over to Tohitapu’s pa,
Tohi’ met him with all the dignity of the true
rangatira, and his mouth full of pork—Villiers
had arrived there at dinner-time. The pakeha
quickly told his story. Tohi’ listened with the
deepest respect. Villiers pointed to some sacks
marked with great red W. hanging on a fence
near by. Tohitapu acknowledged that it was
strange that they should be there—beyond a
doubt some of his fellows were arrant rogues—he
would see to it. Viiliers pointed to a newly-dug
rue, almost under his horses feet. Tohitapu ac-
knowledged that it had been dug that morning,
Viiliers remarked that it was full of potato*!
Tolii’ relinquished his hold on Viiliers’ rein and
called his people together.
“You Ngati-Ata are a bad people,” he said,
“ You always were a greedy, thieving set of men.
I have long felt ashamed of you. Te Wiria here
is niy pakeka he has lo be ' n n friend and the
friend of Ngati-Ata. So you men there, Hakiri,
Titore > and Haneke, when you hear tl.at Te Wiria
has got a fine riwai crop, you go to Te Rapa and
say that I, Tohitapu, told you to dig* it up. Te
Wiria is a guileless man-he let you do the work,
You store the riwai in Te Wiria’s sheds. You
are a low-bred set of men, taurekareka, all of you.
Yon have no shame ; you forget the pakeha thinks
Stealing is a sin ; you forget tlie pakeha people put
thieves into gaol and make them herehcre, prison-
ers. So you go and take Te Wiria’s riwaA crop ;
you steal it in the night-you dare not go in the
( biy. You are great cowards, you Ngati-Ata.
And you bring the potatoes to th ekaim/a, and say
to yourselves, ‘ It is well : we shall have plenty of
food for the winter. ’ He ware te hum,a nci ! You
are a wicked, lazy lot of people; you are a set of
cowards; you are an ungrateful, foolish people;
you have disgraced me in the eyes of my pakeha,
Te Wiria. lam ashamed of you. Get out of my
sight, everyone of you!” And Tohitapu strode
through the spell-bound Ngati-Ata, and resumed
his interrupted meal, his meal of pork and baked
potatoes—Te Wiria’s potatoes.
Viiliers sat on his horse, wondering whether
Tohitapu was a great actor, or a great liar. He
rode home, wondering. He is wondering to this
day.
